UNDER THE
GUMTREE
A Cursory Commen-
tary.
JUSTIN McCarthy’s “Re-
miniscences ” belong to
an amiable elderly per-
son who‘isn’t game to
let himself go. If he told all
he knew there would be wigs
on the green isle and the other
one ; so he remains Parnell’s
“ nice old gentleman for a
small tea-party,” and de tea-
party fabula narratur. All that
the plum - picking people can
gather are remarks like that
which Lord Brougham made
to McCarthy after his Parlia-
mentary entry in 1862 —
I say, you made a devilish clever
speech ; I didn’t agree with all of
it—in fact, some of it was non.
sense—but it was a damned clever
speech. Who the devil are you ?
Omit the Parliamentary adjectives; and admire
the wit and wisdom of this —
I say, you made a clever speech. I didn’t agree with all
of it—in fact, some of it was nonsense—but it was a clever
speech. Who are you ?
Even the adjectives are tame in Australia One
blast from that old Q. pioneer’s throat would have
sent Brougham shivering into the refreshment-
room. When there was trouble in that Q. finan-
cial company, and the London board sent two
enquiring members to Brisbane in all the pride of
British Respectability, light trousers, and tall hats,
their interview with the local Board was thus re-
ported by the man in the next street —
“ Good morning, Sir ’’
“ What the do ■you want ? ”
“ We are members of your London board, Sir ; and
we have come to ask you some questions relative to that
transaction of ”
“Go to , you and your questions! What the
do you take me for ? ”
“ But, Sir ! ”
“ your , you son of a !
What the do you mean by coming here and
cross-questioning me like a barrister? Get to
out qf this, you , you cook’s galley
scrapings, you consumptive waster ! ”
“We are at a loss, Sir ”
“Do you know a team of bullocks from a poley cow,
you ? ”
“ We must admit, Sir ”
“Then clear, you
They cleared ; and the old man wiped the froth
from his lips and called for something to take the
taste out of his mouth. O tempora !
Not ten strong men those great round oaths could raise,
Such men as live in these degenerate days.-'
For the glory hath departed from Israel, and the
D from Dibbs.
A. H. Fullwood’s new picture of Lake Waka-
tipu, M.L., is intended for show at Sydney Art
Society’s August exhibition. Only some sheep
are permitted to feed in the foreground among the
brown tussocks ; the rest is landscape—lake and
shore and hill, with a cloud-hung peak that rises
from the lake : beyond and above are other circl-
ing peaks and the glacier of mighty Mount Earn-
slaw. The time is autumn ; and the touch is
true.
Brunton Stephens has just passed his 64th birth-
day, and is reported evergreen and gay. His
poetical works have recently been taken over by
Angus and Robertson, who contemplate a re-issue
in a new form. Despite a cheap edition, J.B.S.
has had little sale for years—partly, it would
seem, because his publishers were less active than
they might have been. This is mentioned because
it is so uncommon a merit with publishers.
Twenty publishers advertise us to the water, for
one who will pay for our drinks.
Mr SELF.
Drawn by Gelett Burgess.
8° Sarcey is dead : good old Sarcey ! He was
(it ts the inevitable comparison) France’s Clement-
ocott of dramatic criticism; but far better,
because far wider. He stood, indeed, on no higher
plane; but he saw all round on his plane, and
even all round himself—which is a literary figure
not to be taken physically, for Sarcev’s rotundity
was so tremendous that he could not scan his toes
without tottering, much less his sacrum. The
new critic of the Temps will fill only one seat: they
always reserved two for Sarcey, the fattest Jove
who ever thundered over dramatic Olympus. No
style; and the appreciation of the mutton-
head in the street. But he held the
experience of generations ; he was so de-
voted to the stage, so sincere and sure in his
knowledge ; and what he did not know he whistled
the footlights. He loved melodrama ; he
hated Ibsen; and these are evil days for
I ranee.
I had not noticed Mr. A. P. Martin’s second
assault on local bards (in Literature, April 29)
when writing last week ; or in the heat of derision
I might have been tempted’ to print another of
Mr. Martins “poems” — and the disease is not
desperate enough for that. This week I make
Mr. Martin welcome to his opinions ; but I do
wish he would mind his facts. He writes that—
the canons of English literary taste and criticism reject
the more elaborate and pretentious poems of Mr. Victor
Daley and Mr. Will H. Ogilvie.
As no copies of Ogilvie’s book have yet been sent
for English review, I am at a loss to know when
and where “the. canons of English literary
taste and criticism ” were applied unless
they are. all included in Mr. Martin’s own
proper person. Yet even this hypothesis is
improbable, for what some people call “ internal
evidence ” shows that Mr. Martin has not seen
Ugilvies book. More, a business letter from a
friend of Mr. Martin’s has been handed to me,
dated fade of Wight, April,’’and asking that
the following books be sent to the writer—
The Hidden Tide, by R. Quinn ; The Ways of Many
Waters, E. J. Brady; On Our Selection, Steele Rudd;
The Bulletin Story Book; and Fair Girls and Gray
Horses , by W. Ogilvie .
And the letter proceeds in these words—
I should have ordered these before, as I wanted them
to lend to Patchett to complete his articles on Bulletin
writers.
Evidently, then, Mr. Martin had not Ogilvie’s
book when that letter was written ; and as no
copies are on sale in England the conclusion is
that when Mr. Martin wrote his article for
Literature of April 29, and condemned Ogilvie’.s
verses by saying that “as a rule they lack all
originality ; they are neither truly Australian in
character nor do they stand comparison with the
finished work of many a so-called ‘ minor poet ’ in
England ”■—and so on—he was writing with as
much bias as you please, but without any evidence
worth mentioning. We understand Mr. Martin
out here; but 1 am sorry to see a paper like
Literature in this galley.
Alfred Bryan, Eng. black-and-white artist—best
known, perhaps, by his sketches of actors for the
Sporting and Dramatic N^ws—- died the other day
at only 47. His versatility was prodigious; and
Max Beerbohm, who is a Person of Taste, eon-
fessed that his whole performance interests me
more than Mr. Phil May’s, more than that of any
po]udar black-and-white artist.”
His quaint drawings are the best of Gelett
Burgess, the bright young American who admired
and parodied Stevenson. His nonsense verses
would be worth little without picture-enforce-
ment ; and his humourous prose disengages
readers’ hollow groans. Even the notoriety of
Ihe Purple Cow”—-which was lucus a. non — is
passing. I gather two Burgess-buds while I may.
Some correspondents—
“ A. J.G.” weeps rhythmically but unpoetically.
“ Ignotus ” remarks —
In the St. James’s Bit I get, April 7,1899, page 32, I read :
“ A record and review of the life and works of Dante,
Gabriel, and Rosetti is being prepared by Mr. H. C. Maril-
lier.”
That will be a work of very great interest.
Talbot Smith casts an interesting sidelight on
the lively publisher—
I notice you remark Kipling’s power of sarcasm as
illustrated by some sentences of his about Guy Boothb.v.
Those sentences were written quite seriously. I know
this, because they were writtgn to me. But here is the
paragraph as written: “ Boothby has come to great
honours now, and is also a husband and a father.
His name is large upon hoardings, his books sell
like hot cakes; and—he keeps a level head through
it all. I’ve met him several times in England
where he has been living not far from m.v
people; and he gave me an exhibition of colonial
driving (two hired horses, and one that wasn’t amiable)
through the streets of Salisbury that added to my already
large respect for him.” This was written just two years
ago. Boothby being an Adelaide man, anything that
concerns him is interesting here ; and—the passage being
pleasantly complimentary, I gave it to a journalist friend.
Then the Critic copied it, and eventually it got to
England, for its next appearance was in the London
Academy in an advertisement of Boothb.y’s (then) latest
novel. The point of the joke (?) is that in the advt.
Boothby’s publishers leave out all the words from “ been
living” to “streets of Salisbury” in the above passage,
and so make it appear that he has earned Kipling’s in-
creased respect not as Jehu but as scribe ! But that is
not the fault of Boothby, who was always a really good
fellow.

“ Quiz ”:
Have read Mr. Arthur Patchett Martin’s verses with in.
terest, but no amazement (I knew him in Melbourne).
There, of a similar composition, I weakly asked one of his
friends: “If he won’t mend it, why does n’t he patchett?”
The friend nodded wisely : “ He can’t.”
“ Artist ” writes :
Why is it that Melb. Avgas has always preferred mar-
doching native talent to encouraging it ? Even over-
praise is better than a continual cold sneer. I remember
that long after we had all read Kipling’s “ Soldiers Three’
in the Indian paper covers, the English reprint came to
the Argus and was even then too new to praise. “Too
barrack-roomy,” the Argus said. The Argus is never
quite satisfied with a hook till “it might very well pass
for forty-three, in the dusk, with the light behind it.”
The new English 3d. magazines are enjoying an
unreasonable struggle for existence ; and on every
staff there is a wild-eyed member who searches for
New Ideas to increase the circulation—or main-
tain it. Some six months ago one pair of wild
eyes must have flashed “Eureka !” —for a little
later a circular went forth to prominent authors,
explaining that the public wanted comparative
statistics showing the relation of authors’ heads to
feet—and Would the prominent author kindly
“ press his foot well into the basin of gelatinous
matter sent, having first thoroughly wanned the
gelatine, and return the basin to this office?”
The New Idea, however, did not make converts,
and was finally abandoned when a basin was re-
turned with this note: "Mr. ’s compliments'.
He sends back the basin of gelatinous matter.
Will the editor kindly press his head well into it?’>
But it is not every magazine which wishes to
increase circulation. There comes a point when
the expense of handling vast numbers is not met
by new subscription revenue. Tit-Bits has reached
it_; and the American Ladies' Home Journal,
with 850,000 regular buyers. The ideal is to in-
crease circulation to the limit of advertisers, not to
the limit of subscribers.
J. L. Aston’s recent book on 1 Japanese Litera-
ture ” introduced to English readers the fine flower
of Japanese poetry—the “ haikai.” A haikai is a
verse consisting of three unrhymed lines of five,
seven, and five syllables—seventeen syllables in
all. Its style is “light and fresh, a swift, fugitive
impression more often than not ending with a
surprise.” The Academy. asked its readers to send
haikais, and received these examples—
The west wind whispered,
And touched the eyelids of Spring:
Her eyes, Primroses.
Her eyes are the dos e’s,
Her black locks are the raven’s,
But her heart is mine.
A light, rustling stir
In the full-foliaged tree—
Aha, the squirrel ! .
The Bookfellow desires some haikais, which must
have an Australasian reference, and . 10s. fid. will
be paid for the best received before .Inly 21).

THE BOOK FELLOW.
Maoriland’s “Literature.”
SOME Maoriland writers reply to F. Rollitt’s
complaint that the M.L. Government and
public give little or no encouragement and
assistance to struggling local genius—
I want to know if an epidemic of sheer funk is
spreading amongst our young writers. And just
when things literary are looking a little brighter
at this end of the world ! Thanks to those who
have gone before, the way to publicity and Lon-
don is almost payed for the young writer who has
anything really worth'marketing. The difficulty
is in manufacturing marketable goods, not in sel-
ling them. Rollitt should pray to Tangaroa, Tane,
and Tu, and the rest of the gods of Maoriland, to
send us men—and women —that can write. We’ve
got the market, and that market is daily growing
larger and more profitable. Tu is a god of strength
—he ate all his brothers —I will go and make a
karakia to Tu, and ask him to put bowels into F.
Rollitt and the newly-enrolled members of the
Maoi’iland Literary Association. Tu knows the
value of backbone 1 will ask him to remember
the backbone, too. I will say the proper karakia
to Rongo and Haumia (who preside over vege-
tables) to give the struggling litterateurs plenty of
fern-root and kumaras. I will say a karakia to
Tangaroa (the god of all fish) to send them schnap-
per and rock-cod ; and to Tane (god of all
feathered fowl) to give them quail and pigeons. I
have n’t a doubt they will get all these —but to
what god shall I pray to give them inspiration ?
Tangaroa, Tu, and Tane, and the rest, never knew
anything of books': in fact, all the gods of Maori-
land are quite illiterate. Perhaps that accounts
for the scarcity of literary genius in these isles ?
A.A.G.
Thank Heaven, the M.L. Government, while
aiding schools of Music and Art, has stopped
short of helping schools, of Literature. Men can
practise Drawing, and, to a less degree Music,
without annoying the public much. But if they
are to be taught by Government to practise Litera-
ture, may the gods help us! We have some
750,000 souls here —more than Liverpool or Man-
chester, about as many as Glasgow. Yet E. Rollitt
is disappointed because we have no great litera-
ture of our own. Has Liverpool ? or Man-
chester? or Glasgow? All things considered,
our literary men have done very well. As for
Maori legends, the only men we want to write
them are those who write , them because they
love them. They can’t be written to order. Till
someone comes along Who treats them for his own
pleasure, they had best be left unwritten. Mean-
while, the whole world stands ready for our books
—if we can write them. The world will take
them, if it wants them, without the help of the
Govt, grader whom Rollitt seems to hanker after.
And, after all, it isn’t strange that we should n’t
be specially interested in our own literature. For
reading is a reflective pleasure, and whilst we are
still doing, we have n’t much time for reflecting on
our deeds.
PUKAKI.
F. Rollitt, who complains of the lack of Govt,
support to “Literature” in Maoriland, should
read what Buckle, in his “History of Civilisa-
tion,” says of the protective spirit and its literary
influence: “Three times in the history of the
world has this experiment been tried—in the ages
of Augustus, of Leo Tenth and of Louis Four-
teenth. In each of these ages there was much
apparent splendour, immediately succeeded by
sudden ruin.” The great men of letters associated
with Louis’ reign wrote their best either before
he was born or very early in his reign— e.p., Cor-
neille, La Rochefoucauld, La Bruyere, Pascal, and
others. During the last twenty-five years of the
reign, when the magnificent system of patronage
might have been expected to bear fruit—it did n’t.
Maoriland, which F. Rollitt censures for its
indifference to “Literature,” really “patronises”
writers more than Australia does. For the last
half-dozen years or so a system of Christ-
Drawn by Gelett Burgess.
mas story competition has been favored by
editors from Auckland to the Bluff. In these
competitions names of competitors are unknown
to the judges, who are “qualified literary men
residing in the district; ” and the ostensible object
is to cultivate literature and to laurel the unap-
preciated genius. (Incidentally, the newspapers
are provided with “copy” at a cheap rate or no
rate at all.) Seven-eighths of the prize-winners
are generally women (first prize frequently falls to
a man); and the “ stories ” are worse than in the
days when editor, pressman, and paste - pot
arranged the business without the aid of the
“ literary gentleman residing in the district.” The
reason is obvious. This so-called encouragement;
to literature warms into active life a number of
unhappy beings .who otherwise would never have
thought of writing. I surmise that the “ qualified
literary gentleman,” who has to run through a
hundred stories in a month, loses his reason
before he has gone half-way, and decides which is
best by counting the number of ink-spots on the
first page. I admit that I myself have won “a
prize ”in a M.L. story-competition. In extenua-
tion, I plead that it was a youthful offence, and I
am honestly trying to live it down.
C.C.
And Mr. Rollitt rejoins—
I stick to my main arguments —that nothing is
done to encourage literature or literary workers in
this country ; that literature should receive at
least as much encouragement as painting or music ;
and that literary workers could be benefited
greatly by schooling, advice, criticism, encourage-
ment. Ido not wish anyone to spoon-feed Maori-
land writers, but I would like to see them licked
into shape by a more economical and rapid process
than by finding by experience what editors think
is good. I maintain that unless a young writer
gets local publication, he cannot get proper
criticism. As to London being the true test
of a writer’s quality, I simply do not believe it.
Look at the rubbish that is printed there in the
name of “colonial literature”! Think of Lon-
don accepting “ The Shadow of Richard Hern-
brook ”as a romance of Maoriland, or “A Bride
from the Bush ” as a type of Australian woman-
hood—and others, and others. The fact of the
matter is, the London editor or publisher is not so
good a judge of an Australasian story as the cul-
tivated Australasian reader, else we should never
have so many travesties on our national life and
character. You say, “When an Australian miner
can walk from 'Townsville' to Coolgardie for gold
an Australian writer can easily work to Europe
for gold and fame. ” He can work to Europe, of
course ; but even if he is really good there is no
surety that he will get either gold or fame. I
walked from Deniliquin via Wilcannia to Mil-
parinka and Mt. Brown for gold, and did n’t get
any ; and some very good men have tried to work
the London literary market and have failed —men
who had “ the Root of the Matter ” in them, too.
The Maoriland Literary Association is already
bringing good results; and the men and women
who are working for this association already see
numerous ways of benefiting our writers without
spoon-feeding them. In the article criticised so
severely I state that the Literary Association re-
ceived less support than' a new football club would
expect. I wrote in Samoa, when the association
was a very feeble thing. During the last few
months it has gone ahead, and has now 150 mem-
bers. In one part of my article I say—“ A writer
does not spring into being ready-made. He wants
schooling and advice and criticism.” If they got
these things would it be spoon-feeding them ?- and
can they get these things in Maoriland at present ?
During the last few years I have read hundreds of
original stories by young Maoriland writers, and I
consider that many of these stories would be mar-
ketable if the authors had a few technical hints.
I disagree altogether when it is said of the young
writer that “no teaching, no coddling can help
him ; he must teach himself and fight with his own
hand.” You might just as well tell the young
musician not to accept the ad vice of a master, or
the young painter not to attend schools of art.
The writer can be greatly helped ; and teaching
can give him mastery over the technique of his
art which he might never acquire if left to him-
self ; but besides the teaching the writer can be
much benefited by assistance in getting markets
for his wares, or in getting a good price for them.
London publishers naturally try to get MS. as
cheaply as possible ; and many of them try to get
authors’ work for nothing.

Selections from a week’s exams, in a
Victorian secondary school:—
Jeffreys was a cold-blooded man who hanged People by
the thousand upon a lampost in the Street.
The bottom of the sea is covered with a lot of little
bushes which are called Under-Currants.
To modern Invention we owe the Telegraph, the Forth
Bridge, the Torpedo, the Iron clad, and other life saving
appliances.
One of the most interesting, but least
known, institutions of Melb. is the Bo-
hemian Chess Club. Entrance gratis, and
subscription nothing. In the underground
smoking-room of the Victoria Coffee Palace,
afternoon and evening, all the year round, “ the
royal game ”is played. Sometimes the local
talent meets a eounti'y parson or schoolmaster who
“understands” the game; sometimes an ex-
champion or a local alleged * master ” will pass an
hour or two—over a move or two; or a pretty
problem will be set to puzzle the solvers, or an
interprovincial or international game will be
analysed. All meet equal. An ex-miner from the
west, an ex-Minister of the Crown, a retired
parson or two, a University coach, asmart Yankee
patent-medicine man, and Marshall-Hall’s friend
—Dierich, the chain-cigar smoker.
A Bush Evening.
I remember, long ago,
How beyond the old verandah
Round the paths we used to wander
In the golden afterglow.
Slowly was the sunlight fading,
Evening silently invading,
Deeper, darker grew the shading
Down below.
And we walked beneath a green
Mass of branches, making dimmer
Still the place, till but a glimmer
Of the last light stole between.
Willow streamers hanging o’er us,
Willow streamers on before us,
While the frogs sang croaking chorus
Through the screen
Now the sun has set, and soon
Pale and yellow still, but finding
Silver in the river winding,
Through the darkness glows the moon.
Everywhere her rays are gleaming,
Sparkling silvery, softly streaming
Past the cattle lying dreaming
By the old lagoon.
Listen to the curlew's note
Far away across the marshes ;
Now the constant croaking harsh is
Chorused where the branch-ends float.
Rabbits scuttle through the bushes ;
Something rustles in the rushes ;
And, as darkness falls, a hush is
O’er the bush remote.
How the water in its flow
Ripples over stones and mosses,
Sparkling where a moon ray crosses
Through, where branches thinly grow.
Now the night is swiftly spreading ;
As we turn, the darkness dreading,
Moonbeams hold a silver wedding
On the stream below.
CURLEW.
“Stumps.”
Grindin’ up the sandhill with the festive mallee
stump
(Chuck ’em quick an’ lively with a chunk ! plunk!
chunk !)
Creakin’ thro’ the rubble with a joggle an bump
(Very nearly done !
Six bob a ton !
Just about enough to do a good square drunk !)
Sloppin’ thro’ the crab-’oles w’ere you nearly lose
yer load
(Slip ’em over, sonny, ’fore the sun goes down !)
Plowin’ up the metal on the rotten “ council ”
road ;
Axles ’ot as ’ell!
Must take a spell !
Cartin’ kitchen mallee to the “ two-pub ” town !
Fifteen bloomin’ shillurs for the load : two-ten a
half !
(Slew ’em on the weighbridge w’ile they shunt
that truck !)
Fifteen bob between us for a week of steady graf’
(That’s the bloomin’ lot !
Cripes ! ain’t it ’ot !)
Mine’s a “ butcher,” sonny. Got yer own ? ’Ere’s
luck !
NIL.
Disillusion.
I set her on a pedestal apart,
A sweet, pure soul, to worship from afar;
As high above me and my longing heart
As some far-shining star.
Yet from her pedestal she gladly bent
When sweet temptation stretched forth beckon-
ing arms,
And slowly through the vale of love we went
And tasted all its charms.
Above my reach, I would have loved her long;
Laid at her sacred feet my manhood’s best ;
But love is dead—the woman that I wrong
Was just like all the rest!

IN AMERICA.
The Legend.
IT was in the summer of 1778, Wash-
ington, with his army ever active,
determined to oppose the British
general, Sir Henry Clinton, who, having
evacuated Philadelphia, was retreating
through New Jersey on New York. The
forces met on 28th June at Monmouth.
La Fayette conducted the attack. The
heat was so intense that some refused to
fight, and many fell dead from exposure
to the sun’s rays. Both sides fought
desperately. As the day waned, and the
flagging spirits of La Fayette’s soldiers
were at a low ebb, a strange sight aroused
them. Marching with drum and life
was an old man, Jeremiah Wynton, and
his son, and grandson. The old father,
with silvery flowing locks, sturdily strode
through the field. The son with ban-
daged head fifing lustily, the youth,
catching the spirit of his grandsire, rolled
out with him the soul-inspiring rattle of
the drum. Father, son, and grandson
had accompanied the troops as volunteers,
. and were ever in the forefront of the
fight. The fierce light of battle was in
their eyes, they knew no fear, and many
a wild cheer arose, as on that fateful
day Yankee Boodle was heard as never
was heard before or since. . . „

SATURDAY, JULY 8, 1899.
The Politician who Went Away.
Thomas MTlwraith, the boomer and bor-
rower, the magnate of many overdrafts at
the Q.N. Bank, the man who founded and
deserted the short-lived and hazy “ National
party ” in the North, the fat speculator, the
supporter of Kanaka labor, the Politician
who Fled when the storm came and never
returned—is writing open letters to the
people of Queensland imploring them not to
join the Australian Commonwealth. The
latest is dated from “ Kensington Mansions,
London,” and the address is rather a shock
and a surprise to Queensland. For some-
how it has been a legend in the North that
the country shouldn’t be too hard on the
man who helped so much to hustle it down
asteep place into the deep sea of ruin,(because
he had buried himself hopelessly beneath
the wreck of his own boom. He was old,
as everybody was aware ; also, it was sup-
posed that he was bed-ridden, partly para-
lysed by some curious and very rare disease,
incurable, stone-broke, and eking out a
miserable existence on nothing and sixpence
a week at a shabby German watering-place.
And when the Politician who Fled writes
with some vigor from a rather expensive
London address, it is a surprise to his old
acquaintances in the North.
* * • »
This is the plea of the Politician who
Fled :
The great part of Customs duties is raised on goods the
products of the colonies themselves, this has been esti-
mated at from £600,000 to £1,000,000 per annum. This,
the anti-Federalists say, will be lost in Federal collec-
tions. Federalists retort, “ How silly to call this loss, the
money remains in the hands of the people si ill.” Quite
true, but. the argument is a very strong one, neverthe-
less. It is this, that to the extent of that million revenue
must be raised —(1) either by increasing the tariff (which
may not produce the effect), or (2) (which is much more
likely to be the case) by substituting direct taxation.
Queensland must look aghast when contemplating such
an event, for all admit that the direct taxation is much
too heavy now. Some effort is being made to show
Queensland the futility of seeking relief at another con-
vention in which her interests might be represented. I
-nink it essentially necessary to save future grief. The
Convention in Melbourne finished its labors more than a
year ago; yet that time has been consumed by
Wew South Wales mostly in an appeal from the
Convention to get the terms altered a little more
in her interest. Queensland, who has not been
heard at all, has the right to demand that the whole
subject should be re considered, when she can speak for
herself before she takes the irrevocable step of joining
the tederation. We know now the terms on which the
present Convention BUI will admit us. Those terms are
much against our interests, and we know we shall be in a
better position to federate,if Federation be still advisable,
twenty years hence than now. It is amusing to hear
your opponents predicting to us ruin from our isolation
if we do not join now. Queensland owes her strength to
herself, and not to any friendly interest taken in her by
the other colonies. Our friendly relations with them will
continue, and may even increase, although not in the
same Federation ; and when Queensland joins, if she
does join, she will join as a bigger factor in the then
development. If she joins now, she will be yielding a
great deal of ground that we have already won, and must
be content to play the humble instrument allotted to her
in the concert.
* * * *
As a gross and utterly unscrupulous mis-
representation of the case this appeal can
compare favorably with the old balance-
sheets of some institutions with which the
Fallen Idol used to be connected. Queens-
land’s net revenue from customs duties is
£1,160,000 per annum, and not the “great
part ” of it but about a quarter is raised by
customs on the products of the other pro-
vinces. Out of £5,450,000 worth of imports
about £3,200,000, or a good deal more than
half, are from countries outside the proposed
Commonwealth, therefore even if the im-
ports from Australasia paid as much duty in
proportion as those from outside, Queens-
land couldn’t lose more than £490,000 per an-
num by abolishing its interprovincial duties.
The loss of “£600,000 or £1,000,000”
is an utterly impossible bogey which can
hardly impose on any save the most ignorant.
And, as a matter of fact, only about £250,000
of the Queensland customs - revenue, at
most, will be sacrificed by the abolition of
interprovincial duties. The spirits, tobacco,
rice, opium, cigars, cocoa, tea, and the bulk
of the heavily-dutiable articles come almost
wholly from over-sea, and the over-sea trade
consequently supplies the bulk of the cus-
toms-revenue ; the interprovincial trade sup-
plies very little. Queensland’s busted brass
god simply multiplies the possible loss
through abolition of border-duties by four or
five, and asks Queensland to believe that it
will lose 90 per cent, of its tariff-revenue by
abolishing the duties on 43 per cent, of its
imports.
* * * *
In one respect, indeed, Queensland comes
off better than any of the other provinces.
Every one of them, by federating, gives up
the right to collect customs duties on goods
imported from the other provinces of the
Commonwealth. Therefore Queensland gives
up the right to tax 43 per cent, of its im-
ports ; Victoria and New South Wales each
the right to tax 44 per cent. ; Westralia 51
per cent. ; South Australia 54 per cent. ; and
Tasmania 68 per cent. The province which
does the largest proportion of its buying
from its neighbors and the smallest portion
abroad, which is Tasmania, makes the
largest sacrifice of customs revenue. Queens-
land, as the province which buys least from
its neighbors, makes the smallest sacrifice.
And Queensland gains more in another way.
That province, if it federates, agrees to cease
taxing the £2,200,000 worth of goods which
it imports from the neighboring provinces—
which is Queensland’s loss. And the
neighboring provinces agree to cease taxing
the £5,600,000 worth of goods which
Queensland exports to them—which is
Queensland’s gain. Therefore it stands
to make an enormous profit by joining
the Commonwealth—a much larger profit per
head than any other province. A State
which buys comparatively little from its
neighbors and sells them an immense amount
has everything to gain by the abolition of
border tariffs.
* * * *
Then, in addition, the Federal Government
takes over the postal and telegraph services.
Queensland, in 1897-1898, made a loss of
£54,000 on these. The other Australian
provinces among them made a profit of
£41,000, so, when the departments are all
lumped together, the other provinces really
take over most of Queensland’s loss and
divide it among them. It isn’t a large item,
but here again Queensland is a distinct
gainer. After allowing for these things, and
putting matters at the very worst, and leav-
ing a large margin for contingencies, it is
impossible to see that the kanaka State will
have a gap of more than £300,000 a year to
fill by direct taxation. The Politician
who Went Away claims that Queensland is
already so heavily burdened by direct taxa-
tion that it can’t pay any more. Here are
the respective burdens, taken from the latest
issue of Coghlan’s “Seven Colonies,” pages
367 and 416 :
Direct State Direct Local
Taxation per Taxation per
Inhabitant Inhabitant Total.
per annum. in incorporated
area per annum.
Maoriland ... £0 18 2 £lB 5 £2 6 7
South Australia 016 1 013 1 192
Tasmania ... 0 11 8 16 5 1 18 1
N.S.W. ... 011 7 10 2 1 11 9
Westralia ... 0 10 11 018 2 19 1
Victoria ...096 0 18 4 17 10
Queensland ...076 013 3 109
Instead of being heavily burdened by direct
taxation as Thomas M'llwraith represents,
Queensland is the most lightly direct-taxed
province of the seven. And if Queensland,
with the smallest burden of direct taxation,
with no land-tax and no income-tax, and
with the prospect of losing a smaller propor-
tion of its customs revenue by the abolition
of interprovincial duties than any other pro-
vince, because a smaller proportion of its
imports come from the other provinces than
is the case with either N.S W., Victoria,
Tasmania, S.A., or Westralia —if Queensland
dare not face union with all these special ad-
vantages, then it can only be because the
Queenslanders are the one Australasian com-
munity which hasn’t the grit to meet its own
shadow in a lonesome place by night.
* * * *
Queensland, as casually mentioned above,
depends more on the other provinces for a
market than either New South Wales, South
Australia, or Victoria. If Queensland
stands out of the union, then the Common-
wealth tariff will proceed to shut out its
goods from the Federal territory, as far as
possible. And wherever any of the federated
States can supply the others with the goods
which they used to get from Queensland,
the Queensland products will be heavily
handicapped. Victoria sells less than a
third and N.S.W. about a third of her
exports to the adjacent States, so they could
stand out with only a moderate loss. Queens-
land depends on the adjacent States to buy
nearly two-thirds of its surplus products,
and if it stands out, the results in a few
years are likely to be ruinous—or very near
it.
* * * *
The Politician who Went Away preaches
to the country he so judiciously left the
doctrine of delay. He claims that if Queens-
land stands out its “ friendly relations ” with
the federated States will improve just the
same. This is assuming a good deal on no
visible evidence, for if the Commonwealth
adopts any kind of protective tariff the valu-
able N.S.W. market must, to a great extent,
be closed against Queensland until Queens-
land comes in. Again, he urges Queens-
land to lie low because 20 years hence
it will join as “a bigger factor ”
than at present. So it will, pro-
vided it increases in population faster
than the other five provinces—not otherwise.
If all the provinces multiply their population
by 10, then it will be just the factor it is
now. If Queensland multiplies its popula-
tion by 10, and the other provinces by 11, it
will be less of a factor than it is now. At all
events, the Constitution Bill offers Queens-
land rather more than its fair share of Par-
liamentary representation if it joins noiv, not
otherwise —the right to equal representation
in the Senate belongs to Original States only.
And the Queensland democracy has the
solemn assurance of the Q.N. Bank
overdrafter that, if it federates, the result
will be less customs and more direct taxa-
tion—in other words, that the Fat Men of
Kanaka Land are likely to be at last smitten
by the long-postponed land and income tax.
If the Queensland democracy regards it as a
good and fair and wise thing to throw away
Australian unity in order to preserve from
direct taxation a gathering of Fat Men who
have hitherto dodged it with more success
than any other Fat Men in Australasia, the
Queensland democracy has a perfect right to
its own opinion. But if it solemnly declares
at the poll next September that it has that
opinion, The Bulletin would greatly like to
know where it got the opinion from.
A Reconstructed Sydney.
A recent Bulletin article on the above
subject has evoked the following valuable and
trenchant criticism by Alderman Thomas
Hughes, of Sydney :
Your article (24/6/99) only serves to illuminate for a
moment the general gloom hanging over the whole
subject of municipal government in N.S. Wales, and then
to make it still darker by contrast with what has been
accomplished elsewhere. The administration of a
modern city does not begin and end with the collection
of its garbage and the sweeping of its streets, yet under
existing conditions little else can reasonably be looked for
here, and until the citizens of Sydney take an active in-
terest in the affairs of their city, it is almost hopeless to
expect much real progress in the direction of reform.
What use is there in talking about a higher city rate to
be paid by public-spirited citizens for the improvement of
the city, when a few months ago I could only find four
aldermen, out of 24, to support my motion to raise the
rate from Is. 4d. to Is. 6d. merely to provide for the
estimated deficit at the end of the current year, and to
pay interest on a bank overdraft of £120,000 which repre-
sents the accumulated deficits of the past five years?
At present Sydney Oity Council is scraping along in a
parsimonious fashion and practising every variety of
small economy in its endeavor to stop the leakage without
resorting to extra taxation, but with no prospect of deal-
ing with the overdraft except by the time-honored prac-
tice of floating a loan, and passing the liability on to pos-
terity. Yet nobody seems to care, and the sudden energy
shown last year by the Citizens' Reform Committee, and
prompted by the dread of extra taxation, seems to have
died away from sheer lack of interest in municipal affairs.
The one bold attempt to improve the city by widening
Moore-street has proved a dismal financial failure owing
to a combined attempt by many of those charged with
betterment-tax to evade payment on technical grounds,
but an amending Bill will be introduced into Parliament
next session, which, it is hoped, will remedy the blunders
in the original Act. It seems to me that to create and
maintain a healthy interest in city government the fol-
lowing reforms are needed.
* * * *
(1) A broader franchise, extended to all occupiers of
rateable premises (including sub-tenants). This system
works excellently in Great Britain, not only widening the
circle of voters, but increasing the range of choice in can-
didates. In Sydney, many of the best class of city-men
have no voice whatever in city elections. Tenants in
blocks like Wentworth Court, Vickery’s Chambers, doc-
tors’ consulting-rooms, merchants’ offices, &c., have no
municipal votes.
(2) A re-arrangement of the city wards, and an increase
in the size of the Council. Twentj'-four aldermen are too
few to properly govern a city of the size of Sydney.
Manchester has a council of 104, Birmingham 72, Leeds
Sheffield and Liverpool 64, Glasgow 78, and Dublin 60.’
Leeds alone has 15 standing-committees, 40 sub-commit-
tees, and 8 sectional ones. Prior to this year, Sydney had
only one standing-committee—a General Finance one.
Now it has three—General Purposes, Finance, and Works
—but, when special committees on Electric Light and
Garbage were also sitting, recently, it was sometimes
difficult to form a quorum, and one committee—appointed
to revise the by-laws—had to be temporarily abandoned.
(3) An alteration in the mode of election of aldermen.
The present system, by which only one-third of the Coun-
cil retires each year, prevents the possibility of effective
reform. Personally I have always advocated the election
of the whole Council at one time, and the success of I his
system in the case of the London County Council justifies
my view. Public interest would be aroused, and a
definite municipal policy would take the place of a purely
personal issue. My brother's Bill, providing for a general
election of the City Council in December next, has been
passed by the Legislative Council, ami now awaits the
consent of the Assembly in the coming session.
4. VVider powers should be conceded by Parliament to
the City Council. At present it is hampered in every
way. It has no power to resume one inch of private
property, whether for widening streets or any other m, r
pose. It must chaffer and bargain with the owners in
every case, and is helpless if one owner refuses to sell A
Bill containing the necessary powers of resumption is now
under discussion in the Council, and will also be placed
before Parliament next session. City rates are not a
charge upon the properties rated, and are irrecoverable
at law after three years, but yet another attempt will ba
made this year to remedy this. F
• • * .
The Council has no power to borrow money without the
authority of an Act of Parliament, and although the
Sydney public are crying out for some efficient method of
deaung with garbage, and a sub-committee of the Counci
has recommended its destruction hy fire, there are no
funds available for the purchase and erection of destruo
tors. Numerous appointee Boards exercise powers
which properly belong to the Council-e.q., the Water
and Sewerage Board, the Fire Brigades Board the Citv
Improvement Board (now virtually defunct, and with
it, many necessary powers which should be vested in the
Councd). and the Transit Commission (the licensing work
of which is efficiently managed, in Melbourne, bv the
City Council, and the control of the traffic should be as
in London, in the hands of the police). Then again, the
Council regulates the sale of live stock, and the sa e of
dressed meat, but by some singular anomaly it has no
control over the abattoirs, a work peculiarly within the
world* 06 ° f municipal g° verni uent in other parts of the
In short the whole trend of the city government in
modern Europe is collectivist, and is aimed at controlling
all the common services of water, sewerage, gas electric
lighting, abattoirs, tramways, &c., and the’ profits
derived from these services are applied in reduction of
the yearly rates, while in Sydney the tendency appears
to be in precisely the opposite direction.
* • * •
It isn t often that this paper has the pleasure
of speaking respectfully of an alderman, there-
fore it is all the more gratified to be able to
approve of and endorse every word of the
above epistle. But in the way of reform it
would go even a little further than Mr.
Hughes suggests. Its principal ideas for put-
ting the dreadful civic fathers of Sydney and
the 40 suburban municipalities on a common-
sense footing are these :
(1) That every adult male resident should have a vote
after, say, six months’ residence. The great bulk of
adult males are ratepayers directly or indirectly If a
man doesn’t pay rates directly he probably pays them in
his rent.
(2) That the 41 pocket-handkerchief municipalities
which make up the N.S.W. metropolis and a few adjacent
ones (Bankstown, Field of Mars, &c.) should be com-
bined by Act of Parliament under one municipal govern-
ment without W'aiting for their consent. The trouble is
not so much that these tired, ignorant, muddled, garba«-e-
-haunted bodies object to consolidation as that they are
too weary to seriously consider the matter. The metro-
polis can’t wait till they are rested and educated because
meanwhile all improvement is practically blocked. And
it can’t let all Sydney grow to be a by-word and an eye-
sore because municipality No. 39 stands out. If Parlia-
ment wants to be tender with the rights of the 41 munici-
palities let it enact that a vote shall be taken on the
subject on a certain day, and that if a majority of the
municipalities vote for consolidation it shall be carried.
(3) The consolidated city to be governed bv a County
Council of, say, 80 members, so that it can divide itseif
into sub-committees for various purposes.
(4) The new County Council to be dissolved and wholly
re-elected every three years. This would make sufficient
excitement to give the people some interest in municipal
politics. r
( 5 ) The County Council to take over and consolidate
the debts of all the municipalities. These now cost
on an average £4 12s. per cent, per annum interest. By
consolidating them at 3i per cent., which should be quite
easy, the saving would be £26,000 a year. A sinking
fund of 2 per cent, per annum to be made compulsory
both on the old debt and on all future loans.
* » • *
(6) Rates to be levied on land values only—not on im-
provements. The present system penalises the man who
adorns the city with a beautiful building, and lets off
lightly the one whose waste allotment is an eye-sore, and
a goat-haunt, and a deposit for old bashed tins, and a
rendezvous for loafers who curse and play two-up.
(7) All rates to be a perpetual charge on the land till
paid, and to accumulate at 5 per cent, compound in-
terest, and the municipal authority to have power to sell
or let property on which three years’ rates are owing. At
present, as pointed out by Alderman Hughes, rates are
irrecoverable after three years. There are allotments
about Sydney 7, the owners of which judiciously conceal
themselves, on which 20 years’ rates are owing, of which
17 years’ rates are past recovery. The sale of neglected
properties for rates would remove many eyesores.
(8) The municipal authority to have power to order the
removal or repair of dilapidated buildings, fences, &c.,
and, if its orders are not obeyed, to remove or repair
them at the owners’ expense.
(9) The municipal authority to have power to build
bridges ; to supply 7 water, gas, electric-light and electric-
power ; to make new streets (all new streets to be not
less than 90ft. in width); to widen and straighten old
streets ; to resume land and buildings forpublic purposes,
the value to be fixed by arbitration ; to construct model
tenements, public baths, sewage systems, dessicators,
abattoirs, &c. ; to make recreation grounds ; to borrow
money for these purposes, with a sinking-fund attached
to each loan ; and to levy betterment-rates on adjacent
properties which derive special benefit from these im-
provements, as well as ordinary rates, in order to cover
interest and sinking fund. Also, the Council to regulate
street traffic, control the fire brigades, take over the
duties of the Water and Sewerage Board, and do many-
other things.
(10) All municipal accounts to be audited annually by a
State auditor. Loans to be floated for public works only 7,
not to cover deficits in the ordinary receipts. And where
the ordinary receipts fail to meet'expenditure, the State
auditor to have power to fix the rates for the enduing
year at such increased figure as he reckons will meet the
year’s expenditure and cover the previous year’s shortage.
These are some of The Bulletin's ideas on the
subject of municipal reform. Clauses 6,7, 8
and 9 should apply not only to Sydney, but to
all municipalities. So should the proviso about
the dissolution of the Council every three
years. And in addition to all this, the com-
plete Local Government system, for which
N.S.W. has so long been waiting, should
be hurried along. It doesn’t come along mainly
by reason of the old borrowing curse, which
influences every department of N.S.W. politics,
and how the borrowing curse comes in can be
easily seen by looking at the Victorian muni
cipal system. In Victoria, where practically
the whole province has Local Government,
there are shires with few people and an im-
mense area of rough country to supply with
roads and bridges, which can’t possibly do
their work out of rates. Therefore 2nd class
shires receive an endowment from the Treasury
of 245. for every £1 raised by general rates;
3rd class receive 28s. on each £ ; 4th class
receive 40s. on the £ ; sth class get 475. 6d. per
£ ; and 6th class get 555. per £. No Treasurer
has yet had the effrontery to charge the muni-
cipal endowment to loans ; it all comes out of
revenue. So when an outlying shire like Omeo,
Orbost or Tambo spends £l5OO on a new
bridge, the State pays £llOO of it out of
revenue. And if £l5OO is spent on a new
bridge in a similar district in N.S.W. the
State pays the whole of it out of loans.
And because N.S.W., if it extended Local
Government all over the country, would have
to endow the remote shires quite as liberally as
Victoria does, and because it wouldn’t look
well to pay municipal endowments out of bor-
rowed money, and because it is far easier to
pay £l5OO out of loans than £llOO out of
revenue, therefore the N.S.W. Local Govern-
ment Bill hangs fire just as it has hung fire for
the last 25 years or more.


The Anti-Australian Apple of Discord.
Sydney Telegraph, badly beaten in its fight for
parochialism, Sydney monopoly, and newspaper
prestige, is now endeavoring to rob the victors of
the spoils. Its efforts have been brought to naught
by the people; it is now fishing in the narrow
waters of N.S.W. Parliament, where private
jealousies, party feuds, and personal ambitions
enable it to set dog eating dog, and dingo snarling
at dingo.
* » * *
Looking round for tools, the Telegraph finds
William John Lyne —the Man who Wants Hoffis.
Lyne is the discredited ex-leader of N.S.W. Op-
position ; and he lives just now in the Telegraph-
editor’s pocket. He came off the Federal fence at
the Telegraph’s bidding, and proclaimed himself
an anti-Billite and Little-Australian ; and now
the TELEGRAPH, which hitherto has persistently
derided him, is nourishing him on hopes of Hoffis.
Barton, it declares, is no longer Opposition
leader; Lyne is the friend, not Barton. And
Lyne smiles again with hope.
* * « *
The Telegraph’s troubles about Lyne ! Lyne
is a man of no political account—an accident of
the past, an impossibility for the future—and well
the Telegraph knows it. But —any port in a
storm!—and the .Telegraph thinks it can use
Lyne to frighten Barton. It wants Barton to
come down from the Federal platform—come down
from his place as Leader of the Nation —and fight
Premier Reid for precedence in the miserable
arena of N.S.W. Parliament. With Barton and
Reid united, the Telegraph knows the anti-
Federal cause is hopeless. With Barton and
Reid fighting for Hoffis—who knows ? ’Tis a
forlorn hope ! —something may happen to wreck or
postpone Federation. So the Devil takes Edmund
Barton to the height of the Federal referendum,
and offers him all the kingdoms of the Premiership
if he will but cast himself down.
* * * *
Listen to the voice of the tempter : “ You are
the true and natural leader of Federation. But
Reid has usurped your place. You are only play-
ing second fiddle. Reid, as Premier, takes pre-
cedence. He, not you, will nominally lead Aus-
tralia to independence. If there is question of
forming a Ministry for the Federal Parliament, he,
not you, will be called upon by the Imperial
authority. You have time to oust him yet. You
may become Premier yet. He may play second
fiddle yet, if——if you take my advice and drop
Federation while you scramble for Hoffis.” And
there are no horrid hoof 3 and horns about the
TsLEGRAPH-tempter— no wolfish eyes like those
which frightened little Red Ridinghood. He is a
real gentlemanly tempter ; and proposes to be
Barton’s best friend, if Barton only allows him.
* * ♦ *
And the anti-Federalists think that Barton is
so simple a bird that he will be caught with that
chaff—they really do. As if Barton has not now
all the credit of N.S. Wales’s entry into the
Federal movement —which his unselfishness and
patriotism made possible. As if, under the Fede-
ration, Barton will not at once take his proper
place—the place to which his character and talents
entitle him—whoever may be nominal head of the
first party or the first Ministry. As if Barton
was a child, to throw away all the fruits of
national victory for a petty personal triumph.
What fools, after all, these Anti-Federalists be !
PLAIN ENGLISH.
More Blot.
The good old Braddon Blot, which did so
much service with the Parochial push in
N.S. W., is now the prize spectre of Brisbane
Telegraph, which wants to know, in the
grammar of the small school-boy with the inky
collar :
Is it known in the civilised world thatani/ Government
can purposely collect four times as much revenue, or any
at all, in excess of its own requirements ?
That’s an easy one. It is known. Almost
every Government collects money in excess of
its requirements for exactly the same purposes
which the Blot is intended to cover. Under
the Braddon clause it is provided that the
Federal Government shall pay to the various
Local Governments an endowment equal to
three-fourths of the customs and excise revenue
in order to enable them to meet the interest
on their debts—this only until it takes over
their debts, when it wall pay the interest itself
out of the money. Some precedents are :
The Queensland Govt, collects £75,000 a year more
than it wants, and gives it as an endowment to its Local
Government bodies, exactly as the Federal Government
will do under the Braddon clause. N.S.W. collects
£BO,OOO more than it wants for same purpose.
Victoria collects £112,000; S.A., £97,000 ; W.A.
£32,000; Maoriland, £172,000 ; Tasmania, £6OOO more
than they want for similar purposes. Also, they make
large special grants. The Canadian Federal Government
collects nearly £1,000,000 a year more than it wants,
with which to pay subsidies to its States.
Britain collects £8,250,000 a year more than the
Government wants, with which’ to subsidise Local
Government bodies. It follows the Braddon Blot idea
most accurately. According to the admirable Blot three-
fourths of the Australian customs and excise are to be set
apart temporarily as a subsidy. In Britain all the
license revenue, half the probate duties and certain of
the duties on alcohol are similarly set apart.
The German Federation hands back to the States a
large portion of the Imperial customs, excise and stamp
duties. The Government retains a fixed sum, and hands
the rest back. At present, the States get back more than
three-fourths of these revenues; Germany carries its
Braddon Blot further than Australia proposes doing, and
its Blot is permanent—not temporary. Its Federal
Government raises £20,000,000 a year “ in excess of its
own requirements.”
This ought to be a great load off the Brisbane
anti-Federal organ’s mind. Instead of the sub-
sidy idea, as provided for in the Blot, being a
new and reckless experiment, it is exactly the
thing which all the above countries, and others
which it would take too long to enumerate, are
doing, and have been doing all the time. Also,
the manner of doing it is almost an exact copy
of the system which works so admirably in
Britain and Germany.

The Case of Baby O’Callag'ban.
Melbourne has been troubled for a good while
with a mysterious parent. A nurse, one Mrs.
Dillon, received <£9o from somebody in con-
sideration of adopting an illegitimate child.
The child died, which isn’t at all unusual—the
mortality among that sort of child is remark-
able—and there was an inquest. Naturally
enough, the Coroner wanted to know whom he
was inquesting, and demanded that the father’s
name should be given in public court, but the
nurse absolutely refused to give it. She re-
fused with an energy that even the £9O didn’t
seem sufficient to account for. The court
hadn’t a rack handy, and there was no appara-
tus available to brand the witness with, but it
did what it could by threatening her with in-
definite imprisonment for “contempt.” She
still refused. Even if tied by a long rope to
the back of a Bourke-street tram-car, and made
to run up and down the street till she either
perished or imparted the secret, she was re-
solved to die running rather than tell. Then
the warrant was made out, but powerful legal
assistance rose up like a bewigged spectre rising
from the dead, and argued the point bitterly
before the Full Court. The Full Court held
that the Coroner’s warrant was good and the
case started again. But when it resumed all
the cast-iron spine had gone out of the Law.
After all its previous fury it didn’t even ask
the nurse for the father’s name, much less order
her to be suspended by the hair in court till she
overcame her reluctance and spoke. Instead
the Coroner said, in a humble, > hastened
whisper, that publicity would do no good, and
would be painful for the families of both faiher
and mother. Then press and public were
expelled from the court, and the missing father
drove round in a closed vehicle to the back-
door of the morgue, and was silently sneaked
in through the damp room of slabs wherein the
“ found-drowneds ” do congregate. By which
signs Melbourne knows the father isSomebody of
Social Position, for otherwise the Law would have
bawled out his name in open court, and told
him gruffly to get into the dirty box, and kiss
the old diseased “ buke,” and take off his hat,
and stand up straight, and not shuffle with his
feet, and speak up so that the court could hear
him. In the eye of Victorian law the gulf
between the gentleman who occupies a high
position and the fellow who has a job is very
wide indeed. And it is very wide in the eye of
the law’s grovelling servant, Coroner Candler.
That gentleman, so long as it seemed that the
father was just an ordinary person, was solid
on the fact that his name should be given in
public so that the glare of publicity could get
on to it. And when he found out that the
father was somebody he didn’t dare to do him-
self what he had nearly sent a woman to gaol
for not doing—he didn’t dare to do what he
had previously said was the right thing to do.
He just stultified himself, and cringed to the
father of Baby O’Callaghan, and "turned off
the search-light of justice at the meter.

Wanted, a Minimum Wage in N.S.W.
The N.S. W. Law again started out the other
day to help the local slave-owner to re-capture
his two lost chattels. It was the old trouble
over that unspeakable gang of sweaters and
nigger-drivers, the Camel Carrying Co. of
Bourke (N.S.W.). Two of its slaves, both
Afghans, sued it for £2 each wages, and the
co. prosecuted them criminally for “desertion.”
The daily paper report reads as follows :
Naaid then gave evidence. He admitted having signed
the agreementat Bourke, but denied that it was explained
to him, or that he knew its contents. He worked 11
months, a,nd received £3 10s. in wages ; £2 2s. was debited
against him for clothing, but he only got a pair of boots,
a pair of trousers, a blanket, and a shirt. He was cold
and hungry, and had no inone)', so he wanted his wages.
Abdul Wadk, manager of the company, swore that the
agreement was interpreted to the men by the Govt,
interpreter, who was brought over from Sydney. It was
signed by the police magistrate at Bourke, the interpreter
and witness. Plaintiff had worked eight months and 28
days for the company up tn June 19. So far as witness
knew no wages were due to him. For a period of eight
months and 28 days he was entitled to £l7 17s. 4d. His
passage-money, £ll 17s. lid., vyas debited against this,
and £2 2s. for goods he received, leaving £3 16s. 6d. in
cash. That made altogether t'l7 16s. 6d.
Mr. Edwards explained lhat if plaintiff had completed
a full month up to June 22, only ninepence would be
owing to him, but, under the agreement, he was only to
be paid at the rate of 25 per cent. Clause 3 provides that
75 per cent, of the wages shall be paid at the end of the
term if the employb faithfully carried out the agreement.
The Camel Carrying Co. lias frequently been in
Court, pursuing its lost nigger by the aid of
the free and enlightene 1 Cornstalk law, and
during previous cases its smellful policy
was very fully explained. According to
the evidence then given, the men were engaged
in their own country, where they signed an
agreement. When they came to Australia they
were made to sign another agreement which
might, or might not, be quite different from
the first. Anyhow, the first one was never
produced in court. Agreement No. 2 was not
properly explained by the interpreter ; the in-
terpreter swore that the boss of the Camel
Carrying Co. interrupted him, and stopped his
explanation, and insisted on explaining him-
self—presumably in his own way. The men
were engaged for a long term at miserable
wages. Their passage-money came out of the
wages. Their clothes were apparently supplied
them by the co. at its own price. And three-
fourths of the wages were to be retained by
the Camel Co. till the men had completed their
term to the Camel Co.’s satisfaction, the Camel
Co. retaining the right to sack the men
at any time, and thereby prevent them com-
pleting their term, and thus save three-
fourths of their wages. But there was no
corresponding right on the part of the men
to leave at any time ; they were bound to serve
the syndicate which drove the humpy
animal through the wilderness for a long
term of years, or be pursued to the last ex-
tremity of the law. And their food consisted
of anything which the Smellful Animal Carry-
ing Co. thought fit to give them. For the fifth
or sixth or dozenth time the N.S. W. law, to its
own unspeakable disgrace, has upheld this dis-
graceful contract, and the two shivering
Mohammedans, having lost their case for wages,
were sent to gaol for failure to pay costs. And
the Camel Carrying Co. keeps on importing
niggers because N.S.VV. law backs up the un-
speakable sweater. If the law put a large,
honest, Democratic boot through the agree-
ment nigger-importation would cease because
the profit would have gone out of the business.
The Further Contumacy of Annie Smith.
Annie Smith’s primary irregularity consisted
in camping in a culvert in Melt). Royal Park,
for which she was ordered, on Jan. 21, by
Carlton Bench, to proceed to Abbotsford Con-
vent, there to wash the unclean shirt and
mangle the clean-boiled shirt, till further
orders. Tired of the monotony of the mangle
and the wash-tub, from daylight till dark, at
nothing a week, Annie, in company with an-
other inmate, who had been more-or-less com-
pulsorily detained for nine months, escaped
over a 12ft. wall. She was recaptured, and,
with her mate, was sentenced by the same old
Carlton Bench to a further imprisonment of 12
weeks. This term expired June Bth, but, as
this obstreperous girl was just then in the gaol
hospital, and unable to leave her bed, Messrs.
Greenwood and McCrory, J’sP., kindly called
at Pentridge Stockade that morning, and sen-
tenced her to a further term of three months,
on the awful charge of having “ no lawful and
visible means.” The contumacious and un-
seemly behaviour of Annie Smith in failing to
gain a means of livelihood, or even a competence,
while confined in Pentridge, would be evident to
the most ordinary beak, but it is beginning
to strike the callous Melbourne public that this
sort of gaol-trial and sentence is getting too
frequent. Moreover, The Bulletin has ob-
served that when a well-connected prisoner—-an
embezzler or defaulting bank-manager—begins
to suffer in health through confinement he is
usually released on that ground; the prisoner
with influential friends is never sentenced to a
further term in order that he may recover—or
die. Only a month or two ago The Bulletin
commented on a somewhat similar case where
an old lady was sentenced to a further term of
confinement, for no earthly offence, but simply
because, being old and poor, it was hoped the
further term would last till she died, and so
save worry and bother. She duly died, and
was buried on the same identical day that the
girl Smith received her further sentence. Vic-
toria has by this time got itself thoroughly
accustomed to seeing its poor and old and sick
sent to gaol, there to die or recover as the gods
see fit. But if that province is prepared to allow
innocent persons to be gaoled by a couple of
J.’sP., without even the pretence of a trial,
then it might as well do away with its expen-
sive judicial system at once and allow the
police and the honorarium magistracy to run
the whole business. Only a few days later an
old lady who, on June 14, ’9B, had been given
12 months’ imprisonment for “ having no visible
means of support”— i.e., for being poor—was
re-arrested immediately on her discharge and
sent up for a further 12 months for her own
protection ! This, in a pseudo-civilised country
is utterly disgraceful, but it was at least done
in open court, whereas the Pentridge sen-
tences were practically secret. For it is note-
worthy that neither of these secret sentences
or mockeries of trials were even referred to or
mentioned in any way by the Melbourne morn-
ing papers, wherefore the interesting question
arises—-how many more Pentridge confinees are
lingering in that silent hole, never released on
the expiration of their sentences, but sentenced
over again privately, in gaol, without trial, by
casual J.’sP., till they finally die from old age
or disease ? And what has Premier Turner
got to say to all this after his statement in the
House last session that no more indigent per-
sons would be sent to gaol ? And what has
Chief Sec. Peacock to urge in extenuation of
these disgraceful star-chamber methods of
administering alleged justice ?

Holt’s “ Pardon.”
John Holt, convicted at Adelaide August
Sessions, 1898, of garotting, and sentenced to
ten years with hard labor, has been declared
innocent, and was released on June 24, after
serving nearly a year of his sentence. The
evidence at the trial was, as pointed out by
The Bulletin just after the conviction, flimsy
in the extreme. The prosecutor, O’Dea, was
assaulted and robbed on the night of 7th July,
’9B, by three men on Frome-road, a dark avenue
on the outskirts of Adelaide. One of the men
struck him from in front with an umbrella, and
stunned him. O’Dea was unable to see clearly,
as the spot where the assault was committed
was particularly dark, and there was no moon,
neither was there a lamp in the vicinity. All
the men got away. On the same evening, about
an hour after the robbery, Detective Jones,
when passing through Victoria-square—half the
city away from scene of robbery—arrested
Holt on a charge of vagrancy. (At that time
the assault and robbery had not been reported).
When searched by the detective, nothing, save
a few coppers, was found on the prisoner, al-
though O’Dea’s watch and chain and some
gold and silver had been taken from
him, and there bad been only an hour in
which to get rid of the plunder. Holt
was next morning placed in a row with
other prisoners, and, after some hesitation,
identified by O’Dea as the man he didn’t see in
the dark. The latter’s evidence at the trial
convicted the prisoner—there was absolutely
no other evidence of any kind that had any
bearing on the case. Holt, found far from
the scene of the crime almost immediately
after it, with none of the plunder on him, was
convicted solely because O’Dea swore he was
one of the three men who struck him in pitchy
darkness. Holt protested his innocence when
the verdict of guilty was returned, but he
got 10 years. What has happened since
his incarceration has not become fully pub-
lic, but it is announced that the real
offenders, who are now “ doing time ” for
another offence, have confessed to the crime and
entirely exonerated Holt. The police ridiculed
the confession at first, and fought hard to keep
their prisoner, but, after inquiries spread over
four or five months, and an investigation by the
Chief Justice, Holt has been pardoned for an
offence he never committed, regains his liberty
with the magnificent douceur of £2 in his
pocket, and the question of compensation is
being considered ! Holt’s case is another
instance of how “ police identification ” works.
The Provincial Sunset.
There are no more provincial parties in N.S. W.
Parliament. Freetrade and Protection have
gone—and Premier Reid announced the other
day that the great war-cry of Cobden was no
longer a shibboleth to him. Even Ins and Outs
have gone since Opposition - leader Barton
joined hands with Premier Reid to secure
Federation. The only parties now existing are
the Federalists and the anti-Federalists, For
and Against Australia ; and the only question
before the country for the next few months is
how to settle administrative business as quickly
and quietly as possible, while waiting for the
complete revolution in the old order of things
which Federation will accomplish. Sydney
Telegraph’s attempt to galvanise the old
jealousies, and (ostensibly) shove long Bill
Lyne into Hoifis is doomed to disaster. Even
the Antis have swallowed the situation. No
Parliamentary opposition can override a 25,000
majority on a mass referendum, with the biggest
vote ever taken in the province. Prudent men,
who think they can get into the Federal Par-
liament, are plotting out constituencies, and
balancing House of Representatives against
Senate. Even imprudent men who think they
can get in are calculating chances. The throat
of N.S.W. Provincialism is cut. Austra-
lia, like a new Governor, has made her
private landing in the launch, and her formal
entry into her kingdom is only for a little
deferred. Everywhere men are adapting them-
selves to the New Order, and everywhere the
wattle-bud of nationhood is bursting into bloom.
Yet, hoping against hope, Sydney Telegraph
raises the old banner of “Party Strife!” and
rallies the faithful Garbage-trees; and Diogenes
Haynes, the old Irreconcilable, cowers in his
tub to catch the last setting gleams of Provin-
cialism, and calls upon Australia to “Get out
of my sunlight ! ”
♦
N.S.W. Labor party decided that its members
should have personal freedom with regard to
the Federal Bill, and Sleath and Ferguson
thereupon decided to fight for the measure.
The local political labor leagues in their dis-
tricts screamed at them and denounced them ;
and yet, in these genuinely-Labor electorates,
the voting was—Wilcannia, 5 to 1 for the Bill;
Sturt, 3to 1 for it! White Cliffs, where
Sleath’s head was declared to be unsafe from
“an indignant populace,” polled—Yes, 436 ;
No, 133. And Sleath had only one chance of
addressing a hurriedly-summoned meeting there
on the night before the vote. The local Labor
Leagues have a perfect right to scream if they
want to, no doubt; but it is clearly about time
they gave up the idea that theirs is the scream
of the working-men voters. As a matter of
fact, though Sleath and Ferguson were the
only “ traitors ” among the Labor-party, 15 of
the 19 electorates which Labor men represent
gave majorities for the Bill, and in most cases
overwhelming majorities into the bargain.

“ T’othersider ” writes from Westralia :
Federation here suffers much at the hands (or mouths)
of the “ leaders,” while flourishing- in the hearts of the
people—if such an anatomical reference may be per-
mitted. Premier Forrest is lying low, saying nothing.
An awful Scawtch person named Matitieson wastes much
public time in dull and deadly analysis of the alleged
finance question. Mathieson is determined to get into
the Commonwealth Parliament. He is said to favor the
notion that he is helping on Federation ; but if there is
any danger to the cause it is his flatulent finance. Asso-
ciated with him are two or three other dishwater politi-
cians such as flourish only in Groperland. The erratic
Vosper is doing a little harm and very much good for the
Commonwealth Bill. Meanwhile Federation has caught
on with the people. It is seriously stated that" if
Parliament tries to defeat it the Coolgardie and
Kalgoorlie miners will organise an agitation for a
separate government, and having been Imperially
divided from the Forrest country, will sue “ on their
own ” for admission to the Commonwealth. No anti-
Bill barracker has yet ventured into the goldfields. The
National League (of isolationists) held its first meeting
in Perth Town Hall, and received the derisive laughter of
an audience that turned out to be Federal with absolute
unanimity. The “National” League now submits
motions at its meetings favoring the submission of the
Bill to the referendum, so, after all, it is not too
blind to see a hole in a ladder. One Leaguer, auctioneer
B. C. Wood, M.P., suggests that it will be time enough to
have the referendum next March or April. However, the
sweeping N.S.W. majority for union has served to bring
matters to a head, the idea of the Commonwealth forming
and Westralia out in the cold being the very stimulus
needed to thoroughly rouse the urban population.

What would be the use of “a monster petition
to N.S.W. Parliament” against the Federal
Bill ? The Bill passed at the referendum by a
majority of 25,000 votes. Now, a vote is a
tangible, authentic, officially-verified act, but,
as it is utterly impossible to verify the signa-
tures to a big petition, which may be (and
nearly always is) signed over and over again by
the same individuals, and by children, a petition
means absolutely nothing, as compared with the
result of a properly-taken poll. Therefore,
even if the Cabbage-tree Mob manage to whip
up an anti-Bill petition bearing what purports
to be the signatures of three-fourths of the
N.S.W. electors, that petition will, beside the
25,000 majority-vote at the referendum, not
amount to as much as a row of pins.

“ Kennedy ” preaches an old-age pension ser-
mon :
An octogenarian recently charged, at Melb. City Court
with “ no visible,” was a 50 years’ colonist, without a
mark against him. The Salvation officer ran his eye over
the veteran ; no use to him—couldn’t work ; and'several
ladies and gents said there was no room in their charit-
able institutions, so the police conveved the colonist of
50 years to his last home—the Gaol. Yet, I know of men
and women who have spent their best days in gaol or on
the town who are now ending up a useless life in the
charitable institutions. Why? Because thev had dona-
tion-friends who paid for them for, say, a year. Couldn’t
turn them out when pay stopped ; and now Mr. Mealy-
mouth comes round with a gang of visitors and points
out with pride—Sal Smith, 50 years in gaol, or Bill
Sikes, done 50 years in gaol, now “ seeking salvation ”
The visitor weeps over them, and brings them comforts -
while the old colonist wears the broad-arrow and eats his
hominy ! These so-called “ charitable ” institutions, in
fact, are full of donation-patients, at 20s. or more a month
payable to collector by self or agent, and, of course, it is
to the collector’s interest to recommend same—for his
percentage, and doubtless a little oil from other end.
For an alleged Tory Govt, the Salisbury-
Chamberlain combination Ims dealt heavy
blows at Fat-Man. The Workmen’s Compen-
sation Bill and Automatic-Coupling Bill were
good democratic measures. And the Bill to
assist workmen to obtain dwellings of their own
is absolutely socialistic. This is a huge scheme
for turning every municipal or county council
into a land-bank for the purpose of advancing
money to the poorer classes for the purchase of
homes. It applies only to people who desire to
purchase dwellings of the value of £3OO. or
under, and in their case provision is made for
advancing four-fifths of the purchase money at
very low interest. The chief drawback is that,
owing to the accursed tenement-house system,
only a small proportion of the working classes
can find homes to purchase which will answer
the prescribed conditions. Yet, so far as the
agricultural districts and the small towns are
concerned, it is a great socialistic idea and a"
smasher for landlordism.
