Would any decently-discriminating musi-
cian have “discovered” Amy Castles? Amy
Castles without the Age and the Akgus ? With-
out her complement of admiring multitudes press-
ing from afar ? Candidly, no. She sang in Syd-
ney Town Hall to 3000 people last Wednesday.
The house was full, but not in a state of jam-
pack ; that is to say, there were seats unoccupied.
Miss Castles came third on the programme. She
entered in a short white muslin frock, and white
shoes and stockings—a simple little figure en-
circled with the pathos of youth that aroused
one’s sympathies. She was warmly received, and
opened fire with “ Qui La Voce.” Her upper C,
and thereabouts, showed off well in this selection’.
Those high notes are her future at present. They
rang out brilliantly and powerfully, and “ Qui La
Voce gave them an opportunity of exhibiting
themselves often. Why speak of her technique?
Is it fair to demand technique ? Even to com-
ment on it ? It seems to writer that the voice, as
a natural organ, is all we need concern our-
selves with at present. She can buy a lot of
technique. And the voice, as a voice, is not
an extraordinary voice ; not a voice that would
move this listener to deeper admiration than
some other Australian singers do—than, to choose
local products unstamped as yet with the European
hall-mark, Florence Schmidt, with her round,
liquid notes, and Hetty Holroyd, whose voice re-
sembles Amy’s. The upper octave is beautiful
and powerful and charms with its breadth. The
middle and lower registers are again the trouble—
the old soprano trouble. Think of the sopranos
who can throw out superb high notes ; then think
of how sadly few are those whose middle registers
correspond in their own sort of quality. And it
seems strange that critics have classed her as a
soprano drammatico with such a poor base to her
vome. It must be with these fine head-notes that
Amy Castles has slaughtered Melbourne ; for
what else is there that is unusual in her organ ’
She has a tendency to get out of tune. That is
dangerous. She is not satisfied to sing girlishly.
She irritates by assuming a common, genteel sort
of expressiveness (in her singing, not in her
manners, those are as simple as could be). She
sang Tosti’s “ Good-Bye ” in encore, and betrayed
with the first verse what the floridity of “ Qui La
Voce had partially concealed, that there is not
much breadth of mind working behind.
* * * *
tt “ Falling leaf and fading tree ” and
Home, Sweet Home” mean nothing to Amy
Gastles, for she presents them to you with no
meaning. Age and tuition will help her to assume
much of that sort of thing, but one demands some
sort of indication now that the strange, elastic,
emotional soul of a great singer is there in embryo
before one can predict a great future. She sings
and she sings, and no more. With the end of the
song the song is ended. Nothing remains with
the listener. Making all allowance for her
and <£4ooo it will probably be
tbOOO before she leaves Australia—you will
never carry away a wealth of emotion from Amy
Gastles singing. Some young voices have in
themselves a quality that touches, even though
the undeveloped songstress herself knows not
how to touch and thrill. But, unfortu-
nately this moving quality is not there to
assist Amy Castles’ big organ to hide a poverty of
understanding*. One can safely say
she has a beautiful voice—a big voice—which cul-
ture should bring to a respectable cash value.
JNlot its present cash value ; one can scarcely con-
Slder respectable. It sprang out of an inex-
plicable boom. There is nothingto justify thecrowd
in trumpeting her as “ sensational ” and “pheno-
menal ” ; m breaking chairs and tearing off a man’s
ear in a struggle for seats. This scribe has heard
her sing “Qui La Voce,” Tosti’s “Good-bye,”
Home Sweet Home,” “The Wind in -the
1 rees, Hear Ye Israel, ” a Hope Temple ballad,
two local compositions, and, in common with thou-
sands of others, has been moved to nothing* more
enthusiastic than Wfiat a big voice for a girl
m short frocks, with her hair down ! ” The hair-
down and frocks-up lend a sort of false perspec-
tive to the voice. It grows larger by comparison
with these young small things. And it isn’t a
very youthful voice, for it suggests a long period
of weird practising and teaching. At her second
concert it flagged through sheer fatigue, and refused
high notes m the Hope Temple ballad. If there
are any kind friends of hers in Australia they
should carry her away at once to rest, and nourish
her voice on vocalising pap like scales and breath-
ing exercises that will teach her to save what she
is now throwing away so recklessly. For every
head note produced as she produces it, and flung
as she flings it at an audience, means so much the
less chance of a great future for Amy Castles.
* • * *
Probably it is that last sentiment that has
been moving certain Sydney critics to express
themselves with candour over her concerts. Free-
man’s Journal for instance. Freeman’s Journal
lately delivered itself of the following:
This wonderfully irifted young lady happens to be a
devout Catholic, and as it is an axiom of the infidel creed
that one who believes in God and practises virtue must
be totally bereft of either genius or ability, the Sydney
organ of infidelity, The Bulletin, cast its piercing' glance
upon her. 6 b
The Melb. representative of The Bulletin, a
personage afflicted with the vice of telling the
truth as he understands it, at that time had
spoken of her voice as “large, round and fairly
sweet,” but that it failed to thrill. Freeman’s
Journal has now heard Miss Castles itself, and
down it climbs to comment thusly :
Why she was classed as a true dramatic soprano is a
puzzle. She is not a true dramatic soprano of the grand
air, and the ringing tones of dignity, breadth, and
power. Nor is she in the best sense one of Nature’s
sniffers. Having ifiven the voice—a rich (rift — Nature
would seem to have been niggard in supplying musical
sentiment and musical instinct in any common measure.
• • • People had taken Melbourne opinion on its word
and honor. The disappointment experienced, but good-
naturedly concealed, must have been keen.
Looks as if Freeman’s Journal had been casting
‘ a piercing glance ” also. Or can it be that that
pious print is nourishing a viper in its bosom?
* * *
It was left to Miss Castles’ matinee concert
to produce anything extraordinary in the way of a
house. That really was a surge, chiefly a bon-
netted one. The crowd overflowed on to the
organ platform, tilled the aisles, and crammed itself
into all available standing or squeezing room,
Before three o’clock the front doors were shut,
but already the crowd inside was far too big for
the house. The passages leading to the hall were
blocked with struggling women and children, who
fought for admission for half-an-hour after the
concert had begun, and then streamed home
angrily. It was the largest concert house ever
seen in Sydney, quite eclipsing the Albani racket.
* * #
The author of “ One Summer’s Day ”
describes it as “a love story in three acts,” and it
is probably the best description he could have
arrived at. It is a love story pure and simple—an
idyll that begins and middles beside a blue, glassy
river, and ends under the stars on a lawn—a clean,
refreshing little tale which will be appreciated by
every nice girl who has a bath regularly, and
keeps her hair neat and her skirts fresh, and
doesn’t wear holes in her stockings and trail her
boot-laces behind her. Also, it will be appre-
ciated by the fellow who is after that girl. And
as the fellow and the girl are very many, “One
Summer’s Day ” should do good business at Syd-
ney Royal, though The Bulletin doesn’t care to
bet on it. Sometimes the public catches on to the
quaint, pretty, refined play, and sometimes it
rejects the same with scorn, and calls out for the
large, raw, gory, eight-inch beefsteak of drama—
the 6d. kind, without a tablecloth, and with the
big, mangled loaf dabbed down beside it on a
bare board.
* * * •
The “Summer’s Day” is beautifully
staged. It runs two-thirds of its pleasant existence
in a hayfield beside a river (river supplied by the
lamented George Gordon and his son) in which
the reflections of the sun and the trees are painted
with startling fidelity. A cheerfully irrelevant
small boy who fishes with a disreputable worm
during two acts, and blackmails the young man
who is mashed on the gipsy and the doddersome
old man who is partly mashed on her also, is a
commendable feature hereabouts. The small boy
has no direct relation to anything, but young
Sydney Smith makes of him a most valuable
essay on the fiendishness of small boys in general.
Brough is the hero of the play, and Brough, as a
handsome, serious, elderly man of the world, is a
very great success. The Romany woman of Mrs.
Brough—a fiery, much-married gipsy, with long,
bare arms that stretch out to claw jewellery and
foolish boys who can get jewellery, and Miss
Is oble, who is the other half of the love story, are
practically the rest of the show. The latter is one
of the best things that this clever young
lady has done ; it is a narrow part of the
tender, clinging order, but she plays it for all
there is in it. Ihe fat lady with the vegetables in
her mou th and the other people are little more
than incidental circumstances, though Miss Bessie
Thompson is a brilliant success in 'stout, elderly
ladies of uncertain temper. Altogether, the ver-
dict on ‘‘One Summer’s Day ” is distinctly favor-
able. Everyone grows weary now-and-then of the
large, underdone beefsteak of stage-literature, and
this dainty item is a pleasing change. And it
seems to have a beneficial effect on the character.
Almost anyone who had any character at all would
speak a little more kindly than usual to the eat
when he got home after seeing it.
** * *
On Saturday night a great human tide
poured into the Criterion to see Curtis’ Afro-
American Minstrel Carnival. The show was
opened with “ My Creole Sue ” by the whole com-
pany. This immediately challenged recollections
of Rooks beautiful melody at the Palace a week
before. And Rooks’ exquisite gem gleamed on
serene and undimmed. Master Blutch was twice
enthusiastically encored for his comic song about
a girl who stole his heart away. Then Robt. Logan
threw down his glove to Gerard Millar, of the
Palace, by singing “Asleep in the Deep, ” which the
former had boomed so magnificently seven nights
before. Logan can boom too, but bis boom is to
that of Gerard Millar as an American organ to
the one in the Centennial Hall. The Master of
the co. is Ernest Hogan, “Actor, Author and
Comedian.” He is evergreen, full to the brim
with human fun, and his personality is altogether
delightful. The Olio was opened by “The Ken-
tucky Four, ” and they defiantly wave on the
programme a <£looo challenge to the world to pro-
duce their equals. The world would have a pretty
large contract but it would probably get through.
Ihe Bronze Patti ” gave an operatic selection
showing a wonderful range of voice. Then a lone
black figure rose up in the gallery, and the gods,
with blood-curdling threats, ordered him to sit
down or be disembowelled on the spot. But he
was part of the show, and stilled the tempest by
bursting into song. Then Siren Navarro, the Lilli-
putian Creole contortionist, reeled on, and for nearly
10 minutes was so tangled-up that someone declared
she was a cleverly-arranged optical illusion. The
average audience always has a hazy suspicion that
clever contortionists are optical delusions.
“Black Dante ” appeared, and mystified the audi-
ence by making real roses grow out of pots of
sand by placing an empty cone over them,
by making “ spirits ” write from dictation on
the inner side of two slates tied together. Then
he borrowed sundry rings which he hammered flat,
and fired from an old horse-pistol at a box in the
d.c. In the box were found sundry other boxes,
in the innermost of which were all the rings but
one. The lady of the missing ring became
clamorous, and, while search was being made,
Black D. went among the audience with a
bottle, out of which he gave anyone who asked
as much rum, whisky, brandy, or gin as he liked
to take. Then the bottle was broken and found
to contain a white rat, perfectly dry, with the
missing ring attached to its neck. After this, the
audience was pretty well broken up, and didn’t
properly recover till it found itself in the street at
11.30, and all the pubs closed.
** * *
The present Sydney Opera House season
has kept on its two thin legs longer than Opera
House shows usually do, but a few comedies like
“ Tit for Tat ” should kill it deader than Abel of
Genesis. The new comedy, if it started punctually,
and cut its intervals down to five minutes each,
ought to finish about 9.30. It seems to be mostly
interval—long, weary interval like the great sandy
desert of time dotted all over with the bones of
dead minutes and hours. There is something in
the play about a man who has assaulted a ticket-
taker on the railway and is committed for trial,
and who has decided to fill in his last night before
he surrenders to his bail at a supper. After he
has gone to the supper his bail springs a leak, and
the _ police come and arrest a musician who
is in his house and talking to his wife.
Then there is an interval, and the Bony
One with the scythe and the dumb-bell puts
up a record of 15 or 20 minutes before the
play resumes. In Act 11. there is a supper and a
duke with dusty boots ; the hero is very drunk,
and pretends to be a baronet, and tells a confused
tale about a cockatoo ; and a gaol governor is also
drunk and pretending to be a baronet. And, in
Act 111., they meet in the gaol and find that they
aren’t baronets. W. J. Montgomery brightens up
the show somewhat, partly by being drunk, and
partly by giving way to natural excitement when
he finds another man of the same name in the
gaol, and learns that the other man has been
arrested in his wife’s room. But he sheds a great
gloom by calling himself Sir Adolphus Fitzalba-
tross. Mr. Walshe brightens things somewhat by
also being drunk; and the most cheerful thing
about the supper party is the presence of a lady
called Miss Fitzbattleaxe. Another lady sings
something about “ tum-ti-um-ti-ay, ” and some-
body else sings dreadfully. A cold night, and a
thinly-peopled theatre, and a short, thin show,
and the cold, cold wind whistling eerily down the
spacious corridors of these great intervals, make a
sad combination of events.
** * *
“ The Stranger in New York ” has failure
written so largely upon him that his career comes to
a well-earned close on Friday. “A Trip to China-
town ” will be revived on Saturday. This paper
has a vague recollection of having once said un-
kind things about “Chinatown,” but it now takes
them all back. It has seen a lot of musical comedy
since then, and in the light of later experience it
begins to regard it as a very meritorious produc-
tion. Certainly it is one of the best of its class,
and leagues ahead of the present lamented enter-
tainment, so The Bulletin hopes to fix a kindly
eye on it next week.
** * #
At the Palace the organ-like vibrations of
Gerard Millar’s voice still nightly thrill spell-
bound hearts, and Leon P. Rooks, Turner Jones,
Mabel Hearde and Flora Batson make sweet
music that can only be likened to the far-off
falling of many waters.
#* * *
The tremendous doings of Mr. Saunders
and Mr. Stanford have raged at Sydney Lyceum
all the week, and the decapitated remains of the
latter will fall into the basket at a late hour for
some time longer. The public beheading of Mr.
Stanford is a piece of thrill which comes dan-
gerously near to being overdone. Something
severe might be said about the wildly-ridieulous
executioner, who, just when the public begins to
despair of things ever coming right—it is five
minutes before closing time, and Mr. Stanford’s
career of iniquity is still in all the fragrance of its
bloom —suddenly settles matters by executing the
wrong man. But in a play where there are 125
horrors, all more or less ridiculous, a trifle like
that is hardly worth mentioning.
** * *
Mr. Cinquevalli has balanced the unstable
billiard-cue upon his brain all the week in the pre-
sence of large audiences, and the two billiard-
balls on top of the cue have kept up in a marvel-
lous fashion. How the Tivoli juggler does it in
this cold weather, when the draught blowing
through his tights would naturally make his teeth
chatter, and the chattering of his teeth would
naturally unseat the top billiard-ball, is a mys-
tery. The cannon-ball, too, must be very cold to
handle just now, unless it is aired at the fire
before using, but none of these things appear to
trouble Cinquevalli much. His popularity is
growing. The first night he did the most amazing
things so easily that everybody had an idea that
they were easy—quite easy. But, since then, the
public has been trying to balance the billiard-ball
itself, and, not having a cannon-ball of its own, it
has been practising with a big lump of coal, to the
great disaster of both its feet; also it has tried,
in the seclusion of its bedroom at the hash-tower,
to keep all its luggage in the air at once, with the
result that the umbrella promptly got into its eye,
and the portmanteau knocked it endways and
most of the furniture as well, and rebounded off
the bedstead and wrecked the wash-basin. The
Sydney publio has a vast respect for Cinquevalli
now.
«* * *
At Melb. Royal the bloated, irrelevant
millionaire is still getting murdered with much
success in a gorgeous first scene. The balance of
Bland Holt’s show continues to get along about
as well as could be expected. “ Straight from the
Heart ” has the brand of Vane—Sutton Vane—
upon it, and the workmanship of that modern
English drama-maker is evidently getting worse
instead of better as the demand for his goods
increases. He doesn’t take the trouble to weave a
thread of probability into his yarn, therefore the
company, with the exception of’ millionaire Styan,
act like people who have been engaged to talk by
the yard till the piece runs out. Styan is merely
under contract to drop his aitc.hes and make himself
opulently unpleasant for lialf-an-hour, and know-
ing that the wages of vulgarity will be an
interesting death from the dagger of Albert Nor-
man he rushes to his doom with enthusiasm. The
millionaire is just beginning to appear the main-
stay of a promising drama when Norman cuts
him down in his prime like a turnip, and he ex-
pires gently on the drawing-room floor. Subse-
quent events fail to justify this prompt removal of
the aitchless plutocrat. The burning ship is a
bright incident enough, likewise the horsey tableau
on the Algerian coast, and the guillotine at early
dawn; but the plot and dialogue appertaining to
these stage pictures are poor stuff to set before a
mid-winter audience. Bland Holt would do well
to revive one or two of his last season’s successes,
The Bulletin is thinking.
** * *
“The King’s Musketeer” is getting
through a-third week at the Princess’s, and the
artistic naughtiness of Miladi’s bedroom-scene
commands fairly warm support. All the same,
there is said to be some local religious feeling
against the play, on account of one or two matters
which would not be missed if they were cut out.
The passing suggestion that Cardinal Richelieu
once set his apostolic cap at Anne of Austria and
was rejected by that impressionable Queen is an
absurd detail, anyway. Also, sundry little,satires
on Miladi’s easy piety are unpalatable to the Faith
which, having no capacity for laughing at itself,
gets indignant on the least excuse. The satires in
question, written for a country where the Catholic
vote is not a serious consideration, seem to have
been overlooked by the importing Firm. There is
no falling-off in the death-rate among the Car-
dinals guards, and his Majesty’s joyful regret on
learning that five of them have been killed as
usual goes a long way towards commending his
royal legs to the audience’s sympathy.
** # *
The indisposed Keziah recovered her usual
health with great agility, and is now running up
and down a ladder at Melb. Bijou, assisted by the
two other Keziahs. Likewise Harry Shine, the
Englishes, Wallace King, rolling Woodson, and
the Rickards co. in general are doing their best to
keep a prosperous business up to the mark.
** * *
John Fuller’s Mynorama, which has started
a Saturday night season at Melb. Town Hall,
seems quite bright enough to satisfy the chronic
yearning for a “bob ” entertainment. Many large
colored pictures of London are cleverly produced
on the screen, and the discourse of the incidental
lecturer, although rather hard and unfeeling in
the delivery, contains interesting material apart
from its jokes, and some of the jokes carry their
age well. Songs by John Fuller, Miss Minnie
Waugh, Miss Hettie Warden, and basso Hahn
are scattered amidst the Myriorama. All four
artists achieve the desired encore, especially John
Fuller with his light, sugary tenor ; likewise the
ladies are fair to see, but the selection of songs
might be less “popular ” without losing the
applause of low-priced congregations. Melbourne
ballad concerts lately have made the mistake of
rating the popular taste a little too low.
** * *
Benno Scherek’s orchestra continues to
take the platform at the Town Hall on Saturday
afternoons, and the reputation of that rational
musical band is growing and expanding like a cab-
bage in the springtime. Scherek chooses high-
class music of the kind that even a bank-director
can understand and admire, and the orchestra
treats the programme as though it were a labor of
love, with a pecuniary profit hanging thereto.
Everybody (except Marshall-Hall) speaks admir-
ingly of these concerts. They are excellent, and
the man who tries them once, in preference to taking
his usual dose of football-match, comes back next
Saturday afternoon for some more of the same
music.
*m * #
Fred. Villiers, the war correspondent and
artist, is to appear at Melb. Athenaeum Hall this
week, with his up-to-date story of how Villiers
and Gen. Kitchener carried Khartoum by storm.
The top of the lamp that hung over the Mahdi’s
Tomb before it was burgled by the British forces
is included among the lecturer’s exhibits, and,
what with his stirring narrative and his fierce
imperial appearance, the success of old friend
Villiers can be guaranteed in advance.

machines, the same system as has been adopted by the
Govts, of South Australia, Queensland, and Western Aus-
tralia. Mr. Ferguson has a large stock of thee machines
(suitable for meat-preserving co.’s dairies, butchers, and
others) on hand at present, which he is selling at greatly-
reduced prices.*

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
The South Australian Government proposes
taking another wise step in the direction of State
Socialism, by making its State Land Bank also a bank
for the receipt of deposits and the issue of notes. With
Government security behind it, it will be the safest place
to leave deposits, and its notes will be the safest paper
currency, in Australia. The usual Tory groan and lamenta-
tion about the rash innovation of State banking will
doubtless come along, but, as a matter of fact,
it is much too late to talk about State Banks
and Land Banks as innovations, Queensland, India,
United States, Canada, and dozens of other coun-
tries have State-note issues, some of them of very long
standing. Norway’s State Bank is now 47 years old, and
prospering greatly ; also, the State has a large interest in,
and wholly governs, another bank. Sweden, Austria, and
many other countries, have State banks of long duration.
As for State or State-governed land-banks, they are scat-
tered all over the earth and date back as far as the early
days of the oldest inhabitant.
* * * •
The proposal of Maoriland Govt, to extend the
operations of the State Land Bank to advances on town
and suburban lands is the adoption of an idea which The
Bulletin strongly advocated when the Bank was first
established. There is money to be made for the State by
borrowing at low interest and lending at rates a shade
higher, and by extending the operations of the Bank to
towns as well as to farm-lands, the State can do a bigger
business and make more money. A large bank can be
worked at a lower percentage of cost than a small one,
and by extending the business, and thus reducing the
cost of working, it will be possible for the State to
lend to everybody, including the farmer, at lower
rates. And, in a sense, the city landlord is quite as
important as the farmer; if either of them borrows,
probably from a foreign capitalist, at needlessly high
interest, then there is so much extra money
going out of the country, and the country in
general is so much the poorer. Moreover, if the town
landlord must needs be foreclosed upon for arrears of
interest it is better that his property should go back to
the State than that it should be foreclosed upon by a
private individual. The M.L. State Land Bank com-
menced by lending money at 5 per cent. But it has
prospered so well that a reduction, probably to 4)4 per
cent., is to be made this session. And when the in-
stitution grows still larger there is no earthly reason
why a 4 per cent, rate should not be practicable.
* * * *

Sydney Mont de Piefce shares have lately had a
heavy fall —from say 31s. 9d. to 265. or under—consequent,
it would appear, upon those in the inside running be-
coming aware that the co.’s coming div. will be 8 per cent,
instead of 10.
« - * * *
S.A. Govt, gives expert help to wine-shippers at
both sides of the water—here, through the Export Dep6t;
in London, through the province’s cellars, where Burney
Young, a locally-trained man, does the preliminary sort-
ing and the pushing. Results Shipments last year
totalled 138,645 gallons, or nearly double those of the pre-
vious 12 months ; and the names of 500 wholesale buyers
added to the customers list. Still, sales have not in-
creased as rapidly as export ; and stocks are accumulat-
ing on the Other side—mostly to the great benefit of the
quality. \
N.S.W. Judge Owen decides that Sydney Fire
Brigade is entitled to salvage for extinguishing fire on a
vessel lying alongside a Sydney wharf. The owners con-
tended it was the brigade’s business to save property
without looking for reward; and the Buteshire’s was a
test case.
* * * *
S. A. Govt, four years ago took over the manu-
facture of its own water-pipes, with the result that in
that period £29,090 has been saved as compared with pre-
vious contract prices.
* * * •
Silverton tram shares have lost all the advance
which they gained in the last 12 months in consequence
of the increased activity in the sulphide business. The
modified slump is the result of an awful fear that at
length the State will have horse-sense enough to take
over the line or jpuild a competing one ; and as the State
could build the necessary 30 miles of line for £50,000 or
so and would not look for 50 per cent, a year on that
outlay the co., which has had a fabulous 12 years
innings, is naturally anxious. The proposal for a Govt,
line to go via Binnacles and Thackaringa is now before
the Railway Commissioners for report; and the prospects
are that it will go on to the Works Committee in the
coming session. Ear-western members’ political lives de-
pend on their fighting for it hard—if fighting is necessary.
* • * *
The Bulletin office often has a lot of ore in
sight, and though the practice of sending it in has never
been encouraged, this paper receives too many requests
for gratuitous assays and opinions as to value and treat-
ment of samples submitted. Arrangements have now
been made for testing, by assay and otherwise, samples of
ore, provided the sum of £1 is sent with each to cover
cost. Assays alone do not serve the miner’s purpose,
hence certificates supplied to Bulletin correspondents
will not. only give a statement of metallic contents—
owners will be advised by an experienced, practical metal-
lurgist as to the best methods of treating the ore in bulk,
whether by wet or dry crushing and amalgamation, smelt-
ing, cyaniding, chlorinating, etc. Parcels should not be
less than lib. nor more than 101 b.
* * * *
Substantial-investment advisers have been point-
ing lately to Broken Hill stocks as being worth more
attention. So far, Block 10 has been most in fashion ;
its deep-level development caught the eye first. Also its
management gives such ample information that one can
tell what it is doing, what a big margin there is for the
coining zinc-extractor to work on, and approximately
what its prospects are. Still, it does not necessarily
follow that a mine with a working at 800 ft. is better than
one whose deepest level is at 400 ft. Block 10’s ore is par-
ticularly heavy in zinc, and this product is becoming of
value ; but then Block 10 has sold nearly half of the
“ zincs ” it has produced, while the others have theirs
stacked at surface. Block 10 has 100,000 shares ; Block
14 has 100,000 also. But Tens are quoted at four
times the price of Fourteens. Block 14 is most miserly
with its information. Its reports are patterns to avoid.
Nobody knows (from them, at least) what the ore in the
mine is vvorth, or what percentage of metal is being ex-
tracted. If gen.-manager Greenway were not so good a
metallurgist, shareholders would not stand it. The co.
owns a first-rate plant, all paid for ; and it will not be
surprising if it makes the first move in the direction of
zinc-extraction. The mine has very great resources of
ore of rather under-average value. It is the least likely
to do anything sensational ; it is about the most likely to
go oil giving moderate returns, and to reap the first fruits
of local zinc-treatment. (As far as can be gathered, Mr.
Greenway at present proposes only local zinc-ore concen-
tration.)
B.H. South is much like Block 14, without,
however, Block 14’s carbonate reserves. Besides, it has
to go deeper for its ore ; and, spite of all they say
in prospectuses, it is in precious few cases that mines do
“ improve with depth.” The South is worked on much
the same principle as Block 14 ; but it lias nothing like
the same assets. . Also the South Co. consists of 200,000
shares. The shares in the Central (Sulphide Corporation)
are just getting on the Australian market. The Central
is probably the biggest mine at Broken Hill outside the
B.H. Proprietary. Practically nothing is heard of it this
side ; but its development has been surprisingly good,
and its management, if somewhat expensive (hut, then,
an Earl is its chairman), is excellent. The British early
met with disaster, unparalleled in Australia, the most im-
portant of its workings caving in ; ever since it has
suffered from the handicap. It has been too busy battling
for daily bread-and-butter to thoroughly prospect the pro-
perty ; when it has felt that way somebody has begun a
law-suit against it—-or something else has happened. It
can only do very little things at present ; but there are
possibilities about it which do not belong to some others.
Junction shares have been sustained by big
buying on English account ; but for that the mismanage-
ment must have brought prices far below the level
ground. There is not much prospect of a dividend this
side of Christmas. Then the mine should do almost as
much as the South —and the South is valued in the market
at something over £170,000, which is more than twice the
Junction’s present value. Still, the Junction is not worth
more than Block 14, and that is valued at £105,000. It is
worth observing that while South’s have advanced 100 per
cent, in 12 months, Block 14’s have remained practically
stationary. (A good deal of the South’s rise has been jus-
tified though ; and un i :' rround developments have been
somewhat better than Block 14’s.) North’s have ad-
vanced about 110 per cent., mostly at the expense of the
Junction North—which is quite in accord with what the
Wild Catman three years ago, and many times afterwards,
said must happen. So far as appearances go, the Junction
North is a mine in miniature, and, comparatively, a
pretty expensive one to handle. The North has improved
its prospects every month, and must be treading on the
heels of the dividends which it has had an eleven years’
chase after. When it catches them they will not be
large, but they ought to be steady ; and when zinc treat-
ment comes along, it will get a particularly big lift.
The uncertainty of the silver and lead markets
is, however, a big element in appraising Broken Hill
investments. Movements in silver affect the B.H. Pro-
prietary, Block 10, Central, arid Junction most, in that
order ; movements in lead affect comparatively the South,
Block 14, and North most. The B.H. Prop, and Block 10
are best prepared to take advantage of any development
in zinc metallurgy ; Block 14, and perhaps the Central
and British, are most alert in the matter. It is simply
impossible to say which would gain most by the discovery
or the evolution of a zinc process ; it is enough to say
that all will gain so much that details needn’t be con-
sidered until the thing itself happens. Present prospects
are that, directed by the success of the German metal
firm (the Australian Metal Co.), some treatment will be
evolved, a beginning being made with magnetic concen-
tration and the shipment of the product to the cheap
fuel and labor of Europe.

North Centrals. —The little boom in these stocks

would take them. Annoying no doubt to the boomsters,

* 9 * *
Re the over-capitalisation of mines, it has taken
three members of N.S.W. Chamber of Mines to write a
paper on this subject. By-the-bye, how many members
of the O.M. are there whose hopes of making a good
thing don’t absolutely depend on over-capitalisation?
Mr. Harrie Wood, who read the paper, remarked that
although he had bestowed much time on the subject he
could not satisfy himself as to what was over-capitalisa-
tion. Mr. George Robson suggested that “it would be
instructive if they prevailed upon Minister Cook to place
in writing what he meant by over-capitalisation.” For
Mr. Wood’s benefit, writer remarks that over-capitalisation
is brought about in numerous ways. Pegging out some
miles of river-frontages, creeks, or alluvial flats at a cost
of a few pounds, and asking the public to find all the
money and take the whole of "the risk for half the shares,
with or without cash, is one form of over-capitalisation.
To get a mine under offer, with or without an insignificant
deposit, arid to succeed in placing it at three or four
times the price regarded as quite enough by the mine-
owner, is another form. In fact, it is almost impossible
to carry on the business of mining flotation without over-
capitalisation, so degraded has the industry become—es-
pecially in London, where everything, including news-
papers and guinea-pigs, has to be nobbled, and the mine
and the contributing" shareholders have always to carry
this additional burden. Sometimes the mine proves itself
able for it, but mighty seldom. The Minister’s written re-
ply, asked by Mr. Robson, might consist of two words,
“Mount David.”

• V * *
Blayney Mining and Smelting Co., N.S.W. —
With fair weather the first 120-ton blast furnace should be
running within the next month. 1000 tons of sulphide
ore already calcined ; two more heaps of black ore burn-
ing, and heaps of oxides and other raw ores ready for
smelting. In a month or two the co. will be ready to
treat ores of almost any sort. The directors are confident
they can compete against Dapto or any other smelting
works for all ores mined within, say, 100 miles of Blayney.

* »' * •
N.S.W. Minister Cook says he means to stop
the shepherding of mining leases for the purpose of sale.
If his determination is .to be much more than a vigorous
expression, he can count his bands full for the rest of his
official term. Next to good gold comes good shepherding,
where the speculative value of a mining lease is concerned,
and less ingenuity has of late years been employed in the
extraction of the gold than in whistling such tunes to
the sitting Mines Minister as will induce him to remit the
labor conditions. Even though it take all his time, how-
ever, Minister Cook should try to give effect to his deter-
mination. No other Parliamentary business at which he
can employ himself would, if he but half gain his end,
profit the country more. But, unfortunately, N.S.W.
Mines Department is chock-full of similar expressions of
determination made by his predecessors.

A Bulletin correspondent from Mt. Malcolm,
W. A. :—“ Have just returned from Mertondale, scene of
the sensational find by P. Merton. The Reward claim
looks as rich as has been reported. Without doubt it is
a great show. To date, she has crushed some 200 tons
of ore for about 1700 oz. gold, of the estimated value of
£6500 sterling. Half of the ore crushed has actually been
raked up off the surface. W. Callaghan, Merton’s part-
ner and mate in the Australian Peer claim, is seeing him
for a share in the Reward claim, and, meanwhile, develop-
ments will he retarded, hut the manager is actively pre-
paring for the erection of a 10-head mill, with power to
work 20. However, the claim may be only a kidney—in
fact it is in appearance very much like the Emerald mine
at Yalgoo, on the surface of which £40,000 worth of gold
was got, but below it was worthless. The Merton
lode is an ifon - stone formation, and a line of
reef can be traced for about three miles north and
south, the whole of which has been pegged, most of
it with the expectation of a boom. On the north end of
the Reward claim are two leases held by Geddes and
party and Robinson (Merton’s manager) which have every
appearance of developing into good properties ; on the
south end there are only two properties vvorth mention.
The Plying Pig (Drew and party) and another claim held
by tributors are both looking well. The line of reef is a
very narrow strip of country surrounded by a barren
plain of granite. Those holding the shows mentioned
above seem to be in for a good thing, but for the man who
comes behind nothing is left. There are already three
stores on the ground, to supply about 70 men.

sfs ♦ ♦ ♦
Dick Hamilton, the Boulder manager,
threw another bombshell into the directors’ camp re-
cently by a cable declaring that next crushing would be
under 25dwts. ; that it would be impossible to maintain
even this average, as some of the stopes were depleted,
and that the mine’s oxidised ores were being rapidly ex-
hausted. All sorts of excuses have been given, but these
statements cannot be specifically denied. There is a lot
of oxidised ore left in the mine, but the quantity is small
compared with that already taken out. The Boulder has
enormous reserves of good-grade sulphide, but that is
another question. These reserves have been known for
two years, but no attempt has been made to treat them
outside the infamous milling contract. Were such a con-
tract made by any Australian board, not only would the
directors be “ fired,” hut it is very doubtful whether they
would ever get on another board in the country

According to Western Argus (Kalgoorlie) Lake
View Consols will have no difficulty in maintaining the
May gold production for two years without encroaching

* * * «
At present Collie coal is profitably sold in Perth
(W. A.) at 17s. 2d. per ton, while the Newcastle article, by
reason of freight charges and other things, costs 255.
The margin is big enough to make the local coal-owner
feel dead-sure of getting the whole local market to him-
self if he would only dig out enough coal to supply the
local market, and it is also big enough to give him a fair
chance of being able to compete with N.S.W. coal in
Adelaide market, if he would dig his product out. Yet
of the six big coal leases on Collie field four are lying
idle, and another only makes a pretence of being worked.
The groper is almost too weary to pick up cash when it is
lying on the ground in front of him.

Mfc. Lyell.—ll furnaces now running, making
it one of the biggest smelting plants in the world. The
erection of others decided on. “ Now certain that every
ton of ore raised on Mt. Lyell field will be treated by Mt.
Lyell Co.” Not very consoling this in a country where
haulage is so mountainous. It would probably he more
to the point to say that it is now certain that Mt. Lyell
Co. has a mortgage on every mine around it. The co. is
now erecting 56 coke-ovens of its own at Port Kembla,
in Illawarra district.
* * * *
S. Australia is getting used to the awful Social-
istic doings of the Kingston Govt. —it has even left off
predicting that Red Ruin must follow any departure from
the principle of private enterprise. It stood unmoved
successive announcements that the Govt, would provide
cyanide plants, batteries, smelters and concentrators, and
that Govt, prospecting parties would be equipped ; and
now it has not turned a hair at the news that the State
has been running a mine for a couple of years past. The
simple, unheroic statement of the Minister is that the
Govt, had a battery and a cyanide plant at Mt. Torrens,
that alongside was a mine which would yield just about
enough to pay expenses, that there were men wanting
work, and that it was just as well to keep the plant going
with ore from this mine when private owners didn’t supply
it with enough to go on with.
Judge Dash wood comes down from the Northern
Territory, where he has B]>ent half-a-dozen years as Govt.
Resident, gleeful over the N.S.W. Federal vote. The
N.T. is too big a thinff for little S.A. to handle, he says.
As to mining: “ Most exaggerated reports have been
issued, for the London Stock Exchange chiefly. Yet we
have excellent mines—l think they will he the founda-
tions of our progress. The Chinese system has meant
running along the rich parts of the reefs, discarding all
else.” Chinese miners on wages get ss. or 6s. a day. As
to Asiatic markets—“ Not frozen meats; no. But we
can do a big thing in a live-stock trade. The Asiatics
like to do their own killing.”
The first of the S.A. Govt.’s concentrating
plants will be erected at Mt. Fitton, in the far North ;
copper abounds there. The high price for the metal has
not only put the old Blinman mine to work again, but it
has led to a co. being formed to re-open the Sliding Rock,
Both of these were once almost famous northern mines.
And the old Kapunda mine, further south, is under offer
to an English co.

Chillagoes, after running up to 475., have

reason for the rise, there is every justification for the fall.
They may go to 305., or they may stay where they are,
and within' a few days they may go to 475. again.
Speculators who can extract satisfaction out of this tip
are welcome to it.

While the Sabbath Shellback seems to have
largely retired from the mining-tipster business, John
Norton’s Truth has come into the field as a picturesque
boomster of N.Q. copper-mines. According to this new
authority, there are at Mt. Garnet “ 200,000 tons of ore
containing 10 per cent, of copper, 15 per cent, of
silver (ye gods!), and a small trace of gold.” Also
alleged that the gold and silver will pay for work-
ing and smelting alone. Well, 15 per cent, of silver
ought to. It is predicted that the mine will pay £9
per share in dividends every year. “Of course there
may be some flaw in these figures, but I have gone into
them very carefully and have verified them by every
means in my power, and although it is an astounding
position for a mine to be in, I verily believe that these
figures will work out. Then it is given out that Mt.
Garnet is “ only a circumstance ” to Chillagoe, where there
are two leases having bigger ore-bodies of richer ore
than Mt. Garnet. There appears to be one lode 30ft.
wide, which, at lowest computation, is going 20 per cent,
copper. Then there is another, 130 ft. wide, which so far
has averaged 30 per cent. On Red Hill lease there is a2O
per cent, lode 30ft. wide, and on two of the leases alone
there are over 500,000 tons of ore which would go con-
siderably nearer 20 per cent, than 15 per cent, copper.
And it can be all mined and smelted for -375. 6d. per ton.
Yet Chillagoe shares are at a beggarly 38s. or so.

Sohlapp—called in Westralia “Herr” —is
making his first trip to the new alleged copper-mine on
McKinley ranges in Cloncurry (Q ) district, some 200 miles
from Winton, the nearest railway station. After his trip
with Gov. “ Jerry ” Smith through Westralia, Sohlapp
will enjoy making this one alone and will be able to start in
the mornings at the reasonable hour of six instead of at
noon. Schlapp’s luggage while travelling largely consists
of a toothbrush and several other articles, weighing less
than lib., while Gov. Smith’s weighed several tons. It
must have been an appalling spectacle to see Smith’s
luggage being put up on the coach before or after
Schlapp’s.
* * * *
The South Tharsis Copper Co., Mt. Lyell,
have lately entered into a contract with the Austral Otis
Engineering Co., South Melbourne, to supply a complete
crushing and concentrating plant for treatment of their
ores, which, are mostly low grade. The plant is to be a
very complete one and comprises compound steam-engine
and boiler of 100 horse-power, 3 rock-breakers, 2 sets Roger
rolls, 8 4-compartment eccentric jiggs, 4 improved buddies
and Otis circular vanner, with the necessary trommels,
&c., for sizing and classifying the ore. The plant
has a capacity of 100 tons per day, with provision
for 200 tons. The Otis Co. are also supplying a self-acting
tramway from the mine to the mill: also, a pumping plant
to supply the mill with water from the dam.
* * * *
Waihi (M.L.) mine maintains its place as a
gold-producer. Its last four-weeks’ return is a record,
£25,723 being obtained from 7,890 tons ; and the total
value of bullion produced now tops the million. During
the last twelve months the capital has been doubled by
the issue of another 160,000 shares, but the divs. and
price of shares have remained unaltered. The co. has, in
fact, doubled its capital, doubled its divs., and doubled
its share-value. Gold won

Here is an affecting little S.M. Herald
item :
, young ladies of St. Patrick’s school paid a visit to
the foundling hospital at Waitara last Thursday, each
girl taking with her a parcel of clothing, tins of con-
densed milk, and some biscuits for the little inmates.
After a happy day spent in picnicking and nursing the
little foundlings, the girls, many of whose parents also
attended, returned home.
Hum! “Tins of condensed milk.” Bulletin
wonders what the brand was. The average make
of condensed milk—consisting, as it does, of skim-
milk and sugar—is about as deadly to infants as
diphtheria.
* » s. * •
The single girl with one unwelcome infant
in her arms is apt to regard herself as unlucky,
but in Melb., last week, a poor girl fell down be-
side the Sydney-road and gave birth to twins ere
she could be removed. The doubly-unfortunate
one and her family were eventually carried to the
Women’s Hospital, and, as there were no com-
mittee-women about to demand her marriage-lines,
she and the youngsters were admitted.
ALL IN GOOD TIME.
“ I say, old man, you do look seedy. You ought to
have a change."
“Oh, Fm going to Wife’s going up the
mountains next week.”
In unfortunate baby mite, at Port Melb.,
a few days ago, being seized with convulsions*
was given a dose of castor-oil. An hour or two
later it had another fit, and, the mother being
absent from home, the father placed it in a hot
bath, with the result that the infant was badly
scalded, the skin peeling off legs and feet and
portion of the back. Two hours later the child
had a third fit, whereupon the mother gave it
another hot bath, with the result that it came out
of the fit in five minutes! Three hours—three
whole hours—after the horrible scalding episode
the family speculated in some liniment for its par-
boiled offspring, and in another hour the miser-
able 2J-year-old had gone where callous and
ignorant parents are unknown. An illogical jury
duly verdicted accidental death. The absurdity
of putting a scalded child into a hot bath is a fit
and suitable circumstance in a community which
wastes itself on carrying to the heathen the bless-
ings of civilisation and the “ Gawspel.”

“ C.C.” writes :
In view of the child of two years or so that casually
strayed into the bush t’other day—and, so far, hasn’t
strayed back a {rain—and in view of the many children
that yearly boil themselves in washtubs, or roast slowly
at too-easily-attained fires, it strikes one as strange that
lower-class mothers don’t tether up their children
out of mischief as they do the other live-stock about the
place. A system of leg-bail would keep little Johnny in
order better than the rope’s-end. The Chinese have dis-
covered this, just as they have that other plan of teaching
a baby not to yell by putting a hand over its mouth when-
ever it starts, till the baby reasons that the pressure is
caused by the yell, and yells no more. Consequently,
Chinese children, never needing to be slapped and
banged and chivvied here and there, grow up into the
most engaging and sweetest-tempered youngsters imagin-
able. Besides which, thej' have a chance of growing up,
as the Chinese, not being a civilised race, prefer to do
their infanticide out-and-out, and not by the slow-roast
plan
• • * *
It isn’t the city alone that has its mean
tragedies, in which contraband infants form the
central figures. At far-out Wilcannia, the other
day, an unwedded mother was charged with
murder; and here is a fragment of the police-
evidence :
The mother (who had taken the police to an outhouse,
where she admitted the ohild was kept) picked it up ;
it had some little calico bags over its hands, tied round
the wrists ; the mother said they were to prevent it suck-
ing its fingers and making them bleed ; when the mother
picked it up it clawed at her breasts ; the child was a
mere skeleton.
It appeared also that pumpkins formed part of the
12-months Told infant’s diet. The girl was even-
tually discharged, with a characteristic backblocks-
beneh warning never to do it any more.
* * • *
A poor old outcast preached a more
trenchant sermon—an old-age pension sermon—
than ever did priest or parson to the Victorian
public in Melb. the other day. Retiring in ’93, at
the age of 60, from the Railway service, after 25
years’ work as a shunter, he struggled along in
great poverty, camping latterly in a rickety cot-
tage in S. Melb. Rinally, the owner started to
pull the shanty down, and when but a single room
remained, neighbors heard the old man weeping
bitterly. Next morning, workmen, looking in,
found him lying dead, his throat cut. He had
helped to build up Melbourne and the province,
and his reward in his old age was the gaol, the
knife, or the Yarra.
The Bulletin lately complained about
the way in which interpreters were picked-up
anyhow, even in important cases, out-back in
N.S.W. Several times recently Afghan camel-
drivers have either sued or been sued, and an
agreement has every time been an important
feature of the case. Yet, generally, one or other
of the officers or servants of the company, which is
alleged to have got the agreement signed anyhow,
has done the court interpreting. That happened
on the first occasion when this awful agreement
was mentioned—at Tibooburra, months ago—and
it happened the last time it was mentioned —at
Broken Hill, the other day.
* * * *
A lucid interval in the hysterics of parson
Gladstone, of Nathalia, is evidenced by the pas-
sage in one of his discourses in which he
denounced as unspeakable rubbish the columns of
dress-descriptions with which out-back papers
load the local ball. A country editor of the
writer’s acquaintance used to invite each woman
to write a par. detailing her habiliments—and
how the women jumped at the chance ! He said
he adopted this course when he found that if he
employed one or two women to do it they would
glorify themselves and debase their rivals.
Another editor got his wife to do the work—but
the poor woman died suddenly. There was no
inquest, but there were suspicions. Of course,
the country paper follows in this matter the suit
of the city papers ; but the example is bad at best,
and with the machinery available out back only
its worst features can be reached.
* * * *
The good Mr. Gladstone isn’t the first
parson who has tilted at the manners and the cus-
toms of Nathalia. A few years ago—it was in
Mr. Gladstone’s day, nevertheless—a Wesleyan
sermoniser who was permitted the run of the
“ Notes about Town ” column in the local sheet
made remarks therein about the strange things
that happened along Broken Creek. That parson
was unmarried and didn’t have much luggage—a
circumstance particularly fortunate in view' of the
hurried departure that he found expedient. And,
strangely, the “ call ” wasn’t to a higher stipend,
either.

A press application for the names of the
guests at a recent Adelaide vice-regal ball was re-
fused, on the ground that their publication would
hurt the feelings of the people who were not there.
But, then, think how it would have pleased the
folk who were there !
An appeal from
“Tom Collins ”
Dear B.—Give spirit-
ualism a show. Such
questions are not set-
tled while you wait.
Seeing- that the origin
of life is jealously
and justly claimed
as a subject of scien-
tific research, why
should an ulterior
phase of the same
life be left in the
domain of dogma,
or be altogether ig-
nored ? “ Looking
before and after ”
that is the idea. In
stopping half - way,
we stultify our own
pretensions, for sci-
ence is a unit. If
seschatology
be honestly
challeng-
ed as a
branch
of mor-
al sci-
n ce,
THE HUMORS OF
AGE.
YOUNG ENQUIRER:
“ I suppose it feels
rather funny to he
old? ”
ANCIENT : “ Sometimes; but it all depends
on the rheumatism.”
dogma will be choked off all its prey, except
Matthew Arnold’s “three-fourths of life” —and we
demand this residue for science, under the name of
ethics. But asschatology must be captured first.
And, by the way, ecclesiastical hostility to all inquiry
into spiritualistic phenomena is in favqr of their genuine-
ness. In each manifestation there are three factors : (1)
the normal individuality of the inquirer ; (2) the medium
of communication, the bridge from the known to the un-
known—table, vocal utterance, spontaneous writing ; (3)
something beyond ; and the exhaustive analysis of this
something is a legitimate task for science.

News of the death of the S.A. Governor’s
uncle, Mr. Arthur Tennyson, reached Adelaide
during a ball at Govt. House, but was consider-
ately withheld for the moment. The Governor
gave up a military social two days later, but after
that carried out his social engagements.
* * * *
Chief-Justice Way made some scorching
observations the other day about the monumental
incapacity of the South Australian J.P. Three
of them had sat on an affiliation case at Queens-
cliffe; had found that defendant, despite his
energetic oath to the contrary, was the father of
plaintiff’s child, and ordered him to pay medical
expenses and 7s. 6d. a week. According to S.A.
law there must be at least one witness in such
cases who can directly or indirectly corroborate
plaintiff, and in this case all the solitary corrobo-
rator was prepared to swear to was that, eleven
months before the birth of the child, he saw
defendant alone, whistling and leaning against a
wall. And he didn’t even say where the wall
was. Seemingly, a wall is a dangerous thing to
whistle against in S.A.

Dear Bulletin. —Be ’bus-top
women. Struck a passengerless Waver-
ley ’bus, took seat on top, and lit a
pipe. Three so-called ladies followed
me, and one set to work to frown
my pipe out. Stood it for a bit, and
then solemnly told the woman that
really I was awfully sorry, but the
driver wouldn’t let me smoke inside.
She took the hint and got down.—

The picturesque Eastern garb
has its disadvantages. In a Melb.
suburb recently a Syrian hawker
called, and persisted in pressing
purchase till the door had to be
slammed in his face. Even then
he didn’t go. He rang and
rang at the bell till the
house-father, rushing to
the door with a bludgeon,
found that the dusky
gentleman had been se-
curely moored to the establish-
ment by his robe, shut into the
door-case.

N.S.W. Justice Owen, in chambers, inter-
preting the jury- verdict in the libel-action Hutchi-
son v. Star decided that “ independently of ” did
not mean “in addition to.” The paper paid .£5O
into court, and the jury awarded <£2oo “ indepen-
dently of ” the money paid in. But as the jurors
were available, why were they not recalled and
made interpret their own decree ?
TO WILL OGILVIE.
We drink a toast to your fair-haired girl,
But we drink again to the dark-tressed maid.
For you there is charin in a golden curl,
But for us there’s a magic in either shade.
We drink again to your horse that’s grey,
But we drink again to the steed that’s black ;
And our hearts go out to the brown and bay,
And the chestnut, too, when he heads the track.
No fault we find with the grey or gold,
Of either sort there are good and bad.
So to each good horse here's a rider bold :
And to each kind lass here’s a lively lad.

appears the legend :

IN THE BATH.
An acidulated correspondent discourses on
“The Country-Hospital Clique
The hospital clique in a N.S.W. country town is a
recognised evil. The cause is not far to seek. As soon
as a medico is appointed.it is his business to quarrel
with other practitioners, to appoint satellites of his own
as nurses, and to get a name as a good fellow first, as a
good doctor secondly. He is backed up by all the would-
be “toffs” who want cheap adoration as patrons of
charity, literature and sport. These are then smuggled
on to" the committee. One device. At, say, Boolah-
mine station there are 200 men during shearing. Sup-
pose them to average a crown each, the dollars are
bunched into groups of four under one local name, thus
securing 50 votes and fulfilling the law. Next a touting
circular is sent round to the cookies, sawmillers and con-
tractors. Once the clique is established the original ap-
pointers are afraid to jib. The consultant medical
officers are ignored ; the very committee hectored and
flouted Oases needing skilled assistance are conducted
in camera. Death under the charge of the appointed
surgeon requires no inquest. The P.M. is suborned, the
police chin-chinned, and when rusty patients are ejected
“vi et armis,” loafers are run into the beds. Nurses
play cribbage or tennis when moribunds can only appeal
with the eyes, voice and action being gone. A staff ot
winter valetudinarians and hypochondriacs are ready at
the muster, yearly or half-yearly, to reduce the death-
averages, and all goes merrily until the matter becomes
too gross for print. Even then it must be screened by
the sale of the doctor’s practice—his health or character
having given way under his multifarious dues not
duties. „
* * * *
Nothing ever happens in Westralia with-
out champagne popping. Even inquests are often
thus opened. There were dozen of corks drawn
at the opening of Perth Mint. Gov. Gerard
Smith presided and delivered a couple of speeches
after the manner of the “all of the olden time
school,” crammed with elaborate compliments,
punctuated with as much bowing as if he were
dancing a minuet with a poker in his long but
aged back. Premier Forrest started the machin-
ery, and the Bishop of Perth started the lunch
with a muttered grace. Admiral Pearson was
there, and made a speech of a single sentence to
the effect that a man should not talk about what
he did not understand. Those who thought this
was a joke laughed, and, strange to say, some of
those who thought it was no joke also laughed.
Thus was the Mint declared open.

Brisbane 0. of E. folks are getting par-
ticularly lively over the Sunday-closing business.
The same highly-respeotable religious body owns
one of the finest hotels in the city—re which pos-
session it was recently severely chidden by one of
its own clergy. Now, almost every hotel in
Brisbane slakes the thirst of the weary citizen on
the Sabbath, Is aforesaid hotel libelled by those
who say that it also (despite its sacred connec-
tions) dispenses the long beer and the short
whisky to the casual wayfarer?
* * * *
He was a Queensland legislator appealing
to his constituents to return him again in : 99, as
they had done in '96. On the platform behind
him sat his wife —-a lady as ardent in political
warfare as himself. The constituents were lively
and uproarious. They had had enough of him,
and plainly told him so. The howling was simply
terrific, and the M.L.A. almost dropped down on
the platform in disgust and despair. Of a sudden
his wife rose, clipped him in her arms, and kissed
him affectionately and defiantly before the whole
audience. And even that didn t mollify them.

“F.C.” on a Melbourne scandal already
here mentioned :
Melbourne’s soulless commerciality—which has led it,
for long, to gaol its sick and old poor—has made it a scon
and a byword among the provinces, but it has lately ex-
celled itself by gaoling a man who merely asked for work.
At Prahran Police-court, June 13th, a destitute, blind
person applied to the bench to see if that agglomeration
of wisdom could put him in the way of earning a living.
The soulless J’s.P. promptly responded by charging him
with vagrancy and remanding the matter for a week for
inquiries. At the end of the week an investigating con-
stable informed their worships that prisoner had been
earning 20s. a week in the “ Blind ” Asylum, but thecom-
mittee of management had changed his occupation to the
extent that he could now only make 12s. Whereupon he
had made unseemly observations, and had proceeded to
the local police-court to ask if it knew where he could
find a better-paying job—with the result stated. The
outraged beak, on learning the character of this blind
villain who wanted to earn more than 12s. a week, froze
on to him with great violence, and remanded him for
another week. Prisoner protested, and characterised the
proceedings as a disgrace to the country. Which was un-
doubtedly true ; but as the province of booms and stinks
is impervious to any feeling of shame over its treatment
of its poor, the gaoling of one blind man more or less is
not likely to much disturb its equanimity.
* * * *
A most dangerous and pernicious practice
is being allowed to spring up in Vic. County
Courts. There are two or three barristers always
briefed on one side or the other of County Court
cases, and very often a Judge is kept waiting for
a couple of days doing nothing, merely because
Mr. X. or Mr. Z. is engaged before another Judgein
a previous case ! The other day there was a Court
idle, a Judge idle, with a long list to get through
and, when inquiries were made as to why a second
County Court was not set going, it was frankly
stated, “ Oh, Mr. X. is in the next case on the list,
and he is engaged in the case now proceeding.
The Sandgroper is a long-suffering person
and slow to wrath. For 20 or 30 years the people
of Perth have noticed an ungainly hump in the
middle of Hay-street, and some of the more ener-
getic of them have wondered why it was, or have
thought that they would wonder some other day,
when they felt stronger. At last Perth decided
to go in for tramways, and in cutting up Hay-
street to lay the rails a huge tree-stump was found
just below the surface. The early road-makers
were too exhausted to take it out, so they
made a hump in the roadway to cover it, and
generations of gropers went up that hump and
down the other side, and took it as one of the
mysterious dispensations of Providence.

A long, hairy Scot was prosecuting two
pickpockets in Melb. City Court last week, and
identified as his a coin found on one of the
prisoners. “ Will you swear that shillin’s yours?”
asked the accused. “Ah weel,” responded the
witness. “An’ how do yer know it is?” per-
severed the suspect. “ Becaws Ah’ve had eet in
ma posseesion for twa years agone.” That ended
the case. Prisoners pleaded guilty, and prosecu-
tor left the court triumphantly wi’ his shullin’.

Fifteen hundred Mackay kanakas were
recently examined for leprosy by Q. Govt.’s medi-
cal officers. “ Only three suspects were dis-
covered,’’yells a Govt, paper triumphantly. “Only
three suspects ! ” Here’s a ground for gratulation.
How many certainties were quietly put on one
side for deportation?
• • * •
Goulburn Gaol Gov. Mackay’s idea of set-
ting his prisoners to farming, and making them
grow potatoes for the gaol community, looks one
of the most sensible ideas yet applied to local dis-
cipline. The men are kept at healthy and profit-
able work, and trained to a useful calling, instead
of being left to eat their hearts out in confine-
ment.

“At Launceston (Tas.) Benevolent
Asylum, one day recently, a mother, aged 90
years, applied to have admitted her son who had
reached the age of 72 years. The super., in ad-
mitting the youngster, told the mother that if he
misconducted himself he would be returned to
her for correction.”

* * * *
Nearly all the Chinese in Australasia are
from the southern provinces, mostly Canton and
Amoy (writes a Bulletin man from Shanghai).
Recently, however, I met a Kangsi man at Chin-
kiang who staggered me. “ You savee Sydney ? ”
said he. “ You bet,” said I. He then told me
that he was for five years gardening at Waterloo,
and knew the lamented Louisa Collins. As an
E. News reporter I witnessed the hanging of that
lady, and to hear about her here in China, was
indeed a staggerer.

“ Puyyaka ” on a recent question :
The hairy man does not attract the average woman. A
certain number of female reversions to a very primitive
type are gratified by the display of a hirsute masculine
limb, but the smooth-skinned athlete is the beauty-man.
The doctrine of Evolution gives a few pointers. Mans
descent from a hairy ancestor is plainly shown by the
lanugo or woolly covering of the foetus which persists for
a period after birth as an abundant downy coat covering
body and face alike; but he became smooth-skinned soon
after he branched off from the ape-like form Darwin
characterises this as a secondary sexual development,
women being less hairy than men from the first, and
exercising sexual selection in favor of the smoother can-
didates. The hair-loving female is either a morbid pro-
duct or a Homopithecal reversion. As an indication of
bodily strength, hair is a fraud. Consumptives often
become magnificently hairy, and the hirsute growth over
diseased localities in the body is well known. On the
other hand, the majority of exceptionally powerful men
are remarkably smooth-skinned. The writer several
times examined Eugene Sandow, and found his elastic
satiny skin almost devoid of hair, only a liirht down
being apparent on legs and forearms. Daring a
talk on the strength question Sandow affirmed that
hairy men were never as good as smooth ones for top-
notch strength work, although they were frequently the
stronger at the commencement of the training course.
Subsequent inspection of a number of his pupils and of
their records bore this out very remarkably. Therefore
the hairy man may hereby consider himself as a reversion
towards a primitive type, and his female admirers should
get measured for a bear-skin and cave. The Maoris had
a proverb that “There is no woman for a hairy man,
and they were an up-to-date race in physical
especially where sexual selection was concerned.

GWENDOLINE.
They made you of sin, Gwendoline ;
They lighted your eyes at the Devil’s fires,
And a dimple was set in your chin.
And your hair was a mesh of gold—
And ’twas woven by imps in Hell,
From butterflies’ wings, and cobwebby things,
That cling and shimmer as well.
They fashioned your lips from a rose—
From a blood-red rose on a tomb —
Whose roots spread far where the dead things are,
And struggle with skulls for room !
They taught you to laugh, my sweet;
And they made your laugh in the sun
Where the sparkling dew and the rainbow meet,
Where the south wind wanders across the wheat,
Where the poppies blaze in the early heat,
Then wither one by one.
A 1 he laugh stirred wild desire,
Tb... burnt in men’s veins like fire !
For the sin to slip through your rose-red lips
And give them their hearts’ desire.
A whisp’ring voice cried, “ Come ! ”
And dancing eyes said, “ Here ! ”
Hot kisses rest on the dazzling breast;
Sweet eye-lids fall by fierce lips prest.
And now, in Hell, you laugh at the rest —
The end and completion of sin, Gwendoline 1

AN UNKNOWN QUANTITY.
I know her well enough to tell
Her sentiments concerning me;
Her tricks to test and all the rest
Of sobs and sighs and secresee,
But, when she comes to meet her chums,
I fail to gauge most dismallee
The true intent and sentiment
She nurtures towards another She.
O.P.
