A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, July 4, 1899.
My Dear Myee,—
Amy Castles is now in danger of being under-
rated. If she does not get away to Marehesi
quickly, there will be no voice left her on other
people’s tongues. Either a phenomenal voice, or
no voice at all; for nothing but voice makes
itself heard this week. How the tongues of
profesh warblers wag ! Every teacher in Sydney
is telling his pupils how he would have made Amy
Castles sing. Registers are fought over till you
might think the place was full of dogs. And two
tenors have nearly come to blows. One said that
he, for his part, didn’t believe there was any such
thing as a Register. The other took that as a
deadly insult (strange what things ,singers will—or
won’t—take as insults). He was doubtful whether
he would hit the infidel, or expound his own
theories. On reflection, decided the latter would
hurt most, and is still achieving, still pursuing.
Luckily, there is not much blood to run out of a
tenor. Even when he is big it is fat, not “ bleed,”
that abounds.
Her first concert came off on Wednesday.
Rumors of a terrible crush were not fulfilled, for
the audience of 3000 was not too large for the hall
that has seated 4000. tin perfect order were all
the concert arrangements. Lady Darley was led
in on Mr. Aengenheyster’s arm ; Lord Beauchamp
paused at the door till “God Save ” was started ;
so much society rolled up that the distinctive air
was lost in a multitude of airs. Also, the cream
of Sydney’s intelligent and musical people was
represented by that house.
What followed ? A warm reception, but no un-
controllable enthusiasm after her songs. A hearty
encore for each number, but not the slightest evi-
dence that the big, critical house was being carried
off its feet. After Amy Castles’ best bit of
vocalisation, “ The Wind in the Trees,” crowds of
people moved to go out, and the aisles were filling
rapidly when Amy appeared again with “ Home,
Sweet Home ”in her hands. The house had not
waited to see if an encore was forthcoming.
And do you know why I lay stress on trivial
things like these? Because Melbourne Age, still
working at the boom, in a wired notice of the con-
cert, brazenly remarked, “She at once captured the
audience, and was obliged to double and treble her
numbers, in response to encores that would not be
denied.”
The song-bird’s plumage was a skimpy white
frock, made of strips of lace and tucks, sleeveless
and low-necked. The hair was tied back with
white ribbon, and shoes and stockings were also
white. Everything was as simple as possible, and
she stood the fire of those 6000 eyes with a per-
fectly wonderful calm. She grasped her music
firmly, and the usual signs of palpitating sheets
and trembling lips and knees were not to be seen.
She says herself she is nervous. But she is not
nervous to the extent of being unable to conceal it.
Her smile was as free from wobble and as cool and
pretty as if she had been looking at no one but
herself in the glass. It flashed out brightly before
and after each song, and she gathered in her
bouquets and backed off the stage far more col-
lectedly than Albani, who always bumped into
something or other on her way out.
Have you noticed that Amy Castles is always
in the same frock? She wore it when I saw her
last week. She wears it in the Melbourne photos,
taken at the beginning of the boom. Wherever
she goes in Sydney she is always dressed in that
same navy coat and skirt. And only one unpre-
possessing little platform frock ! This suggests
that a tight, strange hand—possibly an uplifted
hand with down-bent fingers—is clutching the
girl’s money. _ A young thing visiting a strange
town-in the midst of such unique popularity might
surely have been allowed a few new chiffons.
Now, don’t say the . money is all to go on her
voice. Rev. Father Robinson allowed £6 15s. for
refreshments at two Melbourne concerts. You
may be sure they didn’t go on her voice.
It reminds some people of a once wildly-popular
actress. At the time when a certain theatre used
to be crammed to the doors with people to see the
star, she had scarcely sixpence to call her own,
and had her street frocks doled out with severe
injunctions to take care of them, for goodness
knew when she would get another.
When Amy’s concert was over, the out-pouring
house was shocked to hear a certain excitable con-
noisseur exclaiming loudly in the passages, “ They
ought to give me back my half-a-crown. I con-
sider I’ve been done.” What did he mean by
half-a-crown? I thought all the seats were five
shillings.
For support there were long programmes of
local musicians at both the concerts. Miss Kate
Rooney, whose mezzo-contralto has been reared in
a convent by Madame Christian, pleased the
second concert audience hugely. Vollinar, Stael,
de Beaupuis, de Alba, Wiegand, Henry Weir, and
the Pleyel Quintette all assisted. On Friday the
“ five, three and two house ” asserted itself in the
usual way of reduced prices, and filed out when the
artist-singer of the evening, Signor de Alba,
stepped forward to roll his big, rich voice round
something operatic.
“And the wickedness, I repeat, the great, the
ghastly wickedness of these women who go to balls in
ball-frocks, and allow the arm of man to encircle
their waists and lead them forth into the meshes of
the giddy raise. . . . Oh, my brethren, let us pray
for the abandoned souls of these , gay gyrators.”
Soothed-down extract from a Victorian parson’s
sermon. That’s the worst of being.a parson. You
know too much about wickedness.' Ordinary, dull
people see nothing in a waltz but a waltz.
I fear this sad, bad Gladdy must have been tak-
ing his ideas of frivolity from novels. For novels
lay to their books as consistently as Melbourne
papers over a certain boom. The beautiful
woman in pure white with the blue-black hair is
still the centre of an eager crowd of men begging
dances. She still bestows her waltzes with a chill
smile and floats away in the arms of one after the
other of her desperate partners. There is still the
Russian Prince sueing for a dance with barely-
veiled admiration in his eye. Still do the dozens
of finely-chiselled young Guardsmen waylay ber
at every whirl. Still does she see across the crowd
a pair of deep, inscrutable eyes that come nearer
and are introduced, and bear her away in a long,
dreamy waltz, and her heart still whispers, “ ’Tis
my fate I have met to-night. ”
But step out of books, and where is she ? No-
where. So subtle are the causes that make the
world go round that ’tis now “provincial” to
dance often at a ball. Only a debutante wants to
fill her programme. Society takes three names on
her card, revolves twice, and stands about lan-
guidly conversating for the rest of the evening.
Even Sydney has arrived that far. His Ex. dances
once; other people twice ; or three remnants.
After that, the very, very suburbs, whose Good-
ness nobody could question.
The tale of a ball - room : Everything was
settled. Who should take in whom was
definitely fixed. When—the lady who was to go
in on his Ex’s arm sent word that she was ill,
but her young, pretty, unmarried daughter would
take her place ! This was the day before the ball.
Wild consternation in the committee ! Imagine
the dang::- of sending His Ex. in with another
woman’s young, pretty, unmarried daughter ! That
committee nearly went further out of its mind
than any other committee on earth. And no
wonder. Then two ladies recovered themselves.
One said, “/ cannot go in after a Miss. I shall
enter before the procession, and receive His Ex.
on the dais by right of my title.” Which she did.
But the other lady put the case in black and white.
“ His Excellency does not go in with Miss Dash !
I come after Mrs. Dash. It’s either Mrs. Dash or
I that goes in on the Governor’s arm.” She
fought it to the death ; fought hard and fiercely,
as only a committee-woman can fight. The re-
sult ? Oh, Mrs. Dash was not as ill as she thought.
Sometimes, Myee, I wonder is there anything
left that is new to say about a ball ? At the begin-
ning of the season there is a certain novelty to be
found in the first brew of new ball-dresses. But
after sampling a few dances, what a palling family
likeness between them all begins to obtrude !
True, it may be a big ball; or it may be a small
ball. There may be many beautiful dresses; or
there may be a noticeable few. But, having re-
marked on these points, what is left, from the
point of view of the Pen ? For who that was not
there, cares to hear about the decorations ? And
who that was there noticed them? And who
wants to be reminded of a supper since consumed ?
And who that was not there to look upon that
supper in the flesh can bear to read of it ? Nothing
then remains to your Goulish letter-writer but the
chronicle of who wore what; the general all-em-
bracing epitliets ; and a painful attempt to give a
picture of what one is really trying to conceal, the
life in the room, the movements of the heroines
and heroes as they meet and part. And
they may not even be heroes and heroines.
They don’t like it. You must know nothing of
their strategies, plans, conversation. They are
lay figures wearing frocks. Pull the string and
you get: “I wore pink satin and a diamond
tiara.” “I was in amber brocade with malmaisons
in my hair.” “My daughter was much admired
in blue chiffon flounces and turquoise necklets.”
“Dear Carrie Otherside looked such a sight in
a green polonaise and pink bouquet.’’ And if
there is more animation in that last sentiment,
who can wonder? Spite has its own mercury.
And yet, let us not be spiteful for the love of
Heaven and the sake of Myee reading at the
other end.
Otherwise, the University Ball was a beautiful,
brilliant, highly-successful dance, crowded with
pretty young girls and society women in lovely
frocks. Quite one of the dances of the season.
Lord Beauchamp and Lady Mary shone on one
corner of the room for several hours. His Ex.
was talked to in the language of the learned for,
strangely enough, reverend prof’s and doctors and
learned ladies never seen before at a ’Varsity ball
came to this one. What was the attraction? Oh !
I forget.
Lady Mary substituted big pearls for the dia-
mond necklet, and wore oyster brocade, with
touches of rose and bands of fur on the bodice.
His Ex.’s big star glittered as he talked through
somebody’s ear-trumpet. Many people Would
have liked those clear tones to ring through their
ear-trumpets or drums, but a band of elderly men
had a great deal to say and look to His Ex. that
night, and they talked to him of the orations of
Demosthenes, and he looked Greek at them, and
lonely women looked plain English at them all.
Mayoress Harris, in a long train, was accompanied
by her pretty, lively daughter, in rose-embroidered
chiffon over silk, and a waistband of violets. Mrs.
Edmund Barton also shed violets on her gold
brocade. Among the ’Varsity girls who removed
the slur of dowdiness from their species I noticed
Miss Caro in a frilly black-spotted white muslin,
with violet velvet swathing on the bodice, and a
simulated polonaise; Miss Ethel Maynard in
pink silk with deep pink roses on the corsage ;
Miss Adams in a jewelled guipure polonaise over
billows of little blue chiffon flounces ; Miss Russell
(whose engagement to Mr. Registrar Barff is just
announced) in rose-red silk with a white lace fichu;
Miss Louisa M‘Donald in black satin and intel-
lect ; and many others. Two pretty rosebud
sisters in pink muslin and white satin rejoiced the
eye. Madame Buhrow was elegantly frocked in a
black satin “empire.” Altogether, it was far and
away the most successful ’Varsity dance to date.
Apropos of the ’Varsity, “ Jem” Pickburn, son
of the well-known Dr. Pickburn, was married last
week to Miss Birdie Prosser. The quiet wedding
came off at Chatswood, where they intend to
reside, the bride wearing simple white brocade
with a trail of orange blossoms from waist to hem,
and a Limerick veil.
Another of last week’s weddings was that of
M iss Phoebe Chisholm, of the pretty soprano
\oice, with Mr. Norman Alston, a lately-arrived
baritone.
Speaking of marriages, people are wondering
why one countryman with dark cheeks (living in
Sydney at present) should own twenty-six wives
to his partner’s three. A few women still find the
old problem big enough—why some men have no
wives at all. But the Indian merchants are much
discussed.
The matinee of “Dandy Dick ” filled the Royal
with a chiefly feminine house. Holiday school-
girls swarmed the gods, and the play went mer-
rily. Long ago, Mrs. Brough made a hole in
people’s hearts with her sporting aunt. She wt . .rs
the same clothes mostly, and the golden-brown
cloth frock has turned old - fashioned at the
placket, and been let down with a frill in front.
She looks as fetching as ever in her hard-
hitter and shoulder-glasses. Winifred Austin
played off a short white muslin frock and brown
shoes and stockings. But why does she look at
the audience so often ? The audience is frequently
looking at her. And Emma Temple ? I always
think of Emma Temple as a very, very good
woman. She plays all the parts without savor or
color. Plays peaceably parts so utterly untinted
that she has to wear a red wig to lend them a little
distinctive hue of their own.
The red wig appeals again in “One Summer’s
Day,” that sweet, little, spineless play now run-
ning smoothly at the Royal. Saturday night’s
crowded house wiped its sympathetic eye after a
few of the moist places, and if the idyl offers a
variation on the usual Brough sparkle, the
change is not aggressive. Mrs. Brough makes
up dark, wears earrings and represents a
gypsy. She looks very handsome in her
swarthy skin. But Mr. Majeroni, billed as
gypsy Seth, is a Murrumbidgee boundary-
rider from his hat to his accent. Grace Noble
shows what can be done with a well-starched blouse
and skirt. A red waist-belt and tie, a high white
collar, and little red-banded sailor-hat tone-up her
smartly-cut pique skirt and linen blouse till they
become things of beauty. Women with next
season’s wardrobe hanging over them took a keen
interest in that simple, snappy summer costume.
Emma Temple plays an ingenue part, originally
read as a girl of fifteen. A beautiful, hot, all-day,
headache picnic surrounds two acts. The second
act gives a lifelike study of things that always
happen after lunch at every well-conducted picnic.
Miss Bessie Thompson reading uncomfortably in
a boat; Mr. Dartrey (made up like this-city’s most
haunting bore) sleeping the sleep of an unhappy
picnicker on bits of stick and earwigs ; all the
wrong people going off together in couples, and
the Headache looming large in the background.
Exceedingly picturesque scenery has been painted
for this picnic on the Thames. The only flaw in
. the charmful picture is that there isn’t a billy,
and nobody even mentions the word tea.
Miss Inez Bensusan is giving dramatic recitals

Mr. Yatman— not Fatman—the Yankee evan-
gelist nowin Sydney, knows howto rake in the
crowds. Fully 4000 found seating-room in the
Town Hall on Sunday night, and many had to be
turned away. Mr. Yatman has a platform manner
• that would do credit to the Shah of all tlie Persias.
When he raises his right arm and says “ Let there
be silence, ” one is irresistibly reminded of the Ist
chapter of Genesis, for immediately there is silence.
Mr. Yatman’s favorite anecdote concerns a giant
oak that stood in the eastern part of the U.S.
People used to say it was worth a fortune to see it
standing there in all its strength and beauty. One
day a cyclone came along and tore it up by the
roots. Mr. Yatman, a boy of 12, sat on a fence
and cried like a child at the sight. The oak was
found to be “full'of worms.” The application of
the story was that probably most of the gentle
shepherd’s auditors were rotten at the core.

“Since Mi is McGeorge, an Adelaide girl,
settled in Melbourne, art-needlework has been
booming in circles where good taste attends
leisure. The result was seen at a recent show,
Lady Brassey, who is clever with her needle,
turned up and critically inspected the exhibits,
some of which were beautiful specimens of work,
Fine art, in golden and silken threads of many
colors, has chased the dear old antimacassar out of
the civilised world. Strange that some people do
not learn tapestry-weaving. It is troublesome to
acquire, but worth the pains ; and so many clever
women are seeking profitable avenues of einploy-
ment which are not overcrowded, that this work
might repay study. Witness the success of the
Morris tapestries in England.

joicing over the arrival of her first-born. So is
Mrs. Vallange (nee Madden). Both the little
strangers are girls.
“Fate never takes its eye off some people. An
up-country doctor has just lost his wife, a lady
not far on in her thirties. Before, she married
Medicus she was the wife of a Vic. bank manager,
who left her for dead, murdered his children, and
killed himself,
“Puff paragraphs about the enterprise of the
Argus in selling the ‘ Encyclopaedia Britannica ’
for the price at which it has been obtainable by
the intelligent purchaser any time within the last
five years, are written in the office, and insertion
is procured in the minor journals. But the show
was given away badly when the pars, got mixed,
and that intended for one paper got into another.
Thus, there are in Melbourne a little tea-and-
muffin social print called the Sun, and a Wes-
leyan-Methodist paper called the Spectator.
The other day the Sun contains a puff about the
Argus enterprise, including this sentence : ‘The
Spectator, with all its heart, wishes good speed
to this magnificent project. ’ The office-boy should
have remarks made to him for leaving the par. at
the wrong office

brother.

ERRORS IN FOOD AND DRINK.
By a Specialist.
It is dawning on the public to try and prevent,
or at least to arrest, disease. It is in prevention
that Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa plays an important
part, acting .solely as a first-class nourishing food
—it strengthens the system to resist, oppose, and
overcome the attacks of disease. You’ll hear
someone say, “So-and-so has a strong constitu-
tion ” ; follow that up, and you’ll find that So-and-
so follows the golden rule of being temperate in all
things, and pays attention to diet and exercise.
Does he or she keep up this strong constitution by
taking medicine and swallowing pills? No in-
deed ! They have discovered that prevention is
better than cure. Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa places a
means in the hands of everyone to build-up and
maintain a sound constitution, which enables its
possessor to travel his life’s journey without the
aches and pains which are in many cases pre-
ventive. Thus we come round to sound common-
sense based on experience.
Dr. Tibbies’ Vi-Cocoa can be obtained from all
chemists, grocers and stores, or from Dr. Tibbies’
Vi-Cocoa, Limited, 269 George-street, Sydney.

The Australian Climate.
Many seem to have an idea that the Australian
climate is more trying than many others. In
reality there are less lung troubles, consumption,
bronchitis, and chronic diseases, and less epi-
demics than in any other known inhabited
country. But we Australians do have to guard
against liver disease, and thousands do that by a
timely use of Warner’s Safe Cure. It seldom
fails to quickly remove all symptoms of bilious-
ness, nausea, giddiness, indigestion, and head-
aches. Mr. R. Moss, of the well-known firm of
Henry Moss Lighting Co., 6 Ferrars Place, Albert
Park, Melbourne, writes : “ I have used Warner’s
Safe Cure for liver and other affections, and found
it very beneficial. It is, without doubt, a first-
class medicine.”*
Mr. P. K. M’Caughan, the genial, gorgeous
Irishman who had that celebrated legal bout with
the Argus last year, is “putting-up” for Mel-
bourne City Council, and hopes to be duly elected
to-morrow (Friday). The advt. of Patrick the
imposing, which appears in this issue, sets forth
his claims to be returned by a majority as large
as himself. His strongest claim lies in the un-
deniable fact that lie knows his Melbourne well
and can find plenty of time to devote to municipal
affairs. M’Caughan has long been a popular
adornment of the Melbourne Block, and Thk
Bulletin can see no cause or reason why he
shouldn’t take up a commanding position among
the City Fathers and do his duty by the rate-
payers in a most satisfactory and picturesque
manner.
PERSONAL ITEMS.
If he cares to take the post, W. J. Lyne
will probably be the next N.S.W. Speaker.
It used to be remarked how very much
alike were Michie, Mill, and Cardinal Manning-
three M’s.
Gov. Beauchamp is billed by Pall Mall
Magazine to write an article on Madresfield
Court, his home at Malvern Link.
John Downer is now the “ father” of S.A.
Assembly, lie has seen continued service in
seven Parliaments although but 55 years old.
Obit the energetic Michael Landy, father
of liop-growing in Gippsland, chief founder of
Vic. beet industry, and director of Maffra Beet
Company. >
Men live long in Warrnambool, Vic.
James Walsh has just left Spudopolis for a
brighter sphere, aged 101. Ho leaves a sister 98,
not out, whose husband lately died at 100.
The explanation of the extraordinary Melb.
MTntyre-trial postponements is not so much the
fact of the defendant being a lawyer as the son of
a Conservative leader and a big gun in sassiety
himself.
The death of E. Webb, M.L.C., breaks
the cordon of ancient names on which Bathurst
prides itself. The official Stewarts, the squatting
Rutherfords, the Lees, and the Webbs have been
Bathurst’s glory.
It is urged zealously in certain circles that
the interval between the sending of the Federal
Bill to England and its passing should be spent in
organising subscriptions over all Australia to
raise a statue to Henry Parkes.
George Brookman, who has endowed S.A.
School of Mines with a eift of <£lo,ooo, is credited
with having made <£250,000 out of the late advance
in Westralian gold-stocks. Brookman was a part-
ner in a grocery business in Adelaide when he and
his brother pioneered the Coolgardie syndicate
which eventually floated the Great Boulder and
Associated Mines.
AT AN AMY CASTLES CONCERT.
Howard Taylor, the Westralian goldfields’
first Parliamentary representative, and later a
silent Federal Conventioner, has set up in England
in great stylo. Ho has bought a racing-yacht from
an Italian duke, and flies at the highest stakes.
Taylor was an early and heavy plunger in Wesb-
ralian stocks. His racing-colors are a black swan
on a yellow ground—appropriate, anyhow.
One delightful phase of the metamorphosis
by which a Labor editor becomes an M.L.A.,
appears in the comments of the Labor journal on
its previous Boss. Brisbane Worker on Higgs
and Towers Eagle on Lesina are indeed delight-
ful reading, and give rise to the suspicion that
somebody in the present editorial sanctum is
wiping out ancient grievances with a great and
holy joy.
Mayor Dickson, of Cootamundra, N.S.W.,
has had, while Mayor, twins born to him on two
occasions. As the municipal habit is to present a
silver cradle to the Mayor endowed with twins,
there is talk of a golden cradle.
Dugald Thomson is the only member of
N.S.W. Assembly who turns over the whole of
his Parliamentary salary to the charities of his
electorate. No member of the House now declines
to take his <£2s-per-month cheque.
W. E. Johnston, lately appointed acting
County Court Judge (Melb.), is connected by
marriage with Donald Wallace. And Donald was
connected with Carbine —so Johnston is connected
with Carbine. No wonder he was appointed 1
The recent unveiling of the monument to
ex-Premier John Robertson at Watson’s Bay
brought together a group of politicians and ex-
politicians, most of whom are deader than the
eight-years-dead man whose memory they com-
memorated.
Bishop Higgins’ promotion from Auxiliary
Bishop of Sydney to Bishop of Rockhampton
gratifies those who, during his 10 years in Sydney,
always claimed to know that he was obliged to
work with tied hands. Bishop Higgins is by
temperament an initiator.
If Chief Justice Griffith, of Queensland,
be not remembered when the law prize of the
Federation is conferred, opinion, based on flying
words, has it that, like his predecessor, Lilley, he
will quit the Bench and enter Federal politics.
His age, 54, is in his favor.
Major Castine—the new S.A. Opposition-
whip—holds the S.A. jawbone record; spoke all
night once. Although Castine is personally very
small potatoes, a U.S. town is named after his
grandfather. Like Neild, the N.S.W. chin-wagger,
Castine is the ferocious commander of a tin-soldier
corps.
Bishop Stone-Wigg, who went aproned to
Papua a short time back, reports himself carrying
the white man’s burden. He has had sunstroke,
neuritis, bunged eye (not open for five weeks),
and ulcer on left leg. Before going north, the
Bishop was a fine, uj)standing athletic and sound-
bodied man.

on 2nd, Dean Cowper, of Sydney, 80 on 3rd,

(S.A.) 55 on 6th, Mrs. Humphrey Ward 48 on

Gov. Beauchamp is the 20th Governor to
rule over N.S.W. He may also be the last of his
class, State Governors under Federation being
likely to be reoruited from local residents. Some
of the Lieutenant-Govs. —as, for instance, Capto.
Grose —held office as long as the average Gov. and
in most cases did the work better.
The veteran Archibald Michie might have
been living still, and have lived to complete his
century, had he not persistently refused to take
the pension granted him by a grateful Govt,
many years ago. The Vic. Treasury, saved
.£40,000 or more by Michie’s devotion to principle.
For a man to refuse a peuaion is to deliberately
knock years off his life.
Dean Selwyn, of Newcastle, N.S.W., dead
at the age of 70, was one of the very few instances
of squatter turned spiritual shppherd. His first
12 years in Australia ho devoted to sheep and
cattle raising, and was fairly successful at the
business ; but the roligious strain in the family
asserted itself, and he souarht ordination at the
hands of Bishop Tyrrell. He was related to the
great Sehvyns.
Of the older school there was hardly a
better-known Australian mining man than George
Armstrong, whose death by accident has been re-
ported from the West. He left Victoria after
years of Bendigo experience to go to the Northern
Territory for the Millars, who, after the Pine
Creek railway construction, had a great idea of
the mines of the inlands. He got back (the re-
sults up there had been disappointing) in time to
take a hand in the Westralian spurt.
Another of the order of Rougemont is
threatened. M. P. Cosgrove, a much-travelled
Sydneyite, who has prospected in most places —he
was early at the Broken Hill, Coolgardie, Papua,
and the Klondyke rushes, and has worked also in
the Kimberley country—has been interviewed by
Reuter’s in London ; and as the outcome of his
sensational tales, publishers have, it is alleged,
trooped in upon him. Cosgrove was one of the
earliest of Rougemont’s attackers.
It is a coincidence that the two promoters
of the rival Broken Hill private-railway Bills,
though comparatively young m»n, died within a
few months of one another. W. P. MacGregor
sat for Wentworth for the few months that
sufficed to put the Silverton Co.’s Bill through ;
G. M‘L. Matheson got a seat for Glen Innes.
But instead of getting his Pinnacles-route Bill
through, Matheson only pulled poor old John
M'Elhone’s nose. And John is dead, too.
J. P. Abbott, who, it is understood, will
retire from the Speakership when N.S.W. Parlia-
ment reassembles, will then have held the chair
longer than any previous N.S.W. Speaker, except
W. M. Arnold, who assumed office in 1865 and
was drowned in ’75. The other Speakers averaged
about three years each, Murray holding office two
years and Wigram Allen seven. Altogether
N.S.W. has had eight Speakers (including Abbott),
of whom four are alive, Cooper, the first, heading
the list.
In the course of legal proceedings at Mel-
bourne between one John Slattery and his uncle,
ex-priest Slattery, the anti-Catholic spouter, it
transpired that the latter’s “ lectures ” had in
three weeks raked in =BBOO. And was it likely,
asked the ex-priest’s counsel, that his client was
intending clandestinely “to leave such happy
hunting-grounds?” Not much! The two most
paying “ lines ” in Australia appear to be those of
.£ LOO-poll-tax-protected-Chow and “ converted
priest.”
Francis Kenna—an Australian native—has
inauspiciously begun his reign as editor of Queens-
land Worker by an ill-advised declaration of
anti-Federal sentiment, and by allying himself
with Unmack, Barlow, Nelson, and MThvraith.
This is the first time that the Labor-paper has run
in harness with that high-priest of Boodlewraith,
Brisbane Telegraph. It is agonising to sincere
Laborists to see their organ backing up Australia’s
wickedest and most selfish clique of political re-
actionaries.
The Maoriland petition to remove Judge
Edwards from the bench, on the ground of bias
in the Jellieoe case, repeats in some of its phrases
the similar petition got up against Judge Willis,
resident judge of Port Plnllip, in 1842. Bias was
one charge, lack of dignity another, and not
having the confidence of the public a third. The
Superintendent of Port Phillip referred the case
to the authorities at Sydney, and Judge Willis
was removed from what was then called the
Colonial Bench.
Died at Bendigo, lately, “ Jimmy ”
M'Craw, one of the earliest, and for a great while
one of the most successful, diggers at Golden and
Pegleg Gullies, and afterwards equally early and
successful at Castlemaine. Then his luck changed,
while Lansell’s (they had been working close to-
gether) kept on. Both were plucky prospectors ;
but, whereas 25 years ago Lansell owned deep
mines and batteries, Jimmy was glad to take a
billet in one of the latter. And Lansell saw to it
that it lasted all his days.
The editor of Brisbane Courier was forced
the other day, in the interests of his literary and
composing staff, to take a rather unusual step.
He wrote to one of the leading champions of pro-
vincialism, who has been swamping the Press with
verbose and undescipherable letters on Federation,
telling him that his contributions were inserted
wholly on account of the writer’s official connec-
tion with the Anti-Bill Loague, and not by reason
of any merit in the letters themselves; and sug-
gesting that the anti-federalist should get his copy
transcribed by some legible person conversant
with English spelling and grammar. The official
is now resting.
Vic. M.L. A. Hume Cook is a 28-year-old,
entered Parliament at 23, and has been pining
for a portfolio ever since. His recent observations
about discontent “ amongst the young members of
the ’ouse” are nothing new. He has been spas-
modically arching his back at Turner for the last
two years, and, though a seat on a royal commis-
sion would probably mollify him, “ plain George”
apparently doesn’t think it worth while. In
person, Code is small and cadaverous, but he has
a muscular memory, and is a mathematical gym-
nast of some skill. When the ’ouse is not sitting,
Cook drives around collecting rents and pondering
the grievances of the numerous tram-employes
resident in his constituency. His seat is sure.
Even in democratic Maoriland dead lions
are at a discount. The Liberals cheerfully
stumped up £lOOO to give brawny Jock M'Kenzie,
Lands Minister, a trip to his Highland hills, but
they have not subscribed .£5O towards a memorial
to the late Hori Grey. At Auckland, where Grey
was idolised during life, the Liberal Association
voted £2 towards his statue, and a few weeks
later gave <£4o to the M'Kenzie testimonial. A
Grey memorial list, after lying for a month in a
public office at Wellington, bore but one name—
that of a little girl, who offered one penny. A
pathetic reminder of Grey’s popularity with
children, and of the ingratitude—or let us call it
the utilitarianism—of the full-grown Democrat!
A NATURAL QUESTION.
HE: “Do you know, I came across a medical hook
giving rules for prolonging life. I’m trying
them. ”
SHE: “ Yes, dear, how interesting! And do you
find that you live any longer than you used
to do? ”
Journalist v. journalist seems to be a
growing fashion in N.S.W. libel eases, both
criminal and civil. In the early days it was
Governors or other high personages who assailed
the journalist with court proceedings. Journalists
then fought one another with pens. The first
important invocation of law by journalist against
journalist was when Stevens and Stokes of S.M.
Herald sued Stephen and Nichols of the
Australian.
Isabella Cockburn, mother of the S.A.
Agent-General, who has just died in Adelaide at
the age of 80, was a woman of real liberality of
view. Although her only daughter married a
pastoralist who is a bitter antagonist of Kingston
and all his works, the old lady always sided with
her Radical and brilliant son in political contro-
versies. Which fact was a great stay and comfort
to him in face of the open and not always very
fair opposition of brother-in-law Giles.
ANS. Wales ex-M.L.A.’s application for
a bankruptcy discharge was postponed the other
day as an unpaid account of £8 10s. owing to the
Govt. Printer stared the Registrar in the face.
But next day the Govt. Printer withdrew the
item, and the discharge was issued. Before the
present Govt. Printer took office it was well
understood that whatever accounts an M.L.A.,
ran up with the Govt. Printer perished when the
M.L.A. lost his seat. In fact, while he retained
his seat he was never pressed, and thus the mem-
ber who got his little speech reset or bound, or
otherwise used the office, escaped payment, what-
ever happened. In Potter and Richards’ days
hundreds of pounds worth of work was done in
the Govt. Printing office yearly, to gratify mem-
bers’ vanity, and for 15 or 20 years not one mem-
ber paid the account tendered to him.

About M‘Gregor’s courtship. At a ball,
up North, bashful M'Gregor fell in love with the
belle. He took her for a ride next day. For the
first six miles he never spoke a word ; then he
asked her if she had ever seen an owl. She looked
at him. “ Yes,” she said, and laughed heartily.
M'Gregor joined in the laugh and thought he was
making wonderful headway.

Apropos Steele Rudd’s “Dad.” T’other
day a lean, heavy, Gippsland selector called at
office of a bung Melb. building society to cash
five cheques of <£lo each, representing interest on
deposits dated ’B5 to ’B9, cheques drawn on since-
defunct City of Melb. Bank. The poor devil hadn’t
been in town since he got the first in ’B5 ; “didn’t
know the society was winding up, but thought it
was time he got some more cheques.”

“ Yarrum ” tells what a South Coast
(N.S.W.) farm-hand does for 6s. a week :
He “turns out” at 5 a.m., makes fire, gropes around
gullies after cows, which are yarded at six. Then milks
for two hours, and has hardly sat down to breakfast be-
fore he is hurried away to separate milk. Separates, feeds
pigs and poddies, catches and harnesses moke, and away
with milk to catch train. Back with “empties” at 11,
washes cans, and off to dig spuds, after which yokes up
bullocks, and fetches load or two of wood from ranges.
After dinner, cuts couple of waggon-loads of greenstuff,
and distributes it about paddocks for cows ; ploughs a
bit, and rides two miles to P.O. Then again after cows,
which are “in” at five. Sweeps milking-shed, milks till
7, and separates till 8. Then cleans separator—an hour’s
graft ; and feeds poddies and pigs once more. Then sup-
per, and after helps make tallow-candles until 10.30, and
attends “ family prayers ” till 11. Then to bunk, from
which he is called in his shirt, on average three times
nightly, to “ sool ” dogs on bull in sorghum-crop, or chase
native-cats from hen-roost. “ Between time 9 ” he is ex-
pected to “set” hens, and run down bandicoots and
hares for dog’s feed. What sort of a boss should he make ?
* * * *
From the Far North :
Besides Chows, Japs and Kanakas, Cairns(N.Q.l is now
over-run with dirty, turban-roofed Hindoos. They mostly
take stumping and chipping contracts from- alleged white
sugarrrs, and are bossed by one of their own lot, who
makes the bulk of the money. Periodically, numbers of
them stink out the local P. 0., where they go to send
money to India. (The official returns of these remittances
should be interesting.) Said that one Hindoo has £4OO in
the Q.N.8., made by gang-bossing. Intends shortly to go
to India for fresh contingent of nigs., who will work for
almost nix. At present, tucker, &0., costs him the
fabulous sum of 4Jd. per nig. per diem. In India, men
will walk 10 miles to work 16 hours for 4d., and then 10
miles back. And yet whites are asked to compete with
such ! May Heaven jump on the Dickson Govt. I —6Y.H.

* * * *
N.S.W. school-inspector’s report on the
aboriginal school at Brungle recommends that the
full public-school course of studies should be fol-
lowed in future. His recommendation is based
on the high merit of the children’s attainments in
their present limited list of subjects. The report
is valuable, especially if compared with the reports
of 20 or 30 years ago, when it was the fashion to
speak of aboriginals as incapable of substantial
brain-development. Govt, then only hoped to im-
part a few of !the barest rudiments, and scholastic
authorities gave small encouragement to that
hope.
• * * *
“ A. A.” : As soon as old and deaf German
Anton started to fill the seed-peas into the drills
prepared for them, Shandy, the pet emu, keeping
a few yards behind, faithfully followed along the
drills swallowing every blessed pea as he went.
Three hours from the start Anton finished, and
turned round in time to observe the last few peas
being picked up. Not a pea was left in the whole
40 drills. The old ’un swore loud and long
in English and German, got the gun out, and
blew the head off the pea-swallower. Then, after
much deliberation, he buried the remains in the
centre of the plot ; and although they were sown
somewhat broadcast, every one of those peas came
up, and we had by far the best crop in the valley.

“ C.F.” : Re snakes’ swallowing capacity
(B. 17/6/’99). Anyone knowing construction of
snakes’ jaw will easily swallow the Clonourry pill.
Know myself of 24ft. python taking in big sow
(containing 13 young). Once killed a 4ft. python
with a dozen duck’s eggs in him. Not very un-
common to find in Borneo jungle skeletons of large
python and of big sambur stag side by side ; the
snake having crushed deer to death and been
choked in endeavoring to swallow the antlers.
Snakes invariably swallow their prey head-first,
and in these cases the “ tines,” acting as barbs,
prevent either further swallowing or rejection.

Dear Bulleiin. —White magpie, I ob-
serve, is “ off but, re albino animals in general;
at a Buddhist temple near Kobe (Japan) is a fine
collection of these, including, when I saw it, deer
and monkeys. Although albinos have, generally,
red eyes, pure white cats often have one brown
and one blue, and such cats, strange to say, are
invariably deaf. Have seen albino Chinaman and
Filipino of Palawan. The white elephant of
Siam is not albino, color being due to skin disease,
as is also the mottling of the “ marbled ” negro.
-C.F.

“Beth” : Re “ Werrimee ” (24/6/’99) on
the social status of the police. A policeman is no
more a “ toff ” out-back ” than “inside,” and he
doesn’t mix “ only with squatters and sich.” He
mixes with everybody, as everybody else does—if
the ordinary interchange of civilities by people
who know each other can be called “ mixing.”
And, out-back, everyone does know the other ;
likewise his horse and his ox and his maid-ser-
vant and the prospects of an increase in the
family—the writer knows everyone within 100
miles of him and most within 150. Wherever one
finds rabbits and saltbush, he can rely on hospi-
tality, whatever his status. That’s all. When
the out-back squatter is more than the mere repre-
sentative of a company—when he is a squatter—he
is in nine cases out of ten a liberal, genial host
whose mind has expanded with his acres ; and,
as a rule, he is intellectually and every other way
superior to the long-faced coastal cookie, who
fancies his farm the universe, himself God. and the
“bobby” but a worm of the earth. Further,
with all deference to “ Werrimee,” the law out-
back is no more one-sided than anywhere else.
There is generally a P.M., and when there isn’t
the friendship of squatter with police would
scarcely breed bias in civil cases ; and it is only in
civil cases that the squatters’ interests are likely
to be involved. Even then his adjudication in
any shape is not an indispensable condition.
Drought has reduced the muster of cattle
on one Cunnamulla (S.W. Queensland) run from
6000 to 200. And south, over the border, a
Walgett station has losb 36,000 out of 47,000 sheep.
Yet, further west, as at Cobham, the season is
particularly good—partly because of patchy rains,
partly because for some years past the country
hasn't been overstocked.
* * * *
Hard times have been responsible for
numerous kangarooing parties in the most acces-
sible districts of Westralia, and the slaughter has
been great. One S.W. trader alone has taken in
3500 skins this season. Native-dogs, following
the hunters for the carcasses, have become a pest
to the sheep-men, who are appealing to the police
for protection.
* * * *
A Maoriland doctor claims to have dis-
covered that early rising leads to kleptomania and
insanity. Present writer fully believes that, so
far as the kleptomania is concerned. The earliest
rising he ever did was 18 years ago, when he got
up at 2 a.m. on a winter’s morning in a miserable
hotel among the Gippsland mountains, to leave in
a coach which started through a snowstorm at
three. That* morning he took an overcoat that
didn’t belong to him (he had one, but he put them
both on), also a pair of gloves that seemed more
woolly than his own, and somebody’s flask. And
before he had been two hours on the roof of the
vehicle he could have passed as a very respectable
lunatic. Early rising leads to stealing clothes,
right enough.
* * * *
“ Heimsdal” : Snake-pets are not so rare
as seems to be thought. A Kilmore (Vic.) resi-
dent used to have a tame “ carpet,” which was
allowed to roam about all day, but was usually
shut in at night. It used to turn up at regular
hour for allowance of milk. Kept the place clear
of rats and mice, and was a great favorite with
family. One night was forgotten, and got on bed
in room set apart for guest, who, on entering late
at night, took a gun and ignorantly shot the pet.
A Mudgee resident frequently carries snakes about
with him—poisonous ones at that —and the reptiles
seem to know him.
“ J.R.” : Re sheep taking to water. When
the Cockburn river, after long drought, began to
flow again in something like a stream, I saw some
sheep walk deliberately in and paddle about
for 10 minutes or so in depths of from up to
bellies to nearly swimming. This, which
I never otherwise saw happen, was about
a dozen miles from Tam worth, at a
place where boundary-riders have to
swear themselves hoarse trying to get
jumbucks across when the stream is
only ankle-deep.

“Kooka”: Re frog-cannibalism. The large
green frog will swallow his smaller brother every
time. The little chap swells, kicks, and croaks;
but he goes down all the same. After dusk in the
bush is often heard the protesting croak of the
victim. Dogs won’t meddle with frogs, but I have
known cats take to eating them, especially the
hind-legs. The big green frog is most useful
about a nursery, as it eats an enormous number of
moths and other nocturnal insect-pests. Have
known these frogs to be exported to the “ Old
Country ” for this purpose. Take small flower-
pot, half-fill with earth ; put in frog ; fill up with
earth ; turn pot upside-down on bottom of case,
and there is your frog good for three months, and
how much longer I can’t say. Best place to look
for this kind of frog is at banana-leaf stalk.
* * * *
“Yang Anyut”: Gippsland leeches (“U.,”
B. 17/6/’99) must be more amenable to reason than
those of Borneo. There, it’s almost impossible to
pull them off, and, in any case, ulcers are the re-
sult of forcible removal. A touch of tobacco or
salt, however, soon shifts ’em. Have wakened up
with a couple hanging to inside of my cheek, and
know of their getting into even less accessible
places, from which dislodgment was only effected
by a surgeon! Socks, “pullys” and gaiters, in
conjunction, even fail to keep ’em off one s feet
during Borneo’s rainy seasons.


One of the most wonderful inventions of
the age is Willson’s discovery of crystalline carbide
of calcium. There is little doubt that acetylene
gas is about to revolutionise artificial lighting.
As with all successful patents, a host of pirates
have started up to try and rob Willson of his
rights. However, the recent decision of the High
Courts at Leipsic, Germany, will somewhat damp
the ardour of Willson’s opponents. It has been
decided that Willson is undoubtedly the discoverer
of crystalline carbide of calcium. Following up
this success, the English Acetylene Company have
issued a batch of writs against infringers, and the
Acetylene Gas Co. of Australasia Ltd., of 36 Pitt-
street, Sydney—who own the Willson patents for
Australasia—are getting ready to give the Aus-
tralian pirates a surprise. Thisco. have now fitted
up some hundreds of installations around Sydney,
and are prepared to give estimates for any kind of
acetylene installations. Write direct to 36 Pitt-
street, and beware of infringers.*

In this issue is the portrait of Bernard
Espinasse, who so recently and effectively drama-
tised “ The Three Musketeers ” for Mr. Geo.
Rignold, and who commenced dramatic author-
ship, as a small boy, with a play in 15 acts and
67 scenes ! He has since been very successful
with pieces of less portentous dimensions, and,
though actually English-born, may fairly be con-
sidered Australia’s best dramatist. Mr. Espinasse,
who has also had a turn at journalism, has in all
about two dozen pieces to his credit, and has at
present two plays under consideration by London
managers.

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ingextending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. 11. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian, gems.*
An English paper publishes a vague and
feeble fiction —extensively reprinted in Australia—
to the effect that a nervous bride lately got- mar-
ried to the groom’s best man by mutual mistake at
the altar. The peculiar inanity of this yarn lies
in the fact that marriages are not completed at
the altar, but in the vestry, where the register is
signed. Even if the. parson were allowed to hitch
the wrong “boy” to the right girl, the other
fellow would be bound to recover his self-posses-
sion when the parties adjourned to the little room
at the back. Moreover, if the parties were not
properly sorted out in the vestry the best man’s
signature in the fatal book wouldn’t correspond
with the husband’s name as entered from the
special license or the marriage banns.

Warren (N.S.W.) Laud Board faces a
unique phase of the unprovided-for. One Mait-
leni, of Grulargumbone, drew a lot at a land ballot.
The law provides that the result of the ballot
must be confirmed ere the drawer takes legal pos-
session. In this case, when the day to confirm
arrived, it was found that Maitlem had died.
But, before dying, Maitlem had devised his
estate, leaving all to relations. Now the relations
claim to go on the land, but the drawer of the
ballot paper next to Maitlem’s insists that the
land should fall to him. And the Board has no
precedent.

In this olf-season of the year the current
wage in some of the best S. Australian farm dis-
tricts is now 3s. fid. per day of about 10 hours. In
the fruitful Stratlialbyn district the best offer any
of a party of young men could get was 3s. a day.
They have “ gone West.”

Year after year S. A.’s gilded stock-
breeder, J. H. Angas, to whom a few lb. of wool
doesn’t make much difference, gives the job of
shearing his Hill River flocks to the aborigines of
the two southern S.A. mission stations. Forty
shearers and eight pickers have just been mustered
for the work.

iSons are forking hay up to old cookie, bare-
footed on top of stack, when suddenly the old man
gives a yell, slides down off stack, and bolts
towards house, shouting “Boys, I’m bitten!”
Sons capture him. One pulls out knife, locates
bite in ball of foot, and carves out egg-sized chunk.
Then they waltz patient around for half-an-liour
to prevent falling asleep, dosing him, at intervals,
with kerosene —only “stimulant”obtainable. But
no symptoms of snake-poisoning appear, and ex-
amination of cut-out piece reveals embedded
thistle-spine !

are most likely to produce a moderate number of well-
endowed children. Genius produces either no children
or degenerates; animalism produces, with rabbit-facility,

The N.S.W. Speakership.
“ Locdst,” which name covers the personality
of a well-known M.P., writes on the subject of
chaos:—
Dear Bulletin.— lt is certain that, at the opening of
next session of N.S.W. Parliament, Speaker Abbott will
retire, in consequence of ill-health, which everyone, I am
sure, regrets. A choice must then be made of the pre-
siding-officer of the rowdiest debating assembly in the
world, perhaps, with the exception of Austria. It can
safely be said that there is less bright debating-power per
representative in N.S.W. House than in any other Aus-
tralian Assembly; and yet there is an oceanic flow of
speeches. Causes not far to seek. Number one : Absence
of local-government. Local affairs come up for debate —
if you can call it debate —in the Assembly, which ought to
be threshed out in shire councils or divisional boards.
You have long ago pilloried the “member for Smith’s
Curse ” and the gigantic hearings of eloquence over the
“ bridge at Monkey.” Yet you would not be so hard on
the member for Smith’s Curse if you knew chat he had for
several weeks received threatening letters from some of
his constituents with regard to the bridge at Monkey, or
from another set, all related, who wanted to get the
failure of the family into the police where the
failures invariably go (when they are brieht and
successful, they become school - teachers). That
member, perhaps, has roosted on departmental door-
mats for several days until he is as exasperated as his
constituents. When the estimates come on he is “ loaded
for bear.” So are others—and the results are deliberative
chaos every time. Cause No. 2—want of local govern-
ment. You don’t elect debaters, you have no use for
them. Statesmen are shunted for departmental hustlers.
[All right, I’m coming to the Speakership gradually.]
You remember the actual, veritable arguments used
against Barton in the Hastings and Macleay election :
“ Mr. Barton is a statesman and therefore the worst man
you can get. He won’t hustle for roads and bridges and
culverts. He may save the country, but he’ll ruin your
district. Federation may result from his election, but
what’s the use if your drays are bogged at Mudtrap?
Elect Mr. Smith. He has absolutely no capacity politi-
cally, is not listened to in the House, and therefore can
sleep soundly while Barton is debating and get ahead of
him at the opening of the Works Department door next
morning.” Cause Nos. 3,4, 5, &o. Same old cause.
Now, as to the rowdiness of the Assembly. Perhaps it’s
the plan of the building which lends itself to the disorder.
Or perhaps it’s the natural hoggishness of the members
themselves. See them trying to catch the Speaker’s eye.
Mr. Blank, a man of large bulk, comes from the cross-
benches, say, from which he should have risen, and sits
on the Government or Opposition benches, well up to the
front. When the previous man sits down (and often
before) a dozen jump up together. The bulky man inter-
poses his elephantine carcase before several better men
than himself, and gets the call. If he doesn’t, he makes
it unpleasant in the lobbies, or worries the Speaker or
Chairman till they are afraid of him. This is an actual
instance —one Of many. It is a rule with Speakers—a
good rule, too—to call, a man from each side successively.
I have seen some of these belligerent members cross sides
in order to secure the call, and speak from the side hostile
to them. This is opposed to the very ancient and honor-
able etiquette, which keeps men on their own side of the
garden fence. Yet these “pointers” are tolerated, and
are more successful in securing the call than those even
whose speeches are worth listening to.
The new Speaker ought to be a man whose first duty
would be to put his boot down on these blatherskites
His next most important duty would be to sit down hard
on interrupters. Speeches in N.S.W. Assembly are rarely
solos, they are generally duets, trios, quartettes, or
choruses. And the stupid, senseless, inane interjections
are actually reported in Hansard (nowhere _ else),
whether they are pertinent to the speech, or imper-
tinent to everything and everyone—which they gene-
rally are. This ought to be stopped, and you want
a man who is capable of doing it. Who is the
man—that’s the question !- It is alleged that M'Coort
is the Governmental choice. If this choice goes,
Chaos itself will be law and order compared to the result.
Mr. M’Court’s amiability of disposition, which secures
for him the liking of the House, absolutely unfits him for
ruling a “ wild-west ” Assembly like ours. Imagine a
lamb presiding over an assembly of wolves—and goats 1
When a wolf rises to a point of order, showing a nice set
of teeth, what is the poor lamb to do ? Especially when
the lamb doesn’t know anything about Parliamentary
law and has never succeeded in learning the faintest rudi-
ments ! But when several wolves rise together, led by the
champion interjector of the House—the Int.erjector-
General—in the person of the Premier—well, then
Debate flies disgusted to the refreshment-room, and
Disorder ensues. Lyne is mentioned— Copeland, Ashton,
none of whom know a single thing about Parliamentary
law, and are therefore doomed to failure for lack of tech-
nical knowledge. Wise would be a good choice ; but
Wise is after higher game, I should think. Crick is
about the only man actually equipped for the position;
but he prefers, evidently, to waste his fine gifts on the
turf. So he’s out of it. I look in despair for a man who
possesses the necessary firmness combined with the
necessary knowledge, who has suavity of demeanor, com-
bined with inflexible impartiality, who has the iron hand
in the velvet glove, and yet is a sociable mortal when off
duty. Such a man would rescue N.S.W. Parliament from
the stigma of brutality and stupidity (a character which
the last two Parliaments have earned and deserved), and
would secure an honorable and well-paid position for as
long as he liked to hold it.— Locust.
That Obnoxious “ State Interference.”
“ F.C.” writes on the above subject:
The Vic. Factories Act, ’96, expires this year, and the
local Anti-Sweating League is already working hard not
only for a re-enactment, but also for a much wider exten-
sion of its provisions. Wherefore, the remarks of F. T.
Derham, president of Vic. Chamber of Manufacturers, on
the subject are interesting. Derham is practical owner
of a large local factory (Swallow & Ariell), and is loth
to admit the existence in the community of any such
thing as sweating, except, perhaps, among a few small
firms. “Although,” remarked the illogical Derham,
“ the existence of this evil must be deplored by every
right-thinking person, to endeavor to cope with it by
widely extending the operations of the present Act would
be a mistake.” He did not approve of Govt, interference
with wages rates. The more those engaged in the work
of building up a country were left alone the better, me
Chamber must set its face strongly against the proposal
of the Anti-Sweating League—that a B 11 should be intro-
duced into Parliament for the appoinim nt of boards to
fix rates of wages and hours of labor in any trade.
Which last—for the information of employers generally—
is exactly what Democrats and Progressives in all the pro-
vinces want to bring about. The provisions of the Yic. 96
Factories Act, especially those relating to hours of labor,
wages boards and apprentices, have already worked im-
mense good in the few trades to which they apply, and
there is no reason why similar provisions should not be
extended to all trades and occupations. How is it that,
in some Melb. suburbs, drapers’ and milliners’ assistants
get no half-holiday, while bootmakers and barbers ana
sundry other workers next door get an afternoon a week .
Why are tinsmiths exempt to such an extent that one
Melb. firm employs nearly twice as many boys as men ?
Why are butter-factories, creameries, and wineries ex-
empt? The Butter Factory Managers’ Conference, sitting
at Melb. recently, told some beautiful tales as to the
sweating carried on in that greasy trade. (An instance
was given of three of the largest Vic. factories whose
annual turnover amounted to £160,000, whose managers
received as salary barely o'4B per cent, of that sum among
them, while the Melb.' agenbs-they were middlemen—
BERNARD ESPINASSE.
Photo., Exchange Studios.
gathered in no less than 4’4 per cent). Also, why are
certain laundries, alleged to be carried on for charitable
purposes, carefully exempted, while the ordinary white-
shirt-ironer has to comply with the regulations? More-
over, the books of the Anti-Sweating League show
that it is not the small man only who grinds his few em-
ployes ; the big wholesale trader, whose name stands
high, who is a pillar of the church and a liberal donor
to charitable and philanthropic objects, owes his wealth
and position to his ability to squeeze his workers. And
as the policy of the Fat Man Govts, of the past has been
that of strict non-interference, in accordance with the
gorgeous theory of free competition and the equally gor-
geous law of supply-and-demand, and as the result has
been unlimited sweating and bone-grinding and industrial
slavery, it is time any Govt, which pretends to call itself
democratic started in to perform the duty so long neg-
lected. The Turner Government made a fair beginning
at the work so far as the brutishly-selfish House of 48
Rich Landlords would permit. But it was only a begin-
ning, and unless the country stands by with a large brick
ready for its House of Landlords that beginning will be
an ending also.

The Commercial World (London) writes re-
garding the Colonial Mutual Life Assurance Society :
“ We always confidently anticipate a good year from this
vigorously-conducted Mutual Society. The circumstance,
therefore, that the figures for 1897 show no falling away
from the stead 3 r and substantial progress which is inse-
parably connected with the Society’s past career occasions
us no surprise. As a matter of fact, the Society earns its
success by deserving it, and, that much being conceded,,
the best possible has been said for the continuance of the
large measure of public support which it has hitherto
fallen to its fortunate lot to receive.” Pleasant reading.*

POLITICAL
POINTS
N.S.W. Justice portfolio, after a short and
not too distinguished rest in the hands of a lay-
man, goes back to the tight grasp of the prac-
tising solicitor, Gould, Lee, Hughes.
“ Are two-thirds of the electors to coerce
one-third ?’’asks ex-Premier George Dibbs, with
a hazy Federal fact in his eye. And G. D.
proposes to slay this monster of oppression by
working up *-be one-third to coerce the two-
thirds.
Reid, the first N.S.W. Attorney-General
Premier since James Martin’s day, will now have
to appoint Judges and sanction or withhold their
claims for holidays. While merely Premier he
used to say, “ I possess no patronage in respect to
the Bench.”
Minister Lee, who goes to N.S.W. Works
Department, is the only properly-called country
member in the Reid team. Brunker and Young
are Sydneyites in all essentials, while Carruthers,
Parkes, Hogue, Hughes, and Reid are Sydneyites
even in accidentals.
DIDN'T LIKE THE SECURITY.
ISAAC ABRAHAMS (financier, etc., etc.): “S’elp
me, Rebecca, one of us must shi ft from here!
Your fortune-telling shop next door to me tells
all de shentlemen’s deg vos coming into a
fortune right avay, and dey come to your poor
old fader and rants to borrow £50 on der spot
on der strength of der hr aspects.”
Sydney Telegraph’s Federal defeat
rankles ; and the bad blood issues in many sur-
prising places. Even its obituary notice of the
late Edmund Webb, a Bathurst resident, whom
999-1000ths of Australians never even heard of,
was disfigured by a final tear for the great man
lost to the Anti-Billites.
J. H. Gordon has designs on the S.A.
premiership. He will leave the Council with the
next batch of retiring members, and, on the Consti-
tution of a Federal Parliament, will seek an
Assembly seat. He is the only S. Australian
front-ranker who promises to make no attempt to
reach one or other of the Federal Houses.
“ The Bill doesn’t acknowledge majority
rule. We’ll petition the Queen against the vote
of the Referendum ; ” and so on at a Sydney
mixed meeting. To whom a local Aristotle : “If
a majority adopt a Bill which doesn’t acknowledge
majority rule those who favor majority rule must
also adopt the Bill to secure the triumph of their
own principle.”
Dr. Cullen, M.L.C., declined, “for private
reasons,” to join the Reid Ministry as Vice-Presi-
dent of the Executive Council. All the reasons,
however, weren’t private : it is publicly known
that the office named carries no salary. In the
re-arrangement of offices, Mr. John Hughes be-
comes “ Justice,” Mr. Carruthers Treasurer, Mr.
Young “Lands,” and Mr. Lee “Works.” Mr.
Carruthers’ removal from the Lands Office will
involve a public loss, as his successor has neither
the capacity nor the knowledge necessary to ad-
minister such a department.
One of the complaints of the Uitlanders
against “OomPaul,” of the Transvaal, is thab
Kruger’s Government levies a tax of 5 per cent, on
dividends. Which doesn’t seem so very dreadful.
Tasmania and Queensland also tax dividends 5
per cent., and the British Government hasn’t
been moved to bombard Rockhampton or Devon-
port—at least, not yet.
Q. Labor-leader Dawson—whose efforts to
extend the franchise, during the passing of the
Enabling Bill, led some people to class him
as anti-Federal—has now publicly and emphati-
cally announced himself as a supporter of the
Commonwealth Bill. Someone stigmatised Daw-
son a “ Yes-No,” and he replied with a vigorous
“ Yes ! I leave the ‘No ’ to the Telegraph
crowd.”
The new Sydney city-railway proposal is
to make a central station on the block bounded
by Park, Elizabeth, King, and Pitt-streets.
It is the very best idea up to date, but
it will make the rebuilding of Sydney even
more imperative than it is now. The closing of
Market-street and Castlereagh-street will further
congest narrow, crooked George and King streets,
and they are already congested enough.
Sydney Daily Telegraph now seeks to
renew the fiscal fight and anxiously looks for a
Freetrade leader and at the same time invites the
Opposition to appoint a successor to Barton—some
one who will wipe out the D.T.’s great Freetrade
policy. Barton’s only followers, according to the
Telegraph, are See, Copeland, Ewing and Dugald
Thomson. Yet a day earlier the paper an-
nounced that Mr. Thomson was to be taken into
the Reid Cabinet. The Telegraph’s motive
clearly is to stir up party warfare on the chance
that in the strife something may turn up to block
Federation.
The cry for federation with Australia
raised by a party in Maoriland is the first sign
of the reversal of old habits. Absolute indepen-
dence of Australia has been the fixed purpose
of all departments of Maoriland activity. Even
its churches keep aloof. Maoriland C.E. has
its own Primate coequal with Australia’s ; its
R.C. hierarchy is also independent of and co-
equal with that in Australia. Yet there are
already signs that Maoriland will one day join
the Australian Federation and make the third
large State in the Commonwealth. And N.S.
Wales, as the mainland province most closely
associated with Maoriland, should “feel good”
at the prospect.
Western N.S.W. Crown tenants are
modest. When Minister Carruthers visited
Bourke t’other day, a squatters’ deputation
bombarded him with three requests : That they
should be forgiven 50 per cent, of their rents
for five years ; that all resumed areas (the parts
now open to selection) should be returned to
their leaseholds; and that for the lot they
should be given 14 years’ longer lease than they
now hold. This despite the facts that their
rents have been cut down lately 27 per cent, on
an average, and that when they can show that
their lands are not wanted for settlement they
can, under the last Land Act, get them at-
tached to their leaseholds. The Minister told
them that the landlord had already made big
concessions, and they must get some from the
mortgagees before they get any more from the
State.
During the few months of delay before the
Commonwealth can be formally welcomed, Bar-
ton’s place is not in N.S. W. Assembly keeping up
pretence of opposition in a Parliament where all
lines of party cleavage have merged into one—for
or against Australia. His place is up in Queens-
land whenever he can be spared—consummating
the work he has so well begun, and completing
the rout of the enemies of Federation.
S.A. Oppositionists, despite confident
Tory forecasts, did not challenge the Kingston
Govt, on its policy. When the last election was
over the Ass. crowd whooped that the Govt,
would meet the new Parliament minus a working
majority. The Tory artillery, it is now an-
nounced, is being reserved for Household Suffrage,
and Kingston, who has a majority of at least four
on that question, gaily invites the enemy to come
on.
Melb. Argus exhorts Premier Turner to
avoid all “ dangerous ” democratic legislation
during the next six months, and spend the time
in “ discussion preparatory and helpful to Vic-
toria’s place in the Commonwealth.” The Tory
journal seems to have a broad, statesmanlike idea
that three months at least might be usefully
devoted to educating the Federal mind up to the
beauties of having no property-vote on the Com-
monwealth.
In a little sub-leader of 30th June, Sydney
Telegraph once more got off its forlorn lie about
how “ the Bill places this populous colony on the
same basis, so far as votes and ultimate power are
concerned, as Tasmania.” (N.S.W. will have 31
votes in the Federal Legislature to Tasmania’s 11).
There is no visible purpose to be gained now by
lying over these matters—the referendum is over,
and the parochial drum has burst, and the country
has wiped its large bluchers on the parochial flag ;
but, after being crooked so long, the paper pro-
bably finds it difficult to get straight all at once.
Melbourne’s Mayor McEacharn, who so
badly put his foot in it over the footballers and
the Women’s Hospital, has again egregiously
blundered by announcing himself as a candidate
for Melbourne in the coming Federal Parliament.
The Mayor’s sole hope for legislative honors rests
on finding a purely Fatman constituency, and
the distribution of seats under Federation would
pive none of these. Melbourne city electorate
in Vic. Parliament is Tory, but under
Federation it would necessarily be so extended as
to include N. Melb., Footscray, and probably a
bit of the Port.
What the Uitlanders in the Transvaal now
demand is “ full citizen rights and a proper re-
distribution of seats,” and it appears that the
British Government is going to swim in blood to
get it for them. And while the British Govt, is
on the job it might swim in blood in Westralia
over an exactly similar matter. It would save
money by doing both swims at once. In Westra-
lia four small constituencies, where the old Sand-
gropers are, have 4 members for 330 voters ; and
four constituencies, mostly inhabited byUitlanders
from the East, have 4 members for 14,118 voters.
In other words, one ’groper is worth 43 Uitlanders
for voting purposes. Westralia gives the
Uitlanders a vote with reluctance, and it is worth
practically nothing when he has got it.
THE REV. FOOTBALL CAPTAIN AS A
PREACHER.
Sydney Telegraph :
Now that some of the opponents of the Secret Con-
ference Bill have decided to accept invitations to cam-
paign against the measure in Queensland, “ spontaneous ”
invitations will probably reach the leading supporters.
The Any-Pricers attach importance to the entry of
Queensland into the Federation.
Which is indeed rich. The “Any-Price anti-
Federalist Party ” which so loudly shrieked “ Waib
for Queensland ! ” and it was the Any-Price anti-
Federal leaders (Pilcher, MacLaurin and Heydon)
who so strenuously demanded a proviso to render
N.S.W. powerless to enter the Commonwealth
without Queensland. Of course the Telegraph-
Heydon crowd is now only too anxious for
Q’sland to stay out: for then they would allege
that as a faint excuse to resist the verdict of
the N.S.W. referendum.
The unsatisfactory issue of the Mercadool
(N.S.W.) land-dummying cases practically hangs
up a number of Riverina cases
which Minister Carruthers had in-
tended to proceed with. With
regard to the latter the Land Board
reported finding, as far as its juris-
diction went, two cases absolutely
proved. But the protracted charac-
ter of the Mercadool cases, the ocean
of public money they swallowed,
and the Full Court’s finding that
the prosecution was bad ab
initio, have taken away the pub-
lic appetite for a fresh exploit.
Only one man has been punished
legally for dummying in N.S.W.,
and he would, probably, have got
off had he not pleaded guilty. Yet
dummying was the local industry
which established several of the
first families of N.S.W.
In 1895 Maoriland shut
down hard on any further ex-
tension of the tied public-
house curse, bv enacting that no
contract of the kind made after
that date would be binding.
But it didn’t know exactly how
to adjust the vested interests
involved, so it agreed that all
existing contracts should still
hold good. Maoriland now
grows restive under this partial
survival of the old curse, and it
wouldn’t mind giving as much as
2s. Gd. to anybody who could find a
remedy with out doing too much damage to good
old Vested Interest.
The conscientious S.A. Parliamentary
Labor-party is making a serious effort, praised on
all sides, to really understand the pastoral problem
—and in S.A. it is a knottier problem than it is in
even N.S.W. So leader Batchelor and a Parlia-
mentary mate have been camel-tripping through
typical country, millions of acres of which have
lately been abandoned. They went over runs
covering 10,000 sq. miles, but carrying only 150,000
sheep. S.A. Govt, pays for improvements on
abandonment of country ; and this policy, which
luckily has never been seriously considered though
often suggested in N.S.W., is giving some start-
ling results.
The Bulletin keeps wondering what the
anti-Federal crowd which denounced the Braddon
clause (providing that each province shall get back
three-fourths of its own customs and excise) and
praised the Canadian system (which they knew
nothing about) would have liked to try the
Canadian idea. In Canada the Central Govt,
doesn’t give back to each province, rich and poor,
a fixed proportion of its own customs and excise.
Instead it gives each province a fixed subsidy
according to its needs. The richest province gets
80 cents per head, and the others go on
a sliding scale till the poorest gets 3 dol.
per head. If it had been suggested that
there be no Braddon clause, but that the N.S.W.
State Govt, should receive an annual subsidy of
3s. 4d. per head, while Tasmania got 12s. 6d. and:
the others varying amounts in between—well
there would have been very few admirers of the
Canadian Constitution left in the camp of Anti-
Bill.
The Forrest Grovt. reluctantly admits that
the question of a redistribution of Assembly seats
must be considered, and it proposes to sit down
and go to sleep alongside that problem in a serious
spirit. In ’9B things were bad enough, when
Ashburton, De Grey, East Kimberley, and Roe-
burne had four members for 336 electors, and the
four Coolgardie electorates had four members for
8289. But this year the first lot has been moth-
eaten down to 330 electors (a decrease of 6) and
the four goldfields constituencies have 14,118
voters (an increase of 5829), and the time is getting
near when the gold country will come and disre-
spectfully deposit all its decayed cats on John
Forrest’s front steps unless it gets justice.
According to official returns the wages of
10,135 operatives in trades mainly affected by the
Vic. minimum-wage law have gone up by
•£101,244 per annum as the result of that law.
And all these trades appear to be prospering des-
pite this handicap. Apropos of the proposed re-
enactment of this law, which is now expiring, J.
M. Davies, M.L.C., made some disappointing re-
marks : “ I don’t know if it has produced all the
results expected,” said Davies, “ for I don’t know
what was expected ” (cheers from Embling,
M.L.0.). “ But it hasn’t produced so much harm
as I thought it would ” (wail from Embling). “In
fact, so far as I can learn, it has been productive
of good ” (dry howl of anguish from Embling).
Even among the 48 Rich Landlords signs of con-
version begin to appear.
Some idea of how heavily that great tired
feeling afflicts the Cornstalk may be gathered
from the fact that, in the city and suburbs of
Sydney alone, there were 35,252 men holding
elector’s rights who failed to cast them either for
or against Australian unity. No doubt some were
dead, some gone a journey, but in all probability
most of them were sleeping, or howling themselves
hoarse at the football-match. Strange to say, it
seems to be the native-born who fails most in this
respect. Writer knows of one case where the native-
born poetically spread himself, and called upon
everybody to stand on his hindlegs, and take off
his hat to the new nation. Then, exhausted by
the great effort, he fell down, dead tired of the
whole subject till he heard that the votes of
104,000 other men had carried the day. Another:
Three families living in one terrace, three fathers
British, eight sons Cornstalk. On the day of
battle, the fathers rose early and cast their three

As anticipated, the mining-boom has been
causing also a burgling-boom. We learn from Adelaide
that a number of sharebrokers’ and other offices have
been entered and robbed of mining-scrip, mortgage
deeds, jewellery, &c., with cheques for £5O and
£67 16s. 6d. The clever gang have left Adelaide, and are
supposed to have come to Sydney. It will be wise,
therefore, for those with valuables to at once place them
in the Sydney Safe Deposit, in Ash-street, off George-
street (back of Paling’s), where they will be absolutely
secure. Inspection is freely invited.*
Hon. (ex-parson) Brentnall, the Brisbane
Tf.LKGKAI'H director whose frenzied literary ebul-
litions against Labor-leaders contributed so largely
to the present strength and cohesion of the Q.
Labor movement, has burst out in his rag against
the Commonwealth Bill with an article on
“ Democratic Federation.” Brentnall is the man
who tried, unsuccessfully, to gaol Labor-leader
Mabbott—now in Paraguay—for slander. The
Judge, in summing-up, contended that the words
used were slanderous, but that the provocation
palliated even illegal reprisals. Does the ridicu-
lous Brentnall seriously think that Democrats
will vote against Federation on his recommenda-
tion ?

2500 tons of sulphuric acid are consumed
annually by the Mount Morgan Gold Mining
Company of Queensland. It has been a matter of
some difficulty to find a material for roofing acid
towers which would resist the intense fumes
generated in the manufacture of acids. One of
the Company’s towers is roofed with P. and B.
Ruberoid, and present indications lead to the
belief that P. and B. Ruberoid will prove an
economical roof for acid towers or other buildings
which are subject to fume attack. The iron
trusses which support the roofs over the immense
roasting furnaces are protected from the acid fumes
by applications of two coats of P. and B. Paint.
This has furnished a perfect protection for over
two years. P. and B. Paint is specially valuable
for coating cyanide vats of either wood or metal.
Messrs. E. Rich and Co., Ltd., of Sydney,
N.S.W., are general agents for the colonies.*

The feature of last week’s Sydney sheep-
show was the close competition between the
splendid rams Premier and Jubilee; and the
experts present in the main agreed with the
decision in favor of the former. The single-judg-
ing system was so successful on this occasion that
it is not likely now to be departed from. There
was a goodly gathering of wool kings to
witness perhaps the finest display of ‘‘growing
wool ” ever seen in Australia, and a novel and
good idea was the holding of the jumbucks, while
being judged, by Technical College students, who
would thus, doubtless, profit by the judge’s remarks.
In the fat-sheep section the condition was hai-dly
as prime as writer would have thought necessary
for the home market; and many of the lambs in
this section were decidedly below the mark. Pre-
mier, the champion of champions, was bred from
American stock by Clark Bros., of Gestingthorpe,
and his fleece (not 12 months’ growth) was esti-
mated to scale, at lowest, well over 401 b. The
ewes’ grand championship fell to S. M'Caughey’s
Queen Annie, and the Lincoln honors were an-
nexed by S. Wiseman, of the Hunter River,
whilst with border Leicosters J. A. Cochrane, of
Victoria, was first. Amongst the Shropshires
(coming into favor for crossing with merinoes), B.
A. Cutler took several firsts. The great attrac-
tion of the show, however, was the sheep-dog
competition, won by a black-and-tan slut, owned
and worked by T. Webb, of Temora. This clever
animal put up what is probably a world’s record
by completing her task in less than 3 minutes.
Last year’s winner got only 4th place.
