SPORTING NOTIONS.
As far as can be gathered from the cricket)
cables, the Englishmen had no bits of bad luck in the
third test-match. When they lost the toss—well, it was
“embarrassing" for Darling to win the toss “under the
circumstances,” although the oircs. didn’t prevent
Worrall from scoring 76 (run out) in the Australians' first
innings. And when Briggs took ill, the critics at this
end laid no stress upon the fact that the A.E. team had
lost about 33 per cent, of its bowling strength, together
with the services of a batsman who could always be
expected to play sturdily “on the pinch.” In fact,
Briggs’s sudden disappearance was put down to the
glorious uncertainty of cricket. And the rain was a
fortunate thing for the Englishmen, seeing that if it had
cleared off in time to give them a ohance of scoring 158
runs on the third day they probably would have been all
out for less than 100 on the soddened wicket.
Cricket “ experts ” laughed at The Bulletin’s
suggestion that the Essex sailor-man (Young) should have
been tried in the first test-match. Here is another foolish
suggestion—of a likely English team for the fourth test
—Young, Hirst, Hearne, Mason, J. T. Brown, Jackson,
Fry, Maclaren, Lilley, Hayward and Jessop. Evidently
Ranji should be omitted. The Indian “prince” is the
Jonah of A.E. Elevens. They have had him with them
four times on English ground and all those matches have
been lost or drawn. The one Australia v. England match
which Trott's last visiting team pulled off, was the one
wherein Ranji played and scored a three-figure innings.
Twice when he didn’t play the Australians were beaten.
* *- * *
The aphorism “a game is never lost till it’s
won ” applies even more to cricket than any other sport.
With the Englishmen needing only 150 to score a win
in the third test-match, and having 10 wickets to fall,
the chances of Australia looked blue indeed; rain,
and with it the abandonment of the match, came oppor-
tunely. But it cannot be said that Australia had all the
luck either, for it would be hard to compute the chances
of three such bats as Hill, Gregory, and Noble again
playing five innings between them for nil in a single
match. However, the match was an object-lesson on the
difficulty of setting in “a rot” on the native boys; for
when {Larne played the hat-trick with Noble, Gregory,
and Hill, the four following batsmen—Kelli', Trumper,
Trumble, and Laver—ran up 101 runs among them.
The littleness of the mind of the average Aus-
tralian barraeker was strongly manifested in the third
Test-match. Most of them saw in the poor Australian
display the hand of Darling “faking” the game in the
interest of future gates. This paper, though uot too strong
on the inherent goodness of human nature, is credulous
enough to think that the ’99 Team will walk a straight
line in all matches.
• * * •
Yet Trumble’s recent successes entitled him to
a better position in the order of going-in, but in both
innings of the recent Test, Darling kept the Victorian
back. Public opinion veers to the opinion that, after all,
Worrall should have led this year’s team.
* * * *
Trumble saved his side in the third test-match
by his 56 run-out in 2nd innings. The long Victorian is
never seen to such advantage as when the odds are
heavy against him ; witness his stand with Hill against
Stoddart’s team in the 2nd test-match of that tour when
6 for 60 became 7 for 210. Noble, up to date, is no doubt
the best all-round performer in the present combination,
but Trumble runs him close. In all matches he is easily
first in bowling, and is 6th in batting, beating Trumper,
Kelly, Laver and Iredale. In the test matches he is
again top in bowling, while in batting he is actually
second, Hill only leading him. His figures—l 6 not-out,
38, 24, 20 not-out and 56 : average 51. A big feat for the
team’s star bowler with a bowling average of 61 wickets
for 807 runs.
» • * »
Nohle got a pull on Hill through the latter
standing down in the Oxford,. Leicester and Derby
matches, three of the softest fixtures of the tour, in which
he scored 240 runs and now leads by 76 for the aggregate.
The three leading averages at the end of the 3rd test-
match are—Noble 43, Hill 42, Worrall 41.
* * * *
“T.S.” writes: One of the surest signs that a
side dunno where it are is the constant chopping and
changing resorted to in England this season. Australia
18 months ago played 11 men, plus Lyons one match,
Worrall one, M'Kibbin two; she was playing a winning
game. England has already, for three matches, tried
men enough to make two full teams. And how curious
and changeable is cricket form 1 Abel and Lockwood
must have been among the first men picked for England
at the end of last season ; this year they apparently do
not come within the first 22, while Briggs—now cabled
to be very ill—returns to international honors, at the
expense of Rhodes. Tyldesley is given two test matches
because he has been one of the few able to play Austra-
lian bowling ; he pockets £4O and contributes 50 runs in
four completed innings—l6s. a run. Meanwhile, of the
four men who have made centuries against Jones, Howell
and co., three are never suggested for the big matches;
probably those dashing undergraduates,. Taylor and
Champain, could not between them repeat the success in
50 tries.
• * • »
The Australasian’s photo, of the Aus. XI.
v. Surrey would indicate that the English crowd stops
away vigorously from the match its own side is losing,
and that the present tour in consequence will prove
financially one of the least successful op' record. The
photo, shows 15 men on the arena—players and umpires
—and exactly 14 men and a small boy watching them.
What the proportion of “ gate ” would be to each player
is a mathematical problem that might be worked out at
the next matric. exam. B.S.—These remarks were
written before accounts of the third test-match came
along.
* * * *
Dear Bulletin. —Your London correspondent,
“ F.G.8.,” tried to explain away the Essex defeat of the
Australians, but the figures in last week's test-match
make “F.G.B.’s” apology look as ridiculous as most
cricketing apologies look six weeks after the event.
Essex howler Young followed up his destruction of the
Australian wickets at Leyton by taking 4 for 30 at Leeds,
so it is clear that the first performance was due to Young,
and not to any “ lifeless and lamentable ” batting on the
Australians’ part. The Britishers don't often achieve a
brilliant bowling feat—let them have full credit when
they do it once in a way.—X.X.
• * * *
The last All England Eleven included three
little ’uns in Quaife, Briggs, and Brown.
• * * *
The leather-hunting struggle Queensland v.
England on Exhibition Grounds (Brisbane) last Satur-
day afternoon drew 10,000 people—looo more than ever
previously patronised a football match in that province.
Gate totted up over £6OO. Q. secured first blood per
medium of a try. Gralton made a splendid attempt to
convert but failed. Score 3—o. Little later Evans,
after some capital passing, touched behind the
posts and Gralton, more successful this time,
sent the ball across the bar—B—o. Before half
time Corfe got a try—Gralton failed again, and the board
showed 11 ; England, nil. Renewing hostilities, Mul-
lineux quickly collared the leather, and by means of a
brilliant effort placed it near corner flag. Adamson tried
at goal without avail; 11—3. No further scoring took
place. Home team kicked with the wind in first
half, and thus gained an advantage. Visitors, with
wind in their favor during the second half, had
all the better of the game—keeping the ball in
enemy’s territory most of the time. Bananaland’s for-
wards played well and proved too speedy for the heavier
Britishers, of whom Martelli, Timms, Bucher and Adam-
son showed best. Nicholls, the top-notcher of the team,
was palpably off. Rev. Captain Mullineux, interviewed,
said the form of his men was unaccountably bad. He
thought the referee stuck too closely to the letter of the
rules, and characterised one or two decisions as unpar-
donable errors.
* * * *
Football, through the initiation of the district
system, booms in Adelaide after a three years’ decline.
The gate at match Port v. S. Adelaide, June 27, was £lBO
—the highest for years. Ports and Souths were .the
strongest opponents of the system which promises to put
them on their feet.

* * * *
The V.R.C. Grand National meeting 1 , now
promoted to the dignity of a 3-days’ fixture, will get a
good send-off on Saturday, weather permitting. Mel-
bourne has been so particularly damp of late that an out-
break of sunshine seems likely to happen this week ; on
the other hand, there is the probability of more flood and
mud. Taking one consideration with another, The Bul-
letin feels sorry for every sanguine punter who has risked
his shirt on two “dead-birds” for the “double.” The
state of the course next Saturday may rob the heavy-
weights of whatever chance they ought to have had, or it
may enable them to show their paces at the expense of
lightly - burdened commoners, such as Last Glen
and Podarces. The G.N. Hurdle - race result is
powerfully influenced by the heavens every year, and this
year the foolishness of an early fancy is rather more
marked than usual. Soult, who promises to start a hot
first favorite, is not the sort of horse to romp home
through a quagmire, although he has only a middle
weight to carry, but another 10 inches of rain before the
event would suit Nilus or Mystery right down to the
ground. Taking a batch of twelve as the most dangerous
—Soult, Valdis, Clamorous, Acton, Collendina, Mystery,
Loch Fad, Podarces, Myrmidon, Nilus, Last Glen,
and Borderer— The Bulletin plumps for Soult,
simply because there is nothing else worth plump-
ing for —on paper. Next to the dandy black
gee-gee, Mystery, Valdis, and Nilus may be worth
talking about, just for the sake of argument. Also,
it may be casually remarked that none of the afore-
said are “ real good things,” and they all have a chance
to fall at the last hurdle. Noctiluca, a very well-bred
mare, looks to have a big show in the Maiden Hurdle
next Saturday, and Reaper ought to score in one of the
flat races during the meeting.
Saturday’s running will throw a little light on
the Steeplechase question, but, meanwhile, Domino,
Pirate, Hayseed, Troy and Vigil appear to have reason-
able prospects of beating Floater, and the hopes of this
journal are pinned to Hayseed and Pirate.
Handicapper Dakin was up to time with Caul-
field and Melb. Cup weights last week. Noticeable that
Gauleon, full-brother to winners of last two Melb. Cups
(Gaulus and The Grafter), only gets 251 b. from
Bobadil as against 231 b. last year, since which time
“Bobby” has done some wonderful things, including
wins of Australian Cup and Champion Race ; while
Gauleon has not, during his career, got away with a
single event. Gauleon’s treatment is just as bad when
compared with that of others. Gozo—lndustry progeny
are apparently not going to be given an opportunity' to do
the “ hat-trick ” with Melb. Cnp, though you never know.
A close study makes writer think that Ace of Diamonds,
Massinissa, Renown, Cocos, Dirk Hammerhead, Parthian,
Wait a-Bit, Mora, The Chief, Scorn, or Chesney will
supply the winner of the big prize, while that at Caulfield
looks like going to Ottoman, Plutus, Dirk Hammerhead,
Mora, Maecenas, Ace of Diamonds, the Watchdog, Cale-
donia, Fleet Admiral, or Wait-a-Bit.

Cobar jumps to the front Among far-west
N.S.W. towns with a proposal for a spring programme of
between £4OO and £5OO. Broken Hill, which ought to be
easily' first among these wayback towns, runs four clubs.
Lately it was proposed that three of them should amal-
gamate and offer big money, but local owners kick—they
don’t want outsiders to come in—and the local public are
too tired to kick back.
* * * ■*
“ Old Timer ”: Saw that wild and woolly M.L.
fighter, Otto Cribb, at work, at Golden Gate Club
(Sydney), Saturday night. What a hurricane he is—quite
devoid of science, as generally understood, but a tough
’un from Toughville. Crude as were Joe Goddard’s
methods, Cribb’s are even more so. He’s on top of his
man from the word go—wiring-in with both hands—land-
anywhere and everywhere, and frequently nowhere ; it
matters not to Cribb where his hands pull-up so long as
they're flying round freely. Twice he missed his man and
punched the ring-posts, and ring-siders had to give the
ropes a wide berth for fear of stopping the misses.
“ Paddy ” Martin constituted the opposing element, and
“Paddy” also made things hum. His plan of campaign
chiefly consisted of headlong rushes, for butting purposes,
and, whenever the object of attack managed to avoid
these, Martin shot fair through the hempen boundary on
to the boards. At times, he flopped on all-fours and
“rabbited” the Maorilander. Once, while ducking for a
mad rush, Cribb caught Martin on his shoulder, and rose
with him like a butcher carrying a carcase of beef, and
the referee had to drag the load off. 700 people alter-
nated howls of excitement with screams of laughter
during 11 of the hottest, funniest, and most indescribable
rounds ever fought in a roped square. Martin skied the
towel ere the 12th round opened, a badly broken-up man,
and as limp as a dead snake. The M.L. boy won a good
stake and hosts of friends.
» * » •
“ M.” : It seems widely believed that Jews are
not game, and could consequently never he successful as
prize-fighters. Mendoza, “ the Jew-boy,” and dozens of
others —grit to the core—are conveniently forgotten.
Saw Otto Cribh fight, last Saturday' nighr., the pluckiest
and fiercest up-hill battle ever witnessed in Sydney. Ap-
parently beaten in the opening round, he hung on grimly
and ultimately won. His opponent butted, hit low, and
in other ways fouled time after time, but Cribb never
flinched or complained. Will swear his name is Jacobs,
Isaacs, Cohen, or Davis—certainly not Cribb —for a more
pronouncedly typical Hebrew could hardly be imagined.
• * * *
A Sydney Sunday paper boomed up novice-
boxer Bert Andrews as a second Griffo. Writer saw
Andrews at Sydney Golden Gate Club on Wednesday
night, and now marvels where the Sabbath scribe got
his notion from. A second Griffo ! ' What has the
unfortunate Sydney Rocks phenomenon ever done to
be thus maligned ? Andrews made such a poor showing
that novice Rollo really won handily on points, but
didn’t get the verdict—the match was declared a draw.

A Wyalong (N.S.W.) resident has been fined
£3 and costs, at the local police-court, for allowing his
son, a lad under 18, to play “ Devil’s pool.”
* » * •
Donnelly has finished his boat (to the order of
Mark Foy) intended to meet the Maid of Kent in England.
She is simply a decked-in flat-bottomed scow, 24ft. over-
all, and 22ft. on the water-line. That she could not live
with the Effie, the Sydney crack 22-footer, writer feels
assured, and it seems a pity Mr. Foy did not take ’Ome a
boat of Australian type to meet the Britisher, as, allow-
ing the new boat wins, the victory will be shorn of its
relish by the fact that she is merely built in Australia
from an English model. Win or lose, Australian boating
men should not accept her as a local representative.
* * • *
On the other hand, “Red Kangaroo” writes:
Sydney sailing • men (Mark Foy included) have
long boasted that Port Jaokson was the home
of small - boat sailing, and that the local un-
limited-crevv-and-Bail 22-footers were the fastest boats of
their size in the world. Nathless, Southern Cross, at
present building by Joe Donnelly, to Mr. Foy’s order, to
represent Sydney against England, is not a typical P.J.
boat at all, but a limited-crew-decked craft—closely
resembling Maid of Kent. Looks as if Sydney’s idol, the
open 22-footer, had been knocked out in one round.
* • • •
Cabled that Australasian champion swimmer,
“little Fred Lane,” started well “down-under” last
Saturday by annexing a half-mile scr. race, easily heating,
among others, the champion of London. Jack llellings,
the Woolloomooloo Bay (Sydney) lad and erstwhile
premier Australasian sprinter, is champion of London,
having won that title last season over a 500yds.
course—licking the London police crack, Claro,
by 2yds. in a very exciting finish. That Fred
would prove Jack’s master was evidenced soon after
former’s arrival, as in a friendly go he, without undue
exertion, held Jack all the w’ay. Lane was expected to
compete in long-distance (5 miles) championship of the
Thames to-day (Saturday), but a letter received last
week indicates that there is some probability of him
being a non-starter.

Champion swimmers Cavill are in clover. Said
that Ernie, the best of his time in these provinces and
now a resident of Boerland, recently hitched on to a
Yankee lady with a big bank credit. Sid., who lately ex-
ploited America, created such a favorable impression
there that he has been offered a good salary as instructor
at the fashionable ’Frisco Olympic Club, and will leave
for the Golden Gate by next boat.
At Poverty Point.
From Perth (W.A.) : War correspondent Villiers
opened to a hip- crowd at the Town Hall. on 12th
June. His limelight ohotos. are most convincing, notably
one showing the civilising effect of the Maxim gun on the
dervish soldiery; the audience could almost smell the
blood from the piled-up corpses. His lecture seemed to
be mostly a reading of extracts from F.V.’s own articles
with chunks of Villiersian humour, e.g. : “The Indian
Govt, lately issued an order that in future all letters ‘ u’
in native names be altered to ‘ a.’ Umritsur to Amritsar,
for instance. The name of dum-dum bullets was not
changed for obvious reasons.’’ (Loud and prolonged
silence.)
Some three years ago, when Miss Olive de Hugard won
the scholarship for pianoforte playing at Melb. Conserva-
torium, The Bulletin, in publishing the young damsel’s
photo., exhorted her not to cut short an artistic career by
getting married to a supposititious bank-clerk, or any-
thing of that sort. The charming Miss Olive effected a
compromise. She became the wife of Herr Eduard Scharf,
the pianist, and the other week she dedicated an inter-
esting little musical composition to her lord and master
under the title of “The Olive Branch.” That’s is to say,
Mrs. Scharf took the diploma of Ma.
Charming little Mdlle. Dreyfus scored a big success at
the Alliance Framjaise the other evening. The tall
Frenchman who played “ Hubby ” in the late Alliance
Frangaise comedietta is a recent arrival in Melb.—stands
over 6 ft., is a fair actor, plays a rattling good game of
tennis, knows a point or two about boxing, and was a,
member of the French team of footballers that played
England a few years ago.
“ C.C.” : Great indignation at St. Mary’s R.C. Cathedral
on Sunday, when outsiders jumped the pews at ten o’clock
to hear Amy Castles and had to be threatened with the
police from the pulpit before they would vacate. But if
St. Mary’s did not want the coin of the outsider, why
advertise Miss Castles’ appearance? If a church behaves
like a theatre, it is liable to be. treated as such.
Amongst the wild things said in Sydney about Amy
Castles is the statement that she aspires to be a “so-
prano assoluta.” The word “soprano,” though desig-
nating a female voice, would require the masculine
“assoluto” just as “barba” (beard), though denoting a
masculine characteristic, is a feminine noun. And, in any
case, the expression is nonsense ; as, though we can speak
of a prirna donna soprano or a p rim a donna contralto,
“ assoluta ” is only applied to the “absolutely” leading
lady, whatever her class of voice, and soprano assoluto
could only mean the leading soprano of the planet. And
Miss Castles is hardly &c. Another critic remarks
that a dramatic soprano is “ more nobler” than a light
one. Possibly this gentleman had himself had “more
nobblers ” than are advisable for the critic of a “great ”
daily.
“Lemuria”: Theatrical competition has now reached
the cut-throat stage in Sydney. The Curtis Co. at Sydney
Criterion is a replica and amplification of the McAdoo
Georgia Minstrels at the Palace. They even sing the
same songs. They also cut into the Tivoli. All three
have jugglers ; and the Criterion juggler beats the Palace
man very badly—that is, as far as amusement goes.
How he stands with Cinquevalli, this writeress knoweth
not, never having seen the man with the startling name ;
but the “Black Dante” of the Palace will take a lot of
beating. Both Palace and Criterion are running “cake-
walkers ” and “ buck and wing dancing.” Cake-walking
about even, but “ buck and wing ” business scores for the
Criterion. But as for singing, ah I—leaving1—leaving the doubtful
Miss Castles out of reckoning as an unknown quantity (not
having heard, cannot say) there are only four singers in
Sydney just now’—and they are all at the Palace, except
one : Mr. Duncan (of the Tivoli), Gerard Miller, Miss Bat-
son, and Mr. Rooks. They simply romp over everything
else running in the shows. Miss Hyer, of the Criterion,
has a voice of rather good range, but so has Miss Batson,
and Miss Batson’s is a date-plum compared to a peanut.
Most other alleged singers in Sydney, especially at the
Tivoli, want their larj nxes taken out and fresh reeds put
in, as their present ones are utterly worn out.
The well-beloved Edgar Straus, ’cellist, who has long
been seriously ill, left Sydney for San Francisco by the
mail on Wednesday, taking w'ith him an address sub-
scribed by many friends in and outside of the musical
profession. The address was presented at Paling’s, on
Monday afternoon by Mr. Edmund Barton, Q. 0., in the
presence of a numerous and sympathetic company.
Many friends will regret to hear that Alfred Dampier is
rather seriously ill at Newcastle.
Melba is said to be very enthusiastic over Regina
Nagel’s contralto voice. But the, question arises as to
whether Melba’s opinion isn’t biassed to some extent by
her alleged unfriendly feeling for Ada Crossley.
That wonderful old violinist, Dr. Joseph Joachim
who recently celebrated his 68th birthday, has now
been before the public for sixty years, having first
drawn the tail of the horse across the intestines of the
cat on St. Patrick’s Day, 1839.

Sure Cure
for Colds
When the children get their feet wet and
take cold give them a hot foot-bath, a howl

Sept. 28, 1895, says:
“ If I were asked which single medicine I should
prefer to take abroad with me, as likely to be
most generally useful, to the exclusion of all
others, I should say Chlorodyne. I never travel

On the assumption that sea-sickness is due
to lack of cerebral blood-supply, the wearing of
red spectacles is recommended. The ocean will
then be itself a red “ spectacle,” and will thus, it
is asserted, promote the flow of blood to the brain.
“Viator”: The Irish - Australian finds
Dublin very different to expectation. First, in
the deserted, flabby, dead look of the streets, after
the bustle of Glasgow, Manchester, and Liver-
pool. Second, in the almost Venetian appearance
of the city from the bay, whereas he had thought
of it as a pig-sticking Rotterdam, dingy and dirty.
But where is the glory of Imperial Federation,
as a birthday celebration, with a sullen Dublin ?
It is the grandest-pitched city on British pro-
perty, with Bombay second. Even the heart of a
Sassenach must swell as he mounts upon Howth
and surveys a panorama beating that from Mala-
bar. Phoenix Park is not named from the Classics,
but from Irish words signifying Fair Water.
“ A.D.” on the humors and brutalities of
the Sydney small-boy :
Pleasant watching the small-boy who haunts the harbor
piers, slipping like an alligator into filthy blood holes and
always alert for a “ cop.” They swim out joyously into
the open, and return with frantic strokes and bulging
eyes whenever the cry of “ Shark-oh ! ” goes up ; only to
go back with magnificent valor on finding that the alarm
is false. Nicknames such as “ Bondi,” “ Liver,” “ Sago,”
“ Stinker,” and “ Dingo,” are much in use. Watch them
for half-an-hour and you begin to see some of the pitiless
savagery that dominates the average boy of 11. And
just when you begin to hate him and his malicious cun-
ning, he will suddenly illuminate the depression with
lightning streaks of humor. Yesterday, at Rushcutter’s
Bav, “ Dingo” and his little “ push ” were soaking in the
billows at the sewer’s mouth. “Dingo’s” lirrle sisitah
Hemma mounted guard over their heaps of rags, while
her hilarious brother floated afar off with heels to shore.
In the mind of “Dingo” was the thought that
many prowling vestals were observing him, and
he felt sure that if they got a side view of
his feet they would mistake him for a man.
From time to time little Hemma left the rag-heap to
wring her hands over the jetty. “ Oh, Jimmy, Jimmy,
th’ sharks’ll git yer ! Come back, Jimmy; they’ll git
yer! ” She has net forgotten the wine-red patch that
smeared the water after “ Bondi ” Cleary had been torn
in halves within an inch of the pier, therefore she does
not approve of “Dingo” washing himself in the harbor.
“ Jimmy, they’ll git yer ! ” “Dingo” cast up his long feet
threateningly. “ Shut-up, will yer ! I’ll kick sparks outer
yer when I come a-out. Bloomin’ shark can have a beef-
steak if he wants one ! ” “ Stinker,” bitten with jealousy,
watched him from the less dangerous sewer, offering occa-
sional remarks. “ Look at ’im ! Blime, ’ow ’e kids his-
self. Thinks all the lydies are a-foxin’ ’im.” It seemed
that his words lashed the floating “ Dingo ” into fury, for
he swam shoreward with double-arm strokes until he
faced “ Stinker” in the sewer. “ Don’t chiak me’cause
yer father ’appens ter be a Crown-street bludger.”
“Dingo” turned to the naked “ push,” to observe the
full effect of his statement. “’ls father's a Crown-
street bludger. Bli-me, I ain’t afraid ter swim afore
the lydies, me body ain’t covered with pimples. Gam,
yer father’s o’ny a bludger.” “Stinker” retreated into
the sweet seclusion of the sewer, rifling his brain for
something bitter in the way of a gibe. “ What’s your
father?” he said, cautiously. “Nobody ever sees’im.
What’s your father, eh ?” “ Dingo ” splashed him gently
with a handful of filth. “ Garn, my old man’s a
toff. My old man,” said Dingo, reaching for more filth,
“is six foot in’is socks. ’E’s in the Govermint.” A flash
of recollection lit up Stinker’s eye. He leaped from the
sewer as if a sword had prodded his back. “ I know
’im ! . . . cripes, I know ’im ! ” He made furious gestures
j to the push. “ Don’t yous know that bloke wot smells
like rhubarb pie ? ” The howl of insane laughter that
followed proved that Stinker had scored.

A Cure for Miners’ Lead Poisoning.
▲ MOST IMPORTANT MATTER TO ALL MINERS.
Hay Street, Perth, 19th May, 1899.
Mr. S. A. PALMER, “ Vitadatio Institute,” Perth.
Sir,—Just a few lines to express to you my thanks for the re-
covery of my health, which is due to the use of your “ Vitadatio. *
While working in the Broken Hill mines I contracted Lead
Poisoning, which brought on a discharge of blood through the
bowels, and left me in such a state that it became impossible
for me to follow my occupation of an engine-driver, or, in
fact, to do any work at all. Luckily for me, you brought this
wonderful medicine to West Australia, and I was persuaded
to try it. I have taken about ten bottles ; the discharge

&

A Cure for what Defeated the Doctors.
Springhurst, Victoria, 30th May, 1899.
Mr. PALMER, Melbourne.
Dear Sir.—l have been a fearful sufferer for the past four or five
years. One doctor would not treat me. He said I was too far gone.
Another doctor gave me up, and another said he could do nothing
for me ; and I was considered to be in a state of collapse. The
doctors called it a general breakdown of the system, my age being
68 next birthday. During my illness I took some 30 bottles of
one advertised medicine, five bottles of another, seven bottles
of another, and two jars of another ; and, as regards doctors’
medicines, I really took same until I was sick and tired. Upon
reference to any of my neighbors they can tell you how bad I
was. I have now taken eight bottles of your VITADATIO,
and feel better than I have for years. I can get about and
do my work, and have a good appetite, and I must can-
didly acknowledge, with gratefulness, that your VITA-
DATIO put me on my feet, and it's the only medicine
that has ever done me any good. You are at liberty to
use my name if you wish.—Yours gratefully,
Springhurst. EZEKIEL M‘CANN.

A Bit of the Truth.
The most pertinent and startling question ever
asked is this :—“ What is truth?"
A Homan Governor propounded it once, under
circumstances which greatly worried and perplexed
his official mind. And he was not the first man
or the last —not by myriads. So far as I know
there has been no satisfactory answer. Some
people (radicals and come-outers of various sorts)
fancy that in this, the tail end of a rather braggart
and conceited century, they have flushed a fair-
sized covey of truths by firing speculative shot-
guns into every wayside bush and bog.
But have they done it ? No, gentle reader, no.
They have put up crows and sparrows, the same
crows that picked the bones of the cave-dwellers,
and the same sparrows that sold two for a farthing
in the time of Pilate. There were plenty of fools
of old, and there are plenty now. The ancient
doctors, indeed, prescribed some horrible stuffs as
medicine—they used electuaries of viper’s flesh and
recommended pomegranate seeds for toothache
because those seeds resemble human teeth. Very
shallow and silly, to be sure, this sounds to us.
But if you wanted to find things that come very
near matching them in modern practice, I could
show you where to look. On my table I have a
1 list of about 300 new “remedies ” introduced to a
suffering world within the past twelvemonth.
“ Must be some good ones among them,” do you
say? Possibly ! Time will tell. Meanwhile let
us stick to whatever we are sure of. “We learn
how to cure diseases,” said Celsus, “by experi-
ence, not by reasoning.”
“ Some of the greatest truths in medicine,” said
a learned Scotch doctor, “ came by the humblest
means ; not by synthesis or venesection, but by
the observations of peasants and the experiments
of motherly women.”
Concerning a medicine discovered by one such
woman, thousands of stories have been told and
letters written. Here is an example:—
“ For many years I have suffered from indiges-
tion and weakness. I seemed to have no energy
for anything. 1 had a poor appetite, and what
little food I ate caused me violent pains in the
chest and between the shoulders. Frequently I
had attacks of giddiness, and when I stooped I
suffered from an unnatural rush of blood to the
head. The pain which I was called upon to bear
was often very severe ; it affected all parts of my
body, and at night I got little proper and refresh-
ing sleep on account of it.
“As time went on and the complaint grew
fixed upon me, I came to be exceedingly weak,
and now and again was obliged to take to my bed.
I lost flesh and became quite thin, living, as I
did, only on milk, beef tea, and other kinds of
liquid food. It will be understood, of course, that
I had medical care, besides attending the South
London Dispensary. Yet I received ho benefit
from what was done for me.
“It happened that in January, 1889, a friend
—Mr. Pullen —told me he had suffered in a similar
manner, and been cured by a remedy called Mother
Seigel's Syrup. Acting on his suggestion, I got a
bottle, and, after having taken it, I found great
relief. Presently my appetite returned, and food
no longer distressed me. Convinced that Mother
Seigel’s Syrup was adapted to my ailment, I con-
tinued the use of it until it was no longer needed.
My health and strength were re-established, and I
have since been well. This medicine has done
what no others had been able to do. My husband,
who suffered from biliousness, used it with thesame
result. You have my free consent to publish this
brief statement, if you desire to do so.”—(Signed)
Mrs. Julia Massey, 133 Lorrimore-road, Kenning-
ton, London, S.E., January 20th,. 1898.
There is no royal road to the discovery of truth
or knowledge. Anybody may find it anywhere.
It is not always he who seeks that finds. Valuable
discoveries are usually made by what, for lack of
a better word, we call accident. The medicine
that cures is the medicine we want, no matter
whether it is as old as the earth or was picked up
yesterday in the fields by a child. That Mother
Seigel’s Syrup, cures is proved by a cloud of wit-
nesses. It is a bit of the truth. Therefore it will
not die out, and nothing can take its place.

With ah ordinary tape measure determine the width
round the joints AA, round theinstep 88, round heel CC,
round the ankle DD ; next ascertain the length and width
of tread by placing the foot on a sheet of paper, and
running a lead pencil round the heel and toes. Cut the
outline thus made with a sci-sors, and enclose with the
other measurements, stating what size you generally take,
what are your peculiarities— a weak ankle, perhaps a pet
corn, ingrowing nails, or a blooming bunion. All these
little matters attended to. If you have a long thin foot, or
a short thick one, with very high instep, send to ELLIS’S,
where you are sure to get a lit. The proprietor, with a

“Struck Down for Death.”
His Own Words*
Experts said he could not be cured
HIS PERFECT CURE A SURPRISE TO EVERYBODY.
(From the Freeman’s Journal.)
(by our own reporter.)
Mr. J. Hawkes, of Hawkesview^
Guildford, N.S.W., is now one of the
strongest and healtliiest-looking men one
could meet in a day’s march. We heard
that for five long years he had been a terrible
■sufferer, and that a course of Clements
{Tonic had completely restored his health,
so this paper {The Fnem in’s Journal, Syd-
ney) dispatched a reporter to see him.
“ You look as though you’d never had a
•day's illness in your life,” said our reporter
«on seeing Mr. Hawkes.
“ I may look well enough now,” was the
tteply, “and
My Health is Perfect,
hut once I was struck down for death. The
doctors have given me up, and it seemed as
though nothing remained for me but to
settle my affairs of this world and prepare
for the next.”
■“You must have had a narrow squeak,
then. What first caused your illness?”
■“ It must have been coming on for a long
time before it showed itself. My occupation
was very trying. I was a market gardener,
and I carted large quantities of vegetables
to the Belinore Markets. This necessitated
getting up very early in the morning, and,
as a matter of fact, I often waited up all
night so as to be first in the field and get
the best prices. My meals I got at very
irregular hours. I began to get a
Queer, Giddy Feeling:,
which I can hardly describe, and one day,
after I came back from the markets, I was
seized with a fit. That was the beginning.
I temporarily got better of this attack, but
I was a changed man. Shooting pains
w. uld suddenly dart through my back and
head, after which a feeling of sickness and
Faintness would Overwhelm
me. My digestion went all wrong, and
what little I ate did no good, for I got more
like a confirmed invalid every day.”
“ Did you see a doctor ?”
“Yes, several; but they didn’t know
what was the matter at first; but some of
them said I was suffering from a sluggish
and disordered liver, and they gave me a
whole host of directions as to diet and how
I was to live, besides any quantity of medi-
cines. & tried hot bandages to my back and
abdomen, but got no relief from them, nor
did the doctors’ physic do me any permanent
good. All day long I was in such a sluggish
state that I cared for nothing. I was in a
sort of sluggish
Stupor or Lethargy,
which never left me. I suffered in this way
Eor over five years. At last I got into such
a despondent state that when I wished to
see the doctor I was too nervous to drive to
his house. I was frightened of going off
through the shaking of the cart, so I was
forced to accept what support I could get
from a friend, and crawl along with him and
the help of a stick.
I had got so Weak
that I couldn’t walk without a stick. . My
face was drawn, and anyone could *•* in it
the pallor of a long-continued discos*. What
I have described was my usual state, but
every now and again the shooting pains .in
my head and back would become more in-
tense, and then I should have another fit.
Thinking that perhaps I could obtain better
treatment I
Went to the Hospital.
Three doctors there gave me a long and
very careful examination, but they could
not tell definitely what was the matter.
And all their medicines and attention failed
to cure me. livery doctor asked me the
same question, * What is the matter with
you ?’
This Silly Question,
in my enfeebled state, used to rouse and
irritate me. * That's what I came to you to
know,’ I replied.”
“ Your case must have seemed hopeless ?
“ Yes ; I had very little hope of ever
retting better. I became very low-spirited*
1 took lip interest iu anything. I
Consulted an Expert
in liver disease. He told me that my liver
was in a very bad state, and led me to
understand t'. at
I Could Never Expect
to be cured ; so I gave up all hope of getting
better again. I resigned myself to my fate.
I felt that I was already in the grasp of
Death, and lay in bed expecting my end.”
“ You cheated death, however, judging
by your appearance.”
“ Yes, and this is how it happened. My
nephew recommended Clements Tonic. I
got some, hoping it would at least relievo
me.”
“ And did it Mr. Hawkes ?”
“ The first dose was more use than all the
other medicines I had tried. It was vory
effective, for it
Struck at the Root
of my complaint. From the first Clement#
Tonic had a stimulating action on my liver.
The pains in my back were soon easier, and
my head felt lighter and clearer than it had
done for a long time.”
“ Did you still have fits ?”
“No, not a fit; it stopped them at once.
I persevered with Clements Tonic, and it
was plain to everyone that I was getting
better daily. I
Sacked the Doctors and scorned
their prescriptions. Their me-
dicines did not touch my dis-
ease. Clements Tonic cured it,
and all the evils went away without my being
aware of it. My appetite returned, I put
on flesh again, and soon got strong. I lost
all that tired weary feeling of lassitude and
was quite bright and cheerful, and instead
of sitting half stupefied in a chair all day or
Lying like a Log
in bed, I felt lively and energetic. A few
weeks’ treatment with Clements Tonic quite
restored me, but, of course after five long
years’ illness one cannot expect to
Rebuild a Wasted System
in a week, but the way Clements Tonic
brought me along was a perfect surprise, not
only to me, but to everybody. The only
regret I have now is that I had not been
lucky enough to strike Clements Tonic at
the start. If I had known iU value earlier
that first attaok would have been my last,
and I should have saved
Five Long; Years of Suffering;*
I am thankful, however, that I came across
Clements Tonic when I did —‘ Better late
than never.’ It entirely eradicated all
disease from my system, and did what
doctor’s physic could not do in all those
years. In short, it saved my life.”
“And may I publish your endorsement,
Mr. Hawkes?”
“ Certainly, it is the simple truth that
I have told you ; I am here to prove it, and
all the neighbours know I was
Knocking; at Death’s Door
for five years. I have proved Clements
Tonic a genuine article ; I owe my life to it.
Why should I, or anybody else, object or be
AAh&med to endorse such a valuable medicine.

[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
She was his housekeeper and the mother of most
of his children—a large, deep-bosomed woman,
with a fine command of language upon occasion.
Evidently the occasion had arisen, for he could
hear her, as he sat in the office, scolding his eldest
and only child of a long-dead wife, and in these
early and more sober hours of the morning he was
apt to wonder how he had been foolish enough to
put so much in her power, and to make valiant
resolutions of pensioning her and her children off
when the station should pay better, and sending
his own daughter to be educated and brought up
decently.
The shrill voice, with its twang, sounded louder
than usual this morning, chiming in as it did with
more pressing worries. It was a bit of bad luck
the stock-inspector dropping upon him just as
those cases of anthrax in the sheep had cropped
up. He had suspected it, but hoped to trade them
off in time, and now they would have to be
destroyed. However, he didn’t know—there
might be a way.
Again that voice! Curse the woman, would
she never let him have peace ! He must really
make an effort to pension her. These were his
morning thoughts ; but as the day wore on and
the nips became more frequent, he viewed things
differently. The old girl suited him very well;
she was plump and yielding, and her cooking was
Al. The kids, too, were jolly little beggars,
especially the boy; he ruled the house, and no
mistake, and had the blind side of his mother
“ properly.”
Her husband was chopping wood in the yard ;
an elderly man, half-witted from the effects of an
accident. He did odd jobs about the place, lived
in a hut by himself, and was known as
“Softy.” The master hardly remembered his
existence, and, when he did, thought of
him with good-humored toleration as a poor
devil who was lucky to have his tucker always
provided for him. But the woman hated him, as
one can hate the human stumbling-block in one’s
path. But for him, she might have been mistress
m name, as she had long been in reality. She
hated him, as only such women can, for his mental
and physical feebleness, and, woman-like, vented
it in petty tyrannies. A habit he had, of hurting
himself through careless handling of tools, seemed
for some inscrutable reason to intensify her
hatred. The sight of him this morning, chopping
wood with a bandage round his foot, as she
turned from her scolding of the girl, roused her to
fury. . . ,
“ Leave that chopping, you wretched idiot, and
get out of my sight quick, or I’ll throw boiling
water over you ! ” she called, and as the old crea-
ture limped away like some poor old cur that has
been kicked, the look in her eyes was devilish in
its hate, and her lips grew firm in resolve.
* * * *
“Oh ! give it a rest. What narm does the man
do you ’ We can’t turn the old beggar away to
starve.” This was the usual tenor of the master s
reply to her periodical naggings on the subject.
Hitherto it had sufficed ; but a time comes when
the worm turns, and the camel is unable to bear
another straw. She had evidently reached that
stage.
‘ r You’re a fool!” she nagged back— “you
haven’t got the spirit of a mouse. Any other man
would have got rid of such useless lumber long ago
for good and all,” and she faced him defiantly.
He took his pipe out of his mouth and looked at
her.
“ What do you mean ? ”
“ You know what I mean ; it’s easy enough,”
and taking a folded newspaper out of her pocket,
she pointed to a paragraph and handed it to him.
He read it and flung it to the farthest corner of
the room with an oath. “By God, what she-
devils you women can be 1 Out of this. I’ve had
enough of you ! ”
• • • *
She took him at his word, and henceforth
confined herself strictly to her duties as working
housekeeper, only speaking when spoken to,
cooking the plainest of meals, and keeping the
children as much as possible to her own quarters.
At first he hardly noticed ; then he began to miss
the family life he had grown accustomed to, the
dainty little meals, and the blandishments she
knew so well how to practise; and, the incident of
the paper having faded from his memory, he tried
by various means to restore the old state of things.
She eluded him with feminine artifice, and he
began to feel all the ardour of a new pursuit; but
with continual denial he grew moody and irrit-
able, and passed from coaxing and pleading to
threats.
“There’s only one way, and you know it!” she
said, significantly. But he shook his head. The
time was not yet.
However, the struggle ended as it always does
where a strong will is pitted against a weak, self-
indulgent nature, and the day came when the
master ordered “ Softy ”to get a sheep out of
No. 2 paddock atid dress it. The man who
always did the killing heard the order and ven-
tured the remonstrance that “Softy” wasn’t
much of a butcher, but was told that the sheep
was not wanted for meat, merely as an experi-
ment.
• * • *
“Softy” died shortly afterwards. Blood-poi-
soning, the doctor said.
KARL CARR.
An exalted personage visited an Australian
watering-place during the past summer, and put
up with his suite at the leading hostelry. When
the visit terminated, the cavalcade departed with-
out mentioning such mundane matters as bills,
and, after a decorous silence of a month, the
account was rendered, charging the visitors the
inordinately small sum of <£2 10s. a week each.
The total came to .£BO, but a shilling or two more
was unavailingly spent in posting the bills to the
exalted debtor. At last, the matter was taken to
a solicitor, who informed the sorrowing boniface
that such personages are exempt from petty-debt
summonses, but he promised to do his best, and
again approached the awesome Presence to have
the account liquidated. But the Presence took ex-
ception to items for wine, horses, and different
things, which it held should have been included in
the 508. a week, and eventually the matter was
compromised by the payment of =£6o, in place of
the .£BO demanded. The next time that hotel-
keeper has any exalted personages at his house he
is going to have each meal paid for as it is dumped
down on the plate.
“As the Sayin’ Is.”
An Aboriginal Idyl.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was a gawky-looking dairyman. I met him
at the township ; and, as I was going his way, we
rode out together. He said he was glad of my
company, as he liked somebody to talk to.
■For a long time he spoke about an old bull his
grandfather owned about 20 years before. The
best ever was in the district. Had taken one
hundred and twenty-nine ‘‘blues ” at the different
shows, and they had “ as much ribbon at home as
would go half round the farm—which was no
pm all one.” He (the bull) was a wonderful
animal, “but they don’t breed ’em nowadays—
don’t pay.”
After he had exhausted the bull, he asked,
rather abruptly, if I had ever done any “ coortin’.”
Told him I had, once. Then he tried to draw me
out on the subject, but, finding that no use, ho
asked “straight” how I got on with it. Told
him I married the girl. He didn’t look satisfied,
and remarked that my experience of the other sex
was small —very small —and that I could know
very little about it. He seemed rather inclined to
pity me.
“Look here,”he said, “I coorted from before
I was eighteen until I got married at chirty-eight—
that is, twenty solid years of it—so that I should
be allowed to know a bit about it.
“The first piece I got in tow with was a good
sort, but she was no match for me, as the sayin’ is.
Yer see, we owned the farm we were on, and her
people were only tenants, and dash’d poor ones at
that. I was considered a catch at that time, as
the sayin’ is. All the same, this girl was all right,
although, of course , she knew, and her ole man
knew, that I’d never marry her. I was only
coortin’ —amusin’ myself like. At _ last, I was
forbidden the house, as the sayin’ is. But that
didn’t make any dif. to me. We used to meet
regularly in the dairy. Mostly we were in there
from 10 or 11 until 3 o’clock in the morning.
Lord ! I used to be that sleepy at the
milkin’ that I could scarcely keep my perch on
the stool—used to hang on to the cow to keep
myself from failin’ back. My old governor blamed
tobacco for it.
“Well, one night me and Mag was in the dairy,
coortin’ away, and we got an awful fright. A
big rat got into a dish of cream—that was before
separatin’ came into use —and if he didn t splash,
it’s a caution. Of course, he soon drowned, and
that was the end of him. But Mag thought it
was the ole man a-comin’, and fainted in me arms.
I soon brought her round with milk, though.
Just threw some in her face, and then rubbed her
wrists with some for a bit. I’m up to all them
dodges. .
“Another night we was billm away like two
turtle-doves, as the sayin’ is, when all at once the
bloomin’ole plough-horse fell down the well. Now,
the well was right in front of the dairy, and we’d
have been in a nice fix had the ole man heard the
splash. I couldn’t give the alarm, and Ma°: was
was not going to give herself away, so the horse
had to die —there was no gettin’ out. He splashed
and grunted and groaned for nearly an hour
before he turned it up, and he was a good ’un too.
I’ve often laughed about it since.
“But one night the ole man did come along.
He must have smelt a rat, as the sayin’ is. All
at once there was a hammerin’ on the door, and
he called, ‘ Come out ——or I’ll shoot! ’
“Is T ow, I knew the girl (although no match for
me, as the sayin’ is) would put herself between me
and all the guns m creation, and so I got a bit
cheeky. I told him to come in and I d brain
him —didn’t care for neither him nor his gun.
There were two doors on the dairy, and, when he
found he couldn’t get in at the front one, he went
round. That was my chance. I pulls back the
bolt, an’ ‘gets’for my ‘natural.’ That was the
last I saw of Mag until the other day. She was
a bit of a sneak, you know, or she wouldn’t have
met me in the dairy; an’ I never could stand a
sneak. Of course, she knew I wouldn’t marry her,
although I never told her so. Besides, she was
no match for me, as the sayin’ is. Just now she
is spliced to one of the leadin’ men here, an’ she
oughter make him a good wife, as the sayin’ is.
I’d give you her address, if you were stoppin’.
“ That’s the man has the money,” he broke off,
pointing to a house over in a paddock.
“ Who is that ? ” I asked.
“My old dad-in-law.”
“And the wife?”
“ One of the best women in the country. Just
a match for me, as the sayin’ is.”
YARRUM.
At Windever, near Mudgee, a Mrs. Mul-
hollar.d a publican, has taken out her fiftieth
license—said to be a N.B. W. record. The pub. is
named “The Good Woman Inn.”
The Tasmanian capital badly needs a com-
prehensive drainage system, and a poll is to be
taken about establishing one—the sewage to be all
run into the magnificent Derwent River at a point
right opposite the city. It is a beautifully Tas-
manian idea, but then they have a lot of Tas-
manian ideas in Tasmania.
“Miles”: Let me piece up Transvaal
history. The Boers, in 1835, began their dash out
for fresh pastures, just like the Mormons going off
to Salt Lake. England tried to keep them in the
net and ungraciously conceded freedom in ’52.
The annexation by Sir Theophilus Shepstone, in
’77, was a soft thing. He had no soldiers, only
26 policemen, and the Boers merely growled.
Rebellion in 1880-1. Colley was negotiating and
yet must needs do a brilliant stroke by seizing
Majuba (Pigeon) Hill. It was a masterly stra-
tegical move, but his soldiers got demoralised by
the deadly rifle-firing of concealed Boers. The
British held the hill triumphantly, and—pouf—
they were gone. A Boer shot Colley in the fore-
head, as he might an obstinate bullock.
S.A. Government Gazette prints the
certified returns of Parliamentary candidates’
expenses. Every electorate in the province is
double-barrelled, and the moral that gets the
biggest lift is “ When electioneering, always have
a pal.” Or, if you are pretty sure of winning,
get three or four pals to share the exes. Theo.
Bruce, who ran “ on his own ” in Sturt, has to pay
,£260 9s. Bd. (for nothing but experience, too), and
Conventioner Glynn, who had a big battle single-
handed at N. Adelaide, spent .£219 18s. 9d. John
Darling (Captain Joseph’s brother) topped the
score, however, with .£260 9s. Bd. But Treas.
Holder, who toured with three or four other can-
didates, put his slender bank balance back by
only £33. Poster, M.P. for Newcastle, declares
he spent only £3 15s. 6d., while his wildly extra-
vagant colleague, Burgoyne, got away with £3
19s. Premier Kingston’s expenses reached £Bl.

The Man Who Told You So.
[FOR THE BULLETIN. ]
Of all the fiends who walk this earth.
Whose game, it seems, is mainly
To make us curse for all we’re worth
And swear and speak profanely,
I’ll back the brute, in time of woe
Who comes and says, “I told you so !”
Does speculation bring you down
And ruin you completely,
Or spielers get your last half-crown
Particularly neatly,
He never fails to let you know
That all along he told you so.
With buoyant hope out West you went
To make a fortune speedy,
But came back home without a cent,
Disgusted, worn, and seedy.
He met you with—his face aglow—
“ Now recollect, I told you so 1”
That little bill, to save a friend,
Accepted by you blindly,
Meets with dishonour in the end,
And lets you in unkindly;
His hope that soon you’ll wiser grow
Is prefixed by “ I told you so ! ”
The maiden whom you were to wed,
Who swore she loved you madly,
Gets married to a pal instead,
Which wounds you very badly;
Again, his sympathy to show,
Out comes the same old “Told you so !'
And much I dread that, by-and-by,
When we’re amongst the tainted,
And with the imps in Satan’s sty
Are getting fast acquainted,
He’ll point to us from Heaven’s front row,
And wag his chin—“ I told them so ! ”
STYX.
Mrs. Steve Kennedy.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
At that time I, in common with most of the other
men on the big western station, thought Mrs. Steve
Epnnerlv n *„ • ,•
y y > nderful and fascinating per-
son. l say most of the other men, for there were
two exceptions who took no interest whatever in
her or her actions, viz., Steve Kennedy, her hus-
band, and Harrington, the book-keeper. Now
looking back with the clearer eyes of middle age
• T 1 uxi * i-
and experience, I know her to have been nothing
more or less than a pretty, bold-eyed, vulgar little
cockney with a good figure and an overplus of
energy and brass
Life, if peaceful, had been drearily monotonous
before she oame. There were one or two women
on the station, but of no very attractive type,
from flat-chested, melancholy Mrs. Blake, the
overseer’s wife, whose thoughts were always full
of the wants of her numerous children, to Mrs.
Reuss, whose husband drove the bullock-dray, and
who was chiefly famous for being able to lift a bag
of flour on to her back with as much ease as the
strongest man on the place.
Steve Kennedy came along with his wife and
two children just as the boss was in need of a man
to drive the engine and a woman to cook for the
men’s hut and the quarters. Mrs. Steve cooked
well on the whole, but nocone of us dared to grumble
when things went wrong. No one wanted a
taste of Mrs. Steve’s tongue a second time. Sceve
was a taciturn, gipsy-faced fellow who never inter-
fered with his wife’s favorite pastime, which was
the harmless one of binding every male, from the
grey-headed butcher to the youngest horse-boy, to
her chariot-wheels.
Every evening, as the six o’clock bell rang, she
would put on a fresh print frock, stick a real gera-
nium in her thick dark hair, and saunter out to
meet Steve —her work-roughened little fingers
busy with crochet, the two pretty children run-
ning after her. She also met ali the hands, as a
rule, returning over the low-level bridge after the
day’s work in the paddocks or yards, and she had
a bright glance and a merry word for each.
I think the other women would have liked her
better if she had been slovenly or a bad house-
keeper—but everything was in order before she
went for that evening stroll. Years on years of
tropic summers and memories of long, hard
droughts had not laid their mark on her as they
had on those other women, poor tilings ; and she
used her ad vantages to their utmost, for she was
the vainest little creature on God’s earth. Some-
times, after the children were tucked into bed and
the piles of greasy dishes got through, with the
help of myself or seme other admirer, she would
come over to the quarters and entertain us with
tales of past conquests and experiences, and we—
starved for the sight of a womanly woman—hung
on every word and believed all she liked to tell us.
She used to say she would “ box any feller’s ears
who attempted to kiss her.’’ I waylaid her in the
passage more than once, and she didn’t box mine,
but I quite thought she meant all she said in
regard to the other chaps, and no doubt one and
all thought the same.
She called us all by our surnames without pre-
fix —except, again, Harrington, who treated her
as if she were a servant and nothing more, and
who never ans wered unless she addressed him as
“mister.” He was a quiet Sydneyite, with a
plain face and wonderful eyes, and he used to
read a lot when he wasn’t working. Mrs. Steve
called him a “ stuck up devil.” All the same, she
looked after his comfort in all sorts of ways, say-
ing privately that he was' just the mean sort that
would go and complain to the boss if she didn't.
He used to read with his back to the lamp and
his feet on the mantelpiece, on off-nights, and it
generally happened that she came in to entertain
us on those evenings, and afterwards, I remember
now, her bold black eyes were always roving in
his direction as she talked.
By-and-by, Blake, the overseer, used to stroll
round regularly after tea to chat with her, leaving
his wife to the companionship of her sickly, squal-
ling children, and the occupation of mending his
moles by the light of a guttering oil-lamp—no easy
task when the dull eyes have been weakened by
attack after attack of sandy blight. Truth to say,
Mrs. Blake was a most woful creature, and
station opinion did not blame Blake, who was a
fine specimen of a man, for seeking amusement
elsewhere if she couldn’t make his home “ pleasant
and cheerful ” for him. _
Pleasant and cheerful!—with fourteen souls
packed into the space for seven, and a pair of
weakly twins howling the house down ever-
lastingly. So might Mrs. Blake have defended
herself, just as she might have blamed early
marriage, many children and the tropic heat, for
making her the washed-out rag of humanity she
was, tho’her years numbered one less than plump,
fresh-faced Mrs. Kennedy’s.
A jealous woman is always ridiculous, and Mrs.
Blake was especially so. Mrs. Steve coaxed the
boss into letting us have the wool-store for a
dance. She contrived herself a wonderful co -
tume ; I believe it was yellow print and black
lace—but it looked A 1 at night, and it was turned
down in front to show the curves of a well-de-
veloped chest. Blake danced a lot witli her, and
Mrs. Blake made a scene ; her shrill wailing com-
plaints were quite drowned by her rival’s smart
and jeering retorts, and it ended in Blake flying
into a rage and sending his wife home. Reuss also
paid Mrs. Steve marked attention that night; but
Mrs. Reuss was a strong woman, and she sent
Reuss home and out of harm’s way. She had not
time to be jealous, and so took care to prevent its
possibility. Mrs. Steve acted the scene with the
overseer’s wife again for us next evening. She
was a born actress—no doubt of that—and she
imitated the “streak of misery’s parting speech
to perfection: “There’s nothin’ fair, there’s
nothin’ just in anything, or you’d get paid-out for
making mischief, you brazen-faced person!”
( Person’ was considered a term of great dis-
respect). _
And so Mrs. Steve gave us over again her own
spirited retort. .
“Person, indeed!” That’s how it began. The
rest isn’t publishable, for Mrs. Steve did not care
what she said, and had a wonderful command of
language useful for such occasions.
(( She ended up by tossing her head and declaring
that there wasn’t a naan in the district she couldn’t
have crawlin .after her if she wanted him ’’—and
such was our infatuation that I believe weone and
all hoped she considered us amongst the crawlers,
and not in the outer darkness that enshrouded the
rrien she didn t want. As I said before, those men
were (apparently) only her husband and Harring-
ton. One day all hands were out starting a big
mob of horses for the coast. They were bound for
India. How it happened is hard to say, but
Dixon, who was in charge, was thrown, and was
picked up with a broken neck, while the horse,
which had fallen with him, struggled up to its
feet uunarmed., Life or death—the workings of a
station can t be upset for accidents, and while
the boss gave orders for poor Dixon to be carried
home, I received mine to take his place, and
I galloped back to the station to get some
things together. I had the shortest time for pre-
t 0 c f ch , t f mob . U P* fc
stor? and fhe rWn T ”!•
store, ana, seeing the door open, 1 ran in, noting,
as I went, how sleepy and dead-alive the big
station homestead looked in its midday drowsiness
The office was only divided from the store by a
slab partition, and I heard Harrington’s voice a
shade removed from its steady evenness, say in"—
«* By,- p, lf iv. 00 i,„ • • • ■ * V.
B or God s sake, spare me any more of this
nonsense. Are you mad, woman ? Think of your
children—of your husband—anything-, in fact,
that is likely to bring you to your senses."
. Then he gave a sigh of mingled rage and vexa-
tion.
I stepped softly to the wall, unable to resist my
curiosity. 1 nearly jumped out of mv skin with
astonishment at what I saw—viz., Mrs. Steve
Kennedy, in her yellow-and-blnck evening dress—
kneeling at Harrington’s feet, her face red and
swollen with weeping. Harrington, perched up on
the tall office-stool, his coat off, and a pen behind
his ear, was not a romantic figure, and the annoy-
ance and disgust expressed by his whole attitude
were almost comical. “Do get up,” he said,
politely, “you—you—will spoil your dress—the
floor is very dirty."
( “ What do I care ! ” said Mrs. Steve, miserably.
“You wouldn’t come to the dance and you
wouldn’t Jiave seen me in this if I hadn’t put it
on. Ain’t there nothing you like the look of me
in? The other men said I was splendid.” Then
she sobbed.
“ Suppose anyone comes in and sees you in this
rig-out,” said Harrington impatiently, “ what
would they think ? ”
“Just the truth and nothing else,’’she answered
defiantly ; “and I don’t care who knows that I’m
shook on you, Mr. Harrington, and always have
been, and I——"
But this was too much for the book-keeper. He
interrupted her by springing off the stool back-
wards to escape her clinging arms, and then
taking her by the elbows he gently but firmly
pushed her outside the door and locked it.
I watched him for a minute. He went back to
his stool and returns, adding up the long rows of
figures as if nothing had happened. None of us
ever had understood Harrington. Even now, with
the light of years and wider experience to judge
men and morals by, I understand him less than
ever.
■ I then took a good long look at Mrs. Steve
Kennedy walking slowly towards the quarters; no
longer a triumphant siren, but a crushed and
defeated “ woman scorned,” the strong noonday
sun shining on her uncovered head and bringing
out all the garish tawdriness of her cheap yellow-
cotton gown and the rusty black lace which had-
looked such a dream of beauty in the glamor of
the flickering oil-lamps.
. I hadn’t time,to say good-bye to her or to Har-
rington—l had wasted too much over them as
it was—and in a few moments I was in the
saddle and hard on the tracks of the horses.
Circumstances which have nothing to do with this
story decreed that I was never to see either of
them again.
But I remember thinking, as I galloped over
the hard red road, of poor, washed-out Mrs.
Blake’s wild tirade against life and its injustice,
and wondering if she would ever know how neatly
fate had turned the tables on Mrs. Steve.
